9. In 1859 the statutes of the United Provinces

right was repeated to Canadian residents, an^to

importation or printing of works written by pc
sons not residing in Canada and not being Britis
subjects residing in Great Britain or Ireland.
This Act remained in force for nine years. In 1867 t
confederated provinces of North America became t
i ominion of Canada, and next year the Canadian; Pari
nient repealed the Provincial Act of 1859 and faced t
position that the Commonwealth is facing- now.
'that did the Canadian Parliament do? Did it pi
another Act on the “ incongruous,” “ barely intelligible
British Acts of that date ? It did not. It availed itself o
the express authority of the Constitution to pass ind
pendent Acts designed in the first place for the protectio
and benefit of Canadians.

Resuming*:
11. In 1875 a Canadian Act (38 Vic., c. 88) met
die necessity for cheap Canadian reprints of
British copyright works, and enlarged the eopy-
'lght field to meet international pressure. Copy-
right was given (sec. 4) to “ any person domiciled
n Canada or in any part of the British posses-
ions, or being a citizen of any country having a l
international copyright treaty with the Unitec
vingdom ; provided that the subject of copy
11- i P r ) n^e< i and published or reprinted anc
epu Wished in Canada, or in the case of works o
rt that it be produced or reproduced in Canada,
vithin ‘ one month from the date of the origina
inblication elsewhere.”
Interim copyright could be obtained during th
ebatable month by registration with the Ministe
a charge of copyright (sec. 10). Canadian reprin
nd importation of foreign reprints was prohibitec
ar that month (sec. 11). Failure to reprint anc
epubhsh in Canada during the month, after
souring interim copyright, was punishable by
ne of $lOO (sec. 17). Sec. 15 gave copyright to
miadian reprints of British copyright books non
Dpvnghteu in Canada (with power under the
jbsequent Imperial Act to exclude foreign re-
mits) ; but British-printed copies of those books
ere still to be admitted to Canada. Sec. 22 pro-
ided that if a work copyrighted in Canada
Dcaine out of print, any person might complain
) the Minister, who was to notify the copyright
wner; and if the owner did not apply a remedy
'thin a reasonable time, the Minister might
Dense any person to reprint or import on payment
royalty. * J
12. In 1875 the Imperial Parliament passed a
'pendent Act (38 & 39 Vic., c. 63) providing
i Canadian reprints of British copy-
pit books should not be imported into the
mted Kingdom; and (sec. 5) that (seeing
madian reprints could now be copyrighted, and
reign reprints of the same works were excluded),
reign reprints of books not copyrighted in
mada could still enter under the 1868 Order in
luncil.
L 3. In 1886, the Canadian Act of 1875 became
apter 62 of the Revised Statutes. Chapter 33 of
3 Revised Statutes was the Customs Act, which
I in effect states existing duties as follows:
a. On books, excepting foreign reprints
British copyright works and Bibles, etc., 15
cent, ad valorem.
b. On foreign reprints of British copyrig
works, 27J per cent, ad valorem.
o. Free imports are bound books which hav
been printed longer than seven years, exce]
foreign reprints of British copyright works.
d. Prohibited imports are reprints of wori
copyrighted in Canada.
Recent Canadian Legislation,
14. In 1889 a Canadian Act (52 Vic., c. 2
varied (sec. 1) the definition of persons entitled tc
copyright to include “Any person domiciled i
Canada or in any part of the British possessions
or a,ny citizen of any country which has an inter
national copyright treaty with the United King
dom, in which Canada is included ” ; provide
that, simultaneously with the first publication
production thereof elsewhere, the subject of copy
right shall be registered in Canada (including
leposit of copy of title and payment of fee), an
;hat, “ within one month after publication or pro
luction elsewhere ,” “ such work shall be printed anc
published or produced in Canada, or repainted ant
•epublished and reproduced in Canada."
This Act contained a provision that it was not to come
ito force till proclamation by the Governor-General. I
ms assented to May 2, 1889 ; but apparentlv was not pro
laimed, and consequently had no effect, though it
ppears in “ Acts of the Parliament of the Dominion o
ianada,” Ottawa, 1889, pp. 133-5.
15. In 1895 a Canadian Act amended the 1889
by adding (sec. 3) that if a book copyrighted
i Canada was not printed and published in
anada so as to meet the Canadian demand, a
cense to print might issue.
With this addition, the section thus reproduced the
commendation of the British Copyright Commission of
78. (Report, clause 207).
jc. 5 provided for the revocation of the license if
te owner of copyright undertook to supply the
xnadian demand for the book.
The Act was assented-to 22 July, 1895 ; but apparently
so far as it depended on the unproclaimed Act of 1889 ’
had no effect.
16. In 1900 a Canadian Act revised the licensing
stem. Sec. 1 provided that if a book in which Cana-
an copyright subsisted, had been first published
any part of the British Dominions other than
mada; and if the owner of the Canadian copy-
ist and of the copyright acquired by such publi-
hon licensed Canadian reprint; then the Minis-
• might prohibit importation of foreign reprints
cept with the consent of the licensee.
'his is existing Canadian law.
In Conclusion.
After a first glance at the Commonwealth Bi
recognising the honest pains that Senator Keatin
had applied to its construction and the features
which it improved upon the present state o
things, the Bui was here praised highly— t
highly. Now that there nas been time
study its provisions, that praise must be
great measure withdrawn. It is now suggestec
that Senator Keating has approached the sui
ject at the wrong end, and traversed it i
the wrong way. Instead of coming to :
as an Australian citizen, jealous for the righ
and dignity of Australia, and putting th
interest of Australia above any other inte
est whatsoever, he has come to it rather as
citizen of the world—with the result that, wlfil
the Australian author is protected, the Australia
reader may be less well served than thi
Canadian reader, and the claims of Australia
industry are certainly not provided for as they art
in Canada.
Now the reader’s claim, and the claim of indus
try, are of great importance. It is important tha
literature should be provided for Australian
readers as freely and cheaply as possible. It i
important that the _ Australian industries—th
industries of the printer, binder, typefoundei
paper-maker, ink-maker, cloth-maker and th<
rest—should receive the greatest possible en
couragement consistent with the rights o
authors and readers. Since Australia needi
population for trade and defence, ever 1
additional man engaged in any of those in
dustries becomes an additional source of nations
prosperity, an additional bulwark in nations
danger. We do not want to pay the wages o
men employed beyond Australia when we cai
with equal or greater profit to authors and reader
pay wages to Australian citizens. And it is sub-
mitted that the Canadian compromise meets the
case better than the repetition of British ideas
which has commended itself to Senator Keating.
There is no question of conflict with the Imperia
authorities. What is good for Canada is
(in the direction referred to) good for Aus
tralia. What has been Imperially authorised for
Canada can without doubt be Imperially authorised
for Australia. And even the British Commission
of 1878, upon which Senator Keating relies, re-
ported in favour of a system by which local
reprints of British copyright books might be
licensed, and in favour of the perpetuation of the
existing British law permitting the importation ol
foreign reprints if that system were not applied in
the interest of readers.
As Senator Symon said : even lawyers may know
little of copyright. There is danger that, coming
with Ministerial authority, Senator Keating’s Bill
will be passed into law too hastily, since legisla-
tors who may not understand the principles in-
volved will naturally rely upon Ministerial
authority. But there is no reason for
haste. The amendment of copyright law is a
matter of urgency, yet not of the greatest urgency.
Once enacted, we may not find the law easy of
amendment; and the question deserves the fullest
consideration before it is fundamentally settled
for the Commonwealth. It is therefore suggested
that the Bill be referred to a Commission, before
which representatives of the trades and profes-
sions affected may have opportunity of giving
evidence. It is urged strongly that the Bill
should not be permitted to pass the Parliament
with its present principles maintained.
A.G.S.

A TORPID LIVER.
A CATALOGUE OF SYMPTOMS.
The Case of Mrs. M. WINC.
(By a Sydney Reporter.)
Events which havo eyidently left an
impression upon the mind never to be
obi iterated—events which at one time
were beheld with grief and dismay—
are here related by Mrs. Maria Wing,
of Dora-street, Hurstville, upon whom
a pressman recently called, and was
courteously accorded an interview.
“It is twelve years ago now," ex-
plained Mrs. Wing, “since I took Cle-
ments Tonic for indigestion and neu-
ralgia, but if it were twice that time I
should never forget how much I owe
to that remedy for its powerful cura-
tive influence."
“Had your afflictions been in exis-
tence long ?"
“muite seven years, so you can ga-
ther from that what a wreck I was.
Even from childhood I had been a
great sufferer from nervousness, and
each year found that complaint more
fully developed then ever. When neu-
ralgia started, the pains in my jaws,
all about my head, behind my eyes,
and at the back of my neck and right
down my shoulders wore unendurable.
Night after night passed by and not
an Hour's sleep, with the result that,
by-and-bve, 1 felt almost dazed for the
want of rest. This, however, was not
the full extent of my adversities, for I
daresay you know what indigestion is
like ? It meant that whenever I took
any food—and I did not have much at
a time, my appetite being so poor—l
got a feeling m the chest like a tight-
ness, as if I had overeaten, and a
nasty, full, uncomfortable feeling also
affected me in the stomach. But tho
bilious attacks I had wore horrible.
Some days, from the time I got up in
the morning till the sun went down in
the evening, 1 could do nothing but
retch, and on those particular occa-
sions I had such a fearful sensation in
my head that I could not hold it up.
Talk about being depressed—why, that
was no namo for it ; I was absolutely
melancholy, and what also helped me
to be so was a continual presentiment
that something was going to happen.
Try how l would I could not get out
of my head the idea that some kind of
trouble was awaiting me, and if it
didn't come to-day I was sure it would
arrive to-morrow. Oh ! it was a life
that one should bo pitied for leading,
and I'm sure I’ll do all I can to help
other poor women out of similar pre-
dicaments. Now I know that an infal-
lible cure is within the reach of every-
body, I consider it but right that I
should make known to the world what
that cure is, and for this reason alone
I am actuated in going into full de-
tails. Yes, when those bilious attacks
were on I was so upset generally that
I could not oven take a drink of water.
Nothing whatever would stay on the
stomach, so it’s nothing to marvel at
that I got as thin as a shadow and as
weak as a kitten. And the giddiness
that I had to contend with. It used to
come on suddenly, and almost cause
me to fall sometimes, and it was
strange how my eyes felt so swollen
and the sight bad besides.”
“Those are symptoms arising from
the disordered state of the liver," re-
marked the writer.
“Exactly, and further evidence in
support of that lay in the fact that
my mouth had a bitter taste, and 1
had a white coating over my tongue;
and not until I had taken some Cle-
ments Tonic could I get rid of those
symptoms cither.”
“What medicine had you taken pre-
viously ?"
“Nearly every kind of pills and me-
dicines ever heard of, and there was
not one of them that was worth my
while to refer to. Such useless trash 1
'“dn’t think possible could exist, and
it just shows you how advisable it is
to relate such experiences as mine so
that people’s money should not be
thrown away. Doctors, too, they used
to attend me and prescribe medicine,
but it never came to anything. I felt
no better after taking it, and that is
why, to tell you the truth, I started
to lose hope, and think that. 1 was
never going to get rid of my com-
plaints again. One thing that caused
me no small amount of alarm was pal-
pitation of the heart, which used to
be particularly troublesome when lying
down at night, but Clements Tonic
soon set my mind at rest by making
the heart’s pulsation regular and
steady, and at the same time I ob-
served a great falling off in the bother
1 previously had. with flatulence. My
chest became eased of its burdensome
feelings, and when I had been using
Clements Tonic a little while the dis-
comforts to the stomach after meals
was entirely absent. So far so good, I
thought ; hut there was a lot for Cle-
ments Tonic to do still, if it w'as only
equal to the occasion.”
‘‘And was it equal to all your re-
quirements V”
“Yes; every one of them, and what
a lucky woman I was to be suro ! Just
fancy being almost mad with pain like
1 frequently used to be, to fruitlessly
spend money in all directions trying to
get relief, to becomo hopeless and de-
spondent, and then, at last, to get a
medicine hituerto overlooked which
was the very one that could cure me !
Don’t you think T had cause to be
deeply grateful ? Ah, well ! I sincerely
trust that my experience may reach
the ears of others, who will soon
know that 1 have spoken nothing but
iho truth about Clements Tonic. With-
out exaggeration, that remedy set me
entirely free from all the pains I have
mentioned, gave me such an appetite
that I could eat anything that was
going, and so invigorated my nervous
system that I could stand any kind of
noire without trembling, and as for
having ugly dreams or spending my
time in worrying over imaginary trou-
bles aheau, why, 1 never thought of
such things after being on Clements
Tonic a few weeks. And if anything
will make a person sleep soundly it is
Clements Tonic, for by using it it be-
came a regular tiling for me to fall off
shortly after getting into bed, and
not till ne’arlv breakfast time would I
wake again. It was a delightful change,
and it will give you an idea of the
perfect cure it was when I tell you
that I am now heavier than I have
ever been before.”
“Well, that ought to be a good in-
dex as to your health.”
“And so it is, for I can now work
well and enjoy the _ privilege of living,
whereas before taking Clements Tonic
life was only a drudgery that was not
worth enduring. I have advised dozens
of people to take Clements Tonic, and
shall always do so. You also have my
permission to publish this interview in
any way you like for the benefit of the
general community.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, MARTA WING, of Dora-streot,
Hurstville, in the State of New South
Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my lull permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing tho same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisions of tho Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Hurstville this
twenty-third day of September, one thousand
nine hundred and four, before me,
W. HUME, J.P.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As Good as New.
Oh, this is a song of the old lights, that came to
my heart like a hymn;
And this is a song for the old lights—the lights
that we thought grew dim,
That came to my heart to comfort me, and I pass
it along to you ;
And here is a hand to the good old friend who
turns up as good as new.
And this is a song for the camp-fire out west
where the stars shine bright.
Oh, this is a song for the camp-fire where the old
mates yarn to-night;
Where the old mates yarn of the old days, and
their numbers are none too few.
And this is a song for the good old times that will
turn up as good as new.
Oh, this is a song for the old foe—we have both
grown wiser now.
And this is a song for the old foe, and we’re sorry
we had that row.
And this is a song for the old love —the love that
we thought untrue—
Oh, this is a song of the dear old love that comes
back as good as new.
Oh, this is a song for the black sheep, for the
black sheep that fled from town,
And this is a song for the brave heart, for the
brave heart that lived it down.
And this is a song for the battler, for the battler
who sees it through—
And this is a song for the broken heart that turns
up as good as new.
Ah, this is a song for the brave mate, be he bush-
man, Scot, or Russ,
A song for the mates we will stick to —for the
mates who have stuck to us;
And this is a song for the old creed, to do as a
man should do,
Till the Lord takes us all to a wider world where
we’ll turn up as good as new.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
Impression.
The Sea is a Sultana
Imperious and fair;
A Queen of the Zenana
With heaving bosom bare.
The Sun, her Lord and Lover,
From his imperial height,
His golden throne above her,
Sends kisses of keen light.
What high dream is she dreaming,
The fair Sultana sea ?
So bright she is in seeming;
Can she know tragedy ?
She is the Queen of Magic,
Of changing smiles and sighs;
Yet in her heart-deeps tragic
The lost Atlantis lies.
N.S.W.
V. J. DALEY.
Kincardine.
You said, when our flocks were driven
To death in the Drought King’s reign :
“ Oh, for the mist on Loch Leven,
And the grey North Sea again ! ”
When we lay heartsick and weary
At the close of a burning day,
You said : “ It were well, my dearie,
Could we couch on a Scottish brae.”
The far land of Scotland ;
The grey land of Scotland;
Where the hearts are guid and fine
In the homes of Kincardine.”
When our wattle wore its yellow,
Crowning all the sandy hills ;
And each bush bird called its fellow
In a thousand mocking trills ;
You said : “ Would we were together
Where the tall stocks bloom and blow,
For ’tis in the land o’ heather
That the sweetest blossoms grow.
The far land of Scotland ;
The brown land of Scotland;
There are roses fair and fine
Blooming red in Kincardine.”
When the floods were on the plain land,
And the creeks were running free;
When the stockyard was the mainland,
And the paddock was the sea ;
When the yellow torrents curled
Round each rocking myall tree—
You said : “ Half across the world
Lie the waters of the Dee
In the fair land of Scotland ;
The great land of Scotland;
It is good in rain or shine
By the Dee in Kincardine.”
Ah 1 the cold North Sea is calling
Over Queensland's yellow plain ;
Snowflakes on its grey waves falling,
Or the icy sleet and rain.
You would give our golden weather
And our wide unpeopled miles,
For a yard or two of heather
In your distant Scottish isles.
The far land of Scotland ;
The grey land of Scotland ;
Does your heart grow cold to mine
Dreaming still of Kincardine ?
Q.
M. FORREST.

THE BAD BOY.
State Premier Carruthers nowadays lives ill a political sense by finding imaginary grievances against the Australian Parliament, by
speaking severely about imaginary injuries done by it to N.S.W., and by posing as a valiant individual who is saving his State from imaginary,
destruction. In fact, he diverts attention from his own mismanagement by being the soured small boy who sticks out his tongue a
Australia’s Parliament.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them, to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
alivays is.

lishers. The right to reproduce or translate y part of
the contents of this number is hereby express . served by
the publishers.

acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” column all contri-

two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. ivhen the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 28, 1905.
SHALL WE BUY POPULATION FROM
BOOTH.
The Bulletin is forced reluctantly to the con-
clusion that Australia should answer with a firm
“No ” Salvation Army leader Booth’s proposal
to dump 5000 agricultural families in the Common-
wealth. It is true that this continent wants
population. It is, also, probably true that the
addition of 5000 families, of average ability and
means to seek out livelihoods for themselves,
would not cause any pro rata increase in the un-
employed, even were no special means adopted
to find them posts in advance. The economic
condition of the country, in spite of the burdens
of debt, partial Freetrade and land mono-
poly, is sufficiently sound to allow that to be
said. It is also most certainly true that, with
effective legislation, discouragement of the im-
porter and the land monopolist, Australia could
absorb 5000 or 500,000, or indeed 5,000,000 families
within a very few years. But, granting all that,
the question to be settled now hinges on things as
they are. not on tilings as they might and ought
to be. The fact has to be kept in mind that, such
is the importance of attracting immigrant popula-
tion, very grave care must be taken against enter-
ing upon a scheme that is likely to fail. Australia
now attracts hardly any immigrants, partly
as the effect of the villainously bad govern-
ment of the Tories in the past; partly because of the
Stinking Fish cry which those Tories raise when
an effort is made to clean up the mess that they
left behind them; and mainly because of* the
neglect of the people to offer a certainty of employ-
ment by developing such “great natural resources”
as the conversion of coal and iron dirt into steel
and sheep covering into cloth. If, in addition to
these handicaps, Australia is going to gibbet on
its coast the wreck of a big scheme, such as Booth
proposes, the effect will be bad. In short Aus-
tralia canr.nv afford to run any serious risk of
failure, and the Booth scheme seems to promise
failure with the assertiveness of the big drum at an
Army meeting.
* • * *
In the first place it has to be admitted that
the agricultural immigrant is, at present, the
immigrant with the poorest chance. There is
a generous area of good land in Australia. In
IN.S.W. alone there is as much usable agricul-
tural land as in France. But very little of it is
available. Some of it Mr. Willis has. Much of
it has been locked up for the benefit of those
gentlemen who were cute enough to recognise
that with Mr. Willis as agent all things were
possible in the Lands Department. Yet more
of it was given away in the early days of
the State for services ranging from convict-
flogging and debauchery to more worthy work,
and is now held from full production by
the grip of dead hands. Yet more is in the
maw of absentee monopoly co. ’s. Not to go into
details, the practical situation is this, that there
are many N.S.W. citizens, who know the country
and its agricultural conditions, and who cannot
get good land on reasonable terms. The same
statement is true of Victoria, S.A., and Tasmania.
Whilst that is so, it is absurd to import an immi-
grant on the representation that he is to be given
a “good chance ” on the land. If there are those
good chances available, why are they not offered
to our own sons ? They have the right to first
consideration. It is probably true that of 5000
men coming here with a fair stock of money
and knowledge, the majority could carve
out a competency for themselves. But there
is no reasonable prospect of success on the land
for 5000 struggling heads of families. If there is,
why is not that prospect held out to our own
citizens, of whom 5000 could readily be found
anxious to have a “ chance on the land ” ?
. When Australia has openings for agricultural
immigrants (that will be as soon as tariff reform
and land reform open the farm and the factory to
labor) it would be still doubtful whether it would
be wise to encourage any “ colonies ’’ of settlers
divided by race, tongue, religion, or social habits
from the bulk of the community. Australia
wants absorbable citizens, immigrants who,
within a reasonable time, will become Aus-
tralians, and whose children’s children will be
indistinguishable from our descendants. A colony
of Germans, or Scandinavians, or Russians,
imported as a colon}’, and settled under such con-
ditions as to emphasise its segregation from the
rest of the community and to preserve its differing
characteristics, would be objectionable, though any
number of individual Germans, Scandinavians, or
Russians might be welcome. To almost the same
extent, a colony, if such were proposed, of Roman
Catholics, or Presbyterians, or High Anglicans, or
any of those curious out-sizes in religions with
which Canada has had such strange experiences,
would invite opposition, if it were proposed as a
condition of the settlement that the colonists
should be disciplined to retain customs and ideas
separating them from the rest of the community.
When the question is narrowed down to a direct
“aye” or “ nay ” to a proposal from “General”
Booth to send 5000 families, organised by himself,
the special objections which obtrude are so many
that a refusal would be justified, even if this were
our only hope of receiving immigrants.
* * * *
“ General ” Booth, it may be premised, is not
in this business for his health. His nose guaran-
tees that. If Mr. Booth takes the trouble to get
together 5000 families to send to Australia, there
is going to be something in it for Mr. Booth. He
took the trouble, years ago, to send a big drum and
a tambourine, and a vociferous saltatory “captain,”
and a strenuous “lass” to Australia, and since
then the continent has paid heavy tribute to
Booth. The big drum bangs in every town to
bring the eleemosynary penny to the tambourine,
and the aggregate penny, after a finely-cut allow-
ance for costs of collection, goes to Booth. In
almost every town the Army has halls ; in
almost every city it has industries. They all
belong to Booth. His rescue homes and doss
houses sell a cheap line in salvation and bunks and
soup on strictly cash terms and commercial prin-
ciples. His industries for the unemployed mostly
represent competition in a refuge by a man or
woman, who is fed on broken victuals and nasal
psalms, against an individual outside who is trying
to make decent food for a family at a similar
calling, and these refuges cause as much un-
emjjloyment as they relieve. It is a matter of
some certainty that if Australia grants land to a
Booth colony, the paw of Booth will in some way
be on the land. Possibly he may not ask that the
deeds shall be locked up in his London office, but
he certainly will keep some sort of control over
the settlers and the settlement, and will be to
some extent its absentee landlord, as he is to-day
the great absentee cadger and sweater of Aus-
tralia.
* * ■* *
Even if the extraordinary happened and it was
found, when “General” Booth came to his terms,
that he proposed to make no profit for his Army
out of his Australian settlement; that he was going
to be paid for his trouble merely by the gratitude
of the British rich for being relieved of so many
British poor, there would still be the best of
reasons for looking askance at his Salv’army settle-
ment. The men would be, without a doubt, about
the worst type to face the problem of wresting a
living from the Australian land, which requires,
above all things, steady pluck. They would be men
who have been down—right down—and whose
misfortunes have taken the heart out of them.
Such colonists would be servile as men and
tyrannical as masters. The man who has
been “submerged” to such an extent as to
have to cling to a Salv’army plank to get back to
the surface—to have to drop his self-respect and
his sense of the ridiculous by joining in the
grotesque antics of that cult—is emphatically not
the man who can face the Australian busli, which
is. on the whole, kind to the brave but very cru-el
to the broken man.
For these and other reasons The Bulletin
would urge the Australian Government to dis-
courage the Booth proposal. We want immi-
grants certainly ; but still more we want occupa-
tion for immigrants. And when the occupation is
offering, the new colonists can be far better
chosen by an Australian High Commissioner than
by the head of a fantastic religious organisation
whose main associations with Australia are of
begging and sweating. There is no reason to get
hysterical over the immigration question. A
strong policy is called for, but not a policy of
getting population of any sort at any price. If
Prime Minister Deakin can decide on—
(1) The appointment of a High Commissioner to com-
bat the lies of the Stinking F.sh party, and so encourage
a stream of immigrants with capital.
(2) A higher tariff to establish new industries with a
consequent new demand for artisan and agricultural
population.
(3) A Federal progressive land-tax to break up land
monopoly.
(4) A system of assisting desirable immigrants after
work and land have been made available for them.
we will attract enough, immigration to more than
double the population within a decade. And that
population will have to pay no tribute to a foreign
religious potentate ; it will have no bonds to keep
it from mingling with the rest of the population ;
and it will not necessarily bring any more savage
noise and big drum, to a land which has already
much big drum and concertina and singing of
“1 he Y\ r ild Colonial Boy. ’
TASMANIA—AN AWFUL EXAMPLE.
A week or two ago Tasmania evolved a financial
statement, and in the course thereof the Treasurer
issued—it would seem unconsciously—about the
most damning indictment of the great loan policy
that has yet been published. Also, he said some
terrible things—apparently without the least idea
that he was saying them —about the fine old
fiction that this country borrows money for “ per-
manent and reproductive works,” and thereby
gloriously develops its “magnificent natural re-
sources.” The “permanent and reproductive"*
idea and the “ great natural ” idea are two of the
solid stand-bys of these States. They are the
two sagging legs on w'hich the loan monstrosity
lives and moves and has its being. It is held in a
general way that the loan business pays, that it
isn’t a burden on the people but rather enriches
them by “developing” the “resources,” &c., and
that, though the interest is high, the profits, in
some obscure fashion, are higher. Tasmania,
being an unusually retrograde place, holds to this
theory very firmly indeed, and when the Treasurer
issued the following table showing the growth of
taxation per inhabitant as debt accumulated he
probably had no intention of throwing clods at
the Ark of the Covenant:—
Tasmanian Taxation.
Customs Direct
and Excise. Taxation. Total.
1865 ..£152 £O7l £1 12 6
1880 .. 1 18 4 0 15 9 2 14 1
1890 .. 2 7 11 0 18 4 3 6 3
1895 .. 2 1 10 1 0 10 3 2 8
1900 .. 2 16 10 0 15 2 3 12 0
1901 .. 2 8 11 0 12 10 319
1902 .. 1 18 6 0 12 0 2 10 6
1903 .. 214 0 16 11 2 18 3
1904 .. 1 17 0 143 313
That is the thing which Treasurer Stewart fired
off without the least knowledge that it was loaded.
* * » »
These figures (and very similar figures could be
produced for every other State) show various
things—all of them unpleasant. The first is that
the loan policy commenced, not on any high prin-
ciple of developing the country, nor as part of any
far-sighted system, but simply that the lazy,
cowardly, shiftless ancestors might have a good
time, and hang the consequences. Tasmania in
1865 had no debt, and the tired pioneers of the land
paid only 325. 6d. per head annually in taxation.
They wanted money for railways, roads, harbors,
and other works, and they borrowed it because
they preferred selling their country, and their
children, into financial slavery to paying more
than 325. 6d. per annum in taxes. The fact that
their policy would leave their sons in 1900 to pay
725. each per annum in taxes troubled them not at
all. In a general way, they damned their sons,
and decided that their daughters might shift tor
themselves. They borrowed money for roads,
bridges, railways, harbor works, wharves, tele-
graphs, fortifications, rifles, cartridges, schools,
gaols, asylums, and sundries—they borrowed to
cover grants in aid of religion, they borrowed
to cover revenue, shortages, and they borrowed
generally. And every time they borrowed some
more for “permanent and reproductive works”
and further “developed the great natural re-
sources” of the State, taxation went up, because
the works were temporary and unproductive, and
the borrowing of £1 to develop 2s. 6d. worth of
resources is a thing that doesn’t pay.
* * * *
Tasmania, like almost every other State here-
abouts, has paid in interest and expenses as much, or
just about as much, as it borrowed, and still owes
the debt. It has borrowed about 9Jj millions. It
has paid something not far short of millions.
It still owes millions. By-and-bye, if the
present system continues, it will have paid
19 millions and still owe 9| millions. Later it
will have paid 38 millions and still owe the
original 9| millions. But if the selfish old dod-
derers who ran things in ’65, and the other selfish
old dodderers who came after them, had paid as
much in taxation as their descendants have to pay
now, there never need have been any debt. If the
taxes in ’65 had been put up to the level of 1900
there would have been £200,000 a year available
for public works, and this sum would have steadily
increased as the population increased, and it
would have further increased because the revenue
from such of the new works as yielded revenue,
instead of going to pay interest, would have been
available to build more works with. Then, as the
income from works expanded, taxation could have
been reduced. If Tasmania had taxed its people
at the rate of, say, 61s. 3d. each in 1865, and
tapered down to 325, 6d. in 1900 or 1904, instead
of beginning at 325. 6d. in 1865 and broadening up
to 725. in 1900 and 61s. 3d. in 1904, the island could
have had all the public works it has now;
it would have owed not one penny instead
of owing £9,500,000 ; and it would have saved
all the millions it has paid in interest.
And on the whole it would have paid no more
taxes without the debt thawit has done with the
debt. But the heavy end of the taxation would
have been at the 1865 end of the period instead of
at the 1900 or 1904 end, and the selfish old shirkers
of 1865 preferred selling their country into slavery
to carrying the weighty end of the burden. The
only satisfaction is that some of them are still
alive to reap the crop of misery they sowed.
* * * *
Tasmania has lately paid a little less taxa-
tion than it did in 1900; that is not because
its position has improved, but simply because
the island has entered on another career of
aggravated shirking. In 1900 it was paying
something off its floating deficit; in 1902-3-4 it
was letting things slide and adding largely to
the deficit. It resolved to let things slide partly
that it could reduce the taxes on PROPERTY ; in
1897 Bs. 4d. per head of the total taxation was
levied on property ; in 1902 only 4s. 9d. per head
of the taxation and in 1904 6s. per head repre-
sented taxes on property. The amount of taxa-
tion levied on property is about £14,000 less
than it was seven years ago. But while property
thus crawled out from underneath its burdens the
situation of the State was incredibly bad. In
1904, the last year for which complete figures are
available, the amount of interest which had to be
made good out of taxation was £249,499. That
was the dead horse, the interest on unproductive
debt, the burden of the lie which the cowardly
shirking fathers of the State told when they
declared that the loans were invested in per-
manent and reproductive works. This amount
represented £1 7s. Bd. per inhabitant for the year,
and when it is compared with the figures of the
leading countries of the world it shows this
amazing result

Tasmania £1 7s. Bd.
Almost every one of the European debts in the
above list is largely represented by public works,
and on an average the income from public works
covers half the interest bill. In some countries
the revenue from public works covers all the
interest bill. But if the European nations owned
no public works at all, and every penny of their
interest had to be paid out of taxation, the position
of the worst of them, with all its war debts and
all its naval and military expenditure, wouldn’t
be so awful as that of Tasmania, which never had
a war, which never supported a single war-ship,
which never maintained enough armed men to
make a single efficient regiment, and which has
done all this ruin inside 40 years. Yet Tasmanian
papers and politicians still talk of their debt being
represented by permanent and reproductive
works, and say that it isn’t like the “ war debts ”
of Europe.
* * * *
Tasmania nowadays is full of patronising Tories
—Women’s Leaguers, Dobsons, and the like—
who speak at great length about how the Austra-
lian Government allegedly “ discourages immigra-
tion.” Also, the fact that departures from Tas-
mania at present exceed arrivals is haughtily set
down to the machinations of the Labor party.
Considering what a genius Tasmania has dis-
played for driving away population by Tory
misgovernment, this attitude is a sample of stu-
pendous ignorance —or stupendous effrontery.
Tasmanian published immigration statistics com-
mence only in 1847, and since then the years in
which departures largely exceeded arrivals were
these:—
1850, 1851, 1852, 1854, 1856, 1858,
1861, 1862, 1863, 1867, 1869,
1871, 1872, 1873, 1874, 1875,
1885, 1886, 18S8,
1892, 1893, 1894, 1895,
1900, 1901, 1902, 1903, 1904.
Even as far back as the sixties the result for a
whole decade (1862-1871) were a loss of 4604 per-
sons by continued excess of departures, and in the
10 years 1892-1901 the loss was 5091. That was
before Immigration Restriction laws were heard
of, and before any Labor party began to trouble
the dead island. Even the ghastly fact that the
population of misgoverned Tasmania actually
decreased during the first six months of 1905 isn’t
quite new in the island’s experience. It had
similar decreases in 1827, 1837, 1839, 1852, 1854,
1869, and in 1875, and it had many years when it
showed an increase of only a few dozen or a few
hundred people. These matters can’t be set down
to the Labor party, though in the very early days
the hard-labor party may have been partly
responsible.
♦ * * *
Tasmania with its decreasing population (it is
the only State of the Commonwealth which is
acquainted with that disgrace), with its decreasing
area under crop, with its huge estates which
it won’t break up to provide land for the people,
because “the rights of property must be re-
spected,” with the greatest burden of unproductive
debt on earth (save that of Queensland alone),
with its renewed deficits caused solely by
reduction of taxes, with its unspeakably sel-
fish, ignorant, bigoted, incompetent and pre-
tentious Upper House, with its Freetrade tradi-
tions (for though the island used to have a heavy
tariff it never had much of a Protective tariff), is
the disgrace and despair of the Commonwealth.
It hasn’t one politician who has the courage to
face the situation ; not one who even seems to
know that there is a situation to be faced. It is
the most fertile and best-watered of the States,
and it is overcrowded with a population of less
than seven persons to the square mile. It has
been a very constant failure for over 40 years, and
now while it is crumbling into decay its Parlia-
ment is engaged on a Bill for the protection of
homing pigeons. And it is full of old political
dullards, who bleat about “ Federal extrava-
gance ” because the Australian Government
doesn’t borrow, but charges its public works to
ordinary expenditure—which naturally swells the
expenditure somewhat. The only visible hope for
Tasmania lies in a new Federal tariff which will
supply really High Protection; in a Federal
graduated land-tax of such a weighty nature that
it will break up the Y.D.L. Co.’s estate in one
act, and many other estates likewise, and make
land so far available that seven persons to the
square mile won’t overcrow’d this well-watered
island ; and in the assumption of the Tasmanian
debt (along with the other State debts) by the
Commonwealth, the only authority which has the
courage or the honesty to try to pay off a debt.
Then, when its State politicians are reduced to
about the status of aldermen, and the ancient sea-
captain who is now Premier becomes a sort of
mayor, some remarkable individuals will find
their proper level for the first time.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Carruthers as a Bogus Federal Authority.
The latest attitude of Joseph Carruthers of
N.S.W., in his constant attempt to usurp the
Federal authority, is such a vast and bounding
insolence that it ought to cause both Houses of
the Australian Parliament to revolt and tread the
State Rights push flat. Joseph has lately ex-
plained his position in the State Assembly thus :
He recently asked the Prime Minister of the Common-
wealth to be furnished with at least 10 copies of all Bills
presented to the Federal Parliament, as introduced, to
enable him to obtain reports from the officers best quali-
fied to express an opinion on any particular measure, and,
if thought necessarv, to make representations to the
Federal Government in the matter. As soon as he per-
ceived that the Commerce Bill seemed in some respects
to be an attempt to pass Federal legislation on matters
which it was decided at the Hobart Conference should be
left to State control, he communicated with the Premiers
of the other States with a view to a joint protest being
made to the Federal Government. Similar action would
be taken whenever a measure was introduced which it
was considered embodied provisions detrimental to the
interests of the States, and members might rest assured
that every legitimate step would be taken to safeguard
the rights of the States. The views of the New South
Wales Government in regard to the Bills relating to the
representation of the several States in the House of
Representatives, and to census and statistics of the Com-
monwealth, had already been conveyed to the Prime
Minister.
The Hobart Conference, to which Carruthers
refers as representing a momentous occasion at
which Federal and State rights were carefully
defined, was a gathering whereat George Reid,
being then half-Premier, professed to usurp the
power of the House of Representatives, and of the
Senate, and of his colleagues, and of all future


Prime Ministers and their colleagues, and also to
alter and limit the Constitution, by promising that
the Australian Parliament would not deal with
certain matters which the Constitution entrusts
to its charge. George Reid’s agreement at the
Hobart Conference is not, and never was, worth
a burnt match, and Carruthers’ attempt to collect
payment from the Commonwealth of Reids
political bad debts is a virulent sample of blurt.
As regards the other points, the guardians or
State Rights are not the State Premiers but each
State’s six senators, and when Joe Carruthers
and Jack Evans and such-like persons profess to
be senators and to take over the duties of senators
it is time for the Senate to inform the Prime
Minister most distinctly that this bumptious act
of usurpation will not be tolerated. In pursuance
of a steady policy for the repression of fussy and
impertinent underlings, CARRUTHERS ought to be
told to pay for his own copies of Federal Buis.
He should be informed that his views on the rep-
resentation of the States and on census a,nd
statistics are of no more importance, seeing
that none of these things concern him
officially, than the views of any casual boot-
black or other individual voter. In these
matters Joseph is a private voter —only that and
nothing more. And it is more than time to
impress upon Carruthers that, if the Australian
Parliament, in spite of the existence of the
Senate, goes beyond its authority, and tres-
passes on State rights, whatever these may be,
the High Court will attend to the matter; its
decisions, and not any alleged resolutions of a
dead and gone gathering of meddlers at Hobart,
are the means by which the Constitution is
interpreted. The people in this matter have a
claim on Prime Minister Deakin —a claim which
that too-affable person seems apt to forget. In
certain matters —a large number of matters they
are supposed to be governed wholly by the two
Federal Houses of Parliament, elected on a pure
adult suffrage. They are not supposed to be
governed in any of these matters by the advice
or interference of Carruthers, or Bent, or Jack
Evans, or Parson Fitchett, or George Pile, or
Wren, of the Collingwood tote, or even of W. N.
Willis. And if Prime Minister Deakin fails to
remember that he is the guardian of the people s
sacred rights in this matter, a time will come
when the fact will be mentioned to him. The
people’s Federal rights are the meat, and Car-
ruthers is the fussy, incompetent little fly
that always wants to settle thereon, and the neces-
sity for shooing him off is urgent. Of course,
Carruthers’ position is easy enough of compre-
hension. As regards the matters which are within
his jurisdiction, he has shown himself a
weakling, a shuffler, and a failure. His
finance has been a disaster ; his Local
Government measure is a mild apology
and a very weak instalment; his “Reform”
policy in general is a dead thing ; and
the papers which supported him a year ago now'
give him at best a contemptuous tolerance. He
must do something to save himself from utter
slump, and the easiest thing, for a man of hispoor
calibre—with no constructive ability, no definite
opinions, no political courage, and no political
principles—is to be cheeky to his betters, to stick
out his tongue at the Australian Parliament, and
to throw an occasional rock at the front door of
the Constitution. The only thing that keeps
Carruthers politically afloat these days is Ins
pose as the virulent bad kid who is always uncivil
to the Australian Government, and the reputation
for valor that he builds up by periodically sticking
out his tongue at the Australian Parliament.

S. Australia’s Tight Corner.
The wrath of the S. Australian public service is
upon the Price Government because it provides for
next to no additions to civil service salaries this
year—it even cut off the few rises that the
mournful Butler agreed to recommend. S. Aus-
tralian public officers are known to be shockingly
underpaid ; the man in Adelaide who draws £250
a year is a proud person who calls no one uncle.
On the other hand, Price in S. Australia has just
about as hard a row to hoe as Morgan in Queens-
land ; and in Queensland, where salaries are
also shockingly low, public servants have not
only had no rises for some years, but have even
had to put up with special percentage reduc-
tions. S. Australia has steadily built up a
debt of £7B Is. Id. per head (Queensland’s is
about 235. per head more than that), and to make
matters worse it has sunk an enormous amount of
it in absolutely unproductive undertakings. In
buildings and similar things which ought to have
been paid for out of revenue, it has buried over a
fourth of all it has borrowed. Moreover, while
this great unproductive debt has been built up,
the population has been almost at a standstill.
In one respect S. Australia has been far worse off
than Victoria, which has been reckoned a pretty
awful example. It is true that, whereas Victoria
has lost something like 145,000 by excess of
emigration since 1892, S. Australia has lost only
23,000; but, then, Victoria had gained 112,377 in
the previous 10 years, while S. Australia actually
lost 30,945. Yet S. Australia has taken no
account of the fact that its adult population was
slipping away to the other States and leaving the
debt behind them, and it has gone on heaping new
burdens upon those who remained. Moreover,
though absolutely Queensland’s is the Greatest
Debt on Earth, per head of population, S. Aus-
tralia’s is, under ordinary circumstances, far more
burdensome than Queensland’s. For S. Australia
is a poor agricultural country, consisting of a few
patches of moderately good soil and vast areas of
howling wilderness —none the less howling wilder-
ness because, in unusually good seasons, it sings a
siren’s song to the unfortunate settler to come and
live upon its fatness —and then the hot winds
come, and the fatness melts into dust. With no
other industries to speak of, but the agricultural
and pastoral, the S. Australian population is poor
—people of simple tastes and habits and few
wants. Though they are almost without factories
to supply their own needs, they yet pay less in
customs duties than the people of any other State,
less even than those of Tasmania. In other re-
spects, too, Price’s State can at present less afford
to drift than probably any other. Queensland
has no heavy bill falling due in the early
future ; its accusing Cohen will not have to
be met till 1915, and then he will not be
the largest of the Cohens. But S. Australia’s
troubles have begun to drop in already, and will
continue to drop in every year from now until
1920. During the next 10 years £9,033,250 will
fall due, and within another three years of that
(a fact which wasn’t mentioned in the tables
issued along with the Budget speech), a further
£14,506,645. Between now and 1920 S. Australia
will not have a single 12 months of breathing time ;
every New Year’s Day it will find a Cohen waiting
on the door-mat; and what that Cohen will do
and say depends pretty largely upon what S. Aus-
tralia has done since the previous Cohen called.
If under these circumstances the S. Australian
public servant thinks the State can afford
to pay him as much as he ought to get,
The Bulletin fears that there is a cold
time of awakening ahead. And meantime
The Bulletin doesn’t hear from Premier Price
and Treasurer Peake any very definite swearing-
off of the habit which has done much more than
anything else to get the State into this mess not
such a definite swearing-off as the circumstances
call for anyway. About all it hears is the Regis-
ter cursing the new land taxation, though that
will only add £40,000 to the revenue, and the
wagging of the tongues of the old State politicians
trying to fasten the consequences of their own
sins upon “ the Federal Government,” and disre-
garding the fact that, so far as S. Australia is con-
cerned, the Federal duties are bringing in about
£50,000 a year more than the old State duties.

The Decay of Victoria and Tasmania.
An amusing feature of the present immigration
movement is the fact that Premier Bent, of Vic-
toria, raises his bull-voice in the back yard of the
proceedings, and says that he is also in the business,
and is desirous of promoting agricultural immi-
gration to his State, while Premier Evans, of
Tasmania, makes faint remarks in the distance to
the same effect. During the first half of this year
—that is to say, within the brief space of six
months— Bent failed so badly to find land for his
own people, let alone for immigrants, that 6613
more people left Victoria than went into it; and
Tasmania struck a still worse experience. It was
almost entirely an agricultural exodus—a rushing
away of young men in search of land. These
people can’t afford to buy land at £lO to £l2 per
acre, miles and miles away from a market,
and slave all their lives only to pay interest
on the mortgage. They don’t want to hire
land at impossible rents from the rich,
grasping loafers of the Upper House, and
toil all their days to keep up Rupert Clarke’s
yacht, or to enlarge the Manifolds’ already
stupendous hoard, or to bloat the V.D.L. Co. So
they leave, and when Victoria, with all its rich
Western District still almost empty, and Tas-
mania, with half the State a blank, can’t find
land for people already in the country, it is mere
folly to talk of finding soil on which to settle a
rush of immigrants. If Bent can provide land
by a remorseless breaking up of the big estates—
it can’t be found in any other way —Victoria can
gain 12,000 people a year easily without any im-
migration ; in other words, it can leave off losing
12,000 people a year, that being about the average
outflow for 14 years past. Failing this it must
drop further and further back till it becomes a
third or fourth-rate State, and of little account in
the Commonwealth or in Australasia. N.S. Wales
has passed it in population long ago. Maori -
land, which believes in breaking up the big estates,
by a compulsory process, will pass it, on present
appearances, within another 10 years. Queens-
land, which gains immigrants, while Victoria
exports population, is growing six times as fast as
Bent’s State, and will also leave it behind in time.
In the matter of population, the two supremely
landlord-ridden States of the Commonwealth,
Victoria and Tasmania, are now the two horrible
examples. This is the interesting record from
January 1 to June 30 this year :
New South Wales .. .. .. .. Increase 16,727
Westralia ~ 8,837
Queensland .. .. ~ 4,714
South Australia .. .. .. .. „ 1,596
Victoria.. .. .. .. .. .. ~ 699
Tasmania Decrease 2,216
South Australia, and still more Victoria, and
most of all Tasmania, have failed for years past to
find land for their own people, and while they can’t
do that the less they talk of immigration the
better. And apparently Victoria and Tasmania
will continue in their present condition until the
Australian Parliament bursts up their big pro-
perties with a graduated land-tax, which will take
all—and more than all—the V.D.L. Co.’s income
unless it sells, and which will take fully half the in-
comes of the Clarkes and Chirnsides and Mani-
folds unless they sell. On present appearances,
Bent of Victoria and Evans of Tasmania are not
wholly responsible for the want of a bursting-up
policy ; even if they wished for such a policy, in
each case an unpatriotic and immovable Upper
House stands in the way. But these two men can
at least lend any weight they have towards
getting the Federal graduated tax, which is the
only hope of better things ; and meanwhile they
can make themselves as little ridiculous as pos-
sible over the immigration question.
The N.S.W. Lands Scandals.
Announced that the N.S.W. Lands Commission,
after spending three months investigating the im-
provement leases got by squatters who knew
enough to employ, the right kind of land agent,
now intends to devote a little while to investigat-
ing exchanges. The exchange was the great, rival
of the improvement lease in allowing facilities for
up-to-date land-agency. The improvement lease,
got through land-agency, secured for a, long term
of years, on the easiest of conditions and
lowest of rentals, land which was not of the
worthless character for which the improvement
lease tenure was invented. The exchange repre-
sented a bargain by which the State gave up
certain land to private ownership as the price of
resuming other land, the theory being that both
parties were to be benefited. What may be pro-
phesied with confidence is that when the ex-
changes were worked by land-agency methods the
State was always “had.” There will be a good
deal of curiosity as to what sort of fees were ex-
tracted for this class of business, but the investi-
gation will, after all, not bring the inquiry nearer
to its legitimate work, which is to find out
why a few men were able to work such
wonders with the Lands Department. The cata-
logue of their achievements is already great; add-
ing to it is mostly a work of supererogation. But
the Government shows not the least inclination to
take any action which would force the inquiry
into what is now the proper channel. What is
wanted is legislative provision to indemnify
some witnesses "who were themselves concerned
in land-agency transactions, so that they may,
without fear of consequences, disclose anything of
a criminal nature —if such there is to disclose—
without hurt to themselves. This has been urged
on the Government, and the idea will doubtless
be adopted—when the need for it has vanished,
just as the Government took every other step in
this inquiry. Unable to smother up the scandals
of land-agency, it does the next worst thing by
managing to be just a little late with every step of
the inquiry.
How the State Governments Drove Out
Population.
A casual statement made last week by Prime
Minister Deakin, that one of the great hindrances
to immigration lies in the inefficient land laws of
the States, moved the State Frights party to great
anger, and roused the Sydney Telegraph to its
usual yell of fury. When it had finished boiling
with rage it issued a cold and rather superior set
of remarks as follows :
Mi. Deakin has just discovered that the real obstacles
to immigration are State laws, presumably land laws. It
is not the six hatters species of legislation, which he, as
one of a Ministry, accepted against his own will, under
pressure and threats from the Labor party. It is not the
sort of legislation which would not, while a general elec-
tion was in progress, allow a shipwrecked crew to land on
the coast. It is not the sort of legislation that gives a
groom who comes to Australia in charge of horses a ticket
of leave for six months. This kind of legislation has done
no harm to Australia, or discouraged people from emi-
grating here and settling. It is laws passed by the State
Parliaments that are responsible, says Mr. Dearin.
What he attempted to do was to divert attention from
the mischievous type of law which prescribes permits and
tickets for men, and may, at the dictation of a political
union, bail them up when they arrive, keep them on a
steamer for several days, and release them only because
the counter agitation has become too strong for a timid
and terrified Government.
In view of the fact that a good many people
believe this kind of story, and fancy, ..through,
hearing the Telegraph’s constant yelp about the
Six Hatters, that this country was a fine place for
immigrants before the Commonwealth happened,
it is worth while to get down to the facts of the
case. The Commonwealth began to exist in 1901,
and the Aliens Restriction law only came into ex-
istence some time after that. And long before
there was any Commonwealth or any Federal
immigration laws, or any Six Hatters, every State
had frequent and unpleasant intervals when its
departures exceeded its arrivals, a dismal circum-
stance that could only be set down to bad land
laws, insufficient tariffs, and generally to the
sorry incompetence of the State politicians
who now blather so loftily about certain Hatters.
By way of evidence, this is the record, taken from
Coghlan’s “Statistical Account of Australia and
Maoriland.” for 20 years before the beginning of
the Commonwealth
Years when the Various States Lost Poru ration by
Excess of Departures over Arrivals.
New South Wales : 1893, 1895, i 396, 1897, 1900.
Victoria : 1892, 1893, 1894, 1895, 1896, 1897, 1898, 1899,
1900.
Queensland : 1881, 1890, 1891,1892.
South Australia : 1882,1885,1886,1887,1888,1889,1891,
1894, 1895, 1896, 1897, 1898, 1899, 1900.
Wbstralia : 1888, 1899.
Tasmania : 1885, 1886, 1888, 1892, 1893, 1894, 1895, 1900.
Australia and Tasmania Combined : 1892, 1893, 1898,
1899, 1900.
The largest gain recorded in one State in one year
since 1881, by excess of arrivals over departures,
was 35,891. The largest loss recorded in one State
in one year, by excess of departures over arrivals,
was 22,134. The five States which had no gold
boom—N.S.W., Queensland, Victoria, S.A. and
Tasmania—averaged, in the 20 years before Fede-
ration, 12 years of gain by excess of arrivals
and eight years of loss by excess of departures.
But for the last ten years before Federation the
States that had no gold boom averaged only four
years each of gain by excess of arrivals over depar-
tures, against six years of loss by excess of depar-
tures over arrivals. And even if Westralia, with
its gold and loan boom, is included, there were
on the average within the last ten years more
years of loss than of gain. The State politicians
who produced this dreadful result feel it necessary
to disguise their own failure by professing wild
anger about the Six Hatters, and by pretending to
believe that Australia’s inability to attract popu-
lation is a new trouble arising out of some alleged
blind tourist at Hobart. But the failure and the dis-
grace existed long before there was any Federation,
and at that time it must have been exclusively the
failure of State politicians. And considering how
little power the Federal Government exercises
now, and how much power is left to the States,
the chances are that the failure is still mainly
owing to the incapacity of State politicians. At
present the cry is for agricultural immigrants.
The first essential for agricultural immigrants is
land, and the State Parliaments control the land
and make the land laws. Prime Minister Deakin
offers to find the agricultural immigrants if the
States will supply the land. And it transpires
th it, mainly by reason of land monopolies,
Tasmania, Victoria and S.A. have no land to
offer. Westralia offers mostly sand and dust.
N.S.W. proposes to shunt its immigrants out into
the drought and scrub region ; and Queensland
seems to be the only State which is really pre-
pared to find a bit of decent soil for the bucolic
stranger who is to be within our gates.

The Proposed Victorian Code.
From a lawyer :
As the resuit of 15 months intermittent hard graft
Crown Prosecutor Finlayson and lawyer Woinarski have
presented the Bent Ministry with a couple of draft Bills
codifying and consolidating the Victorian criminal code,
at present in a singularly chaotic state. Some of the
suggested amendments are so drastic as to scare the re-
maining hairs from the heads of Tory persons of stodgy
habit, but a lot of progressive proposals are conspicuous
by their absence.
Briefly, the two lawyers put the proposed code into two
sections : (a) crimes—offences to be dealt with by juries
onlv ; (b) summary offences—with which the common
honorary is competent to deal. They suggest the aboli-
tion of the death penalty except for murder, treason,
piracy, and arson of Edward Vi l.’s ships or dockyards;
that arrests may be made without warrant on charges in-
volving a heavier penalty than five years ; the abolition
of the legal presumption that a married woman acts
under her husband’s compulsion—she ha n’t yet got full
political rights in Victoria, but she is to stand the racket
if she does anything wrong ; the sweeping away of the old
distinctions between actual offenders and aiders, abettors
and accessories before the fact—all are to bedeemed equally
guilty ; the application to all crimes of the law as to com-
pounding ; the wiping out of the distinction between
perjury committed inside a court and that committed
outside —the maximum penalty in both cases to be 15
years instead of the present 10 and seven years respec-
tively ; the abolition of the common law offences known
as maintenance, champerty, and the buying and selling
of disputed titles ; and the provision of penalties for
neglect to procure assistance in childbirth—there being
nothing at present between concealment of birth and
manslaughter. Burglary in their code is to be called
housebreaking by night; arson, wilfully burning pro-
perty ; uttering, using ; larceny, theft; also juries may be
empowered to return a verdict of certain lesser crimes
when the charge is one of manslaughter.
After recommending the utter abolition of the foolish
old grand jury, the consolidators spring their most drastic
suggestion, viz., that in criminal trials, after the jury has
agreed to disagree for the space of three hours, a three-
fourths verdict may be taken. The discordant penalties
at present provided for various similar offences are
brought into line, and in some specified cases the discre-
tion of the judges is enlarged as to imprisonment, fine,
flogging, “ solitary,” and payment of costs. Also, the
adoption of the objectionable English tioket-of-leave
system is recommended. The Bent Cabinet being under-
stood to be at present considering the question of the
indeterminate sentence no mention is made thereof; but
that very omission itself discounts a lot of the codifiers’
work. The amendments constitute a considerable ad-
vance on the present mass of anomalies miscalled a code,
but the omissions are a good deal more obvious and im-
portant than the improvements.
There is nothing about empowering juries to assess the
penalties, or to return a verdict of murder in the Ist, 2nd,
or 3rd degree ; the antiquated, degrading and useless
punishments of solitary confinement and the lash are to
be continued; there is little curtailment and much
extension of the powers of the ignorant and venal
honorary magistrate—it is even proposed to let him deal
with such offences as “ unlawfully using cattle,” etc. ;
there is no suggestion of any provision for the defence of
persons accused of any crime involving less than the
death penalty (British fairplay seems to have been
thrown overboard here); there is no suggestion that the
penalty in a case of perjury by a prosecuting witness
should be heavier than in the case of an accused (the one
is trying to save himself only, the other is endeavoring t)
injure an innocent person). In fact, considering the
known characters and characteristics of the two lawyers
who drew up the codifying- Bills, they are a distinct
disappointment; and, if accepted, they may destroy any
hope of further advance in the direction of an en-
lightened, scientific code for a good many years.

A Defence Distinction.
A big step forward will have been taken in the
organisation of a National Defence scheme when
the public is educated into recognising the differ-
ence between “conscription” and “universal
service.” Conscription picks out certain citizens
and converts them into soldiers. Universal sei«
vice makes all male citizens soldiers, but leaves
them citizens. Under the one system militarism
forms a caste separate from the mass of
the people—a caste which must be either
- tyrannical or inefficient. Under the other there
is a general diffusion of the military spirit among
the people, and since every citizen is a
soldier, and every soldier a citizen, there can
be no tyranny, and the standard of efficiency will
reflect the general standard of the nation’s skill
and public spirit. Conscription takes a percentage
of the citizens away to cloister them for years in
barracks, where they may lose the home spirit and
the home virtues. Universal service breaks none
of the citizen’s home ties ; his absences from home
are too short to rupture any family relations or to
inculcate a barracks spirit. Conscription taxes the
industrial life of a nation by calling from their
work a big proportion of the young men at
the formative period of their lives, to keep
them at drill, much of which is supererogatory.
Universal service does little more than insist
that male citizens shall give up a proportion of
their holidays to the healthful and patriotic pur-
pose of training themselves for defence ; its indus-
trial effect would, therefore, be salutary rather
than otherwise, and add to, rather than detract
from, the national wealth. Conscription may turn
out a better-drilled soldier ; universal service will
make a better-trained citizen, and, except in the
highly technical branches of warfare, a man good
enough to defend his country. Conscription is
perhaps the best policy for a nation with Imperial
ambitions, anxious to train up an army of veterans ;
universal service is the defence method for a
nation which wants to go about its business
peaceably, with no warsof conquest and annexation,
but a reliable gun handy if any enemy attempts
aggression.
The Drunk and the Moderately Drunk.
In N.S.W. statistical publications nowadays
(Statistician Cogiilan being in England) the
State’s borrowing disease is treated with a good
deal less disrespect than it deserves. The
Mon i'll i, y Statistical Bulletin for August
shows total loan expenditure for 1904-5 as
£1,510,489; a month previously the Auditor-
General had certified that it was £1,570,669. But
that discrepancy is a comparatively small matter.
There is a greater one in regard to the description
of the works in which Cohen’s money has been
sunk. The Statistician’s publication drafts the
expenditure under two main heads—“ Repro-
ductive Works” and “Other Works and
Services.” The year’s total for the reproductive
works is represented at £1,210,613; for the other
sort, £299,876. The Auditor-General, in his dis-
section, calls the undertakings reproductive, par-
tially reproductive, and unproductive ; the latter
two total, on his figuring, £366,316. The repro-
ductive works, according to the Statistician’s publi-
cation, include water conservation, on which appar-
ently £42,816 was spent last year. The expenditure
in running this branch amounted to £14,657 ;
and the only items of revenue that can be traced
are “ Watering stock (tanks, &C.) £444,” and
“ Rent of public watering places, &c., £6324.” So
far from being a reproductive work, therefore, the
water-conservation branch seems to call for a sub-
sidy of over £ for £to pay working expenses. As
a matter of hard fact, there is no justification
for putting even the Railways under the heading of
reproductive undertakings, wherefore even the
Auditor-General’s classification is too generous.
Last year the Auditor-General published with his
report a valuable table (which for some reason has
been dropped this year), showing that, since 1891,
the N.S. W. railways have only twice paid the in-
terest bill, and for 13 years' they show a net
shortage of £2,154,190. In the past year they
added £54,887 to the deficit; and it is stretching
things a long way to label as “reproductive”
undertakings that have only paid their way
two years in 14. Would the Auditor-General
certify that a man was a sober person because,
though he was drunk 12 days in a fortnight, he
was moderately upright on the two Sundays when
the pubs were shut ? If, however, the Auditor-
General means that this man was sober compared
with the other man who took something home in
a bottle, and so kept up the Saturday drunk on
the Sunday, the matter is different. But in that
case it might be just as well to add a note to this
effect to any statement regarding the amount of
N.S. Wales’ “reproductive” debt—that, with
luck, it is reproductive one year in seven, while
the “partially reproductive” is less reproductive
than that.

The N.S.W. Arbitration Act.
The Carruthers Government proposes to deal
with the N.S. W. Arbitration Act in a character-
istic Carruthersy way. The Cabinet wishes to
abolish the Act; that is part of the price it is
pledged to pay to its Tory supporters. At the
same time it is afraid to propose, m a straightfor-
ward fashion, either repeal or vital amendment,
being not at all satisfied that there is a Parliamen-
tary majority prepared to ratify such a breach of
faith with the country, which consented to the
Arbitration experiment for a certain term, that
has not yet expired. The first indication of the
trend of the Government’s attitude was provided
when it “hung up” the operations of the Act
for a long period by failing to appoint a. Judge to
preside in the Arbitration Court. Obviously the
idea was then—as The Bulletin pointed out —to
quietly strangle the Act. But the growth of
public indignation upset that plan. Now a more
determined, though equally sneaking and under-
hand, effort is to be made to accomplish the same
purpose. The Arbitration Court is to be left in
existence, but side by side with it is to be
set up another industrial tribunal modelled
on the Wages Boards of Victoria. This new
tribunal is to have in all cases concurrent
jurisdiction with the Arbitration Court; it
will be also an appellate body from the
Arbitration Court’s decisions, which it may
vary or reverse ; and no case can be carried to the
Arbitration Court without the consent of both
parties and the decision of both parties not to go
before the Wages Board. It requires no special
knowledge of law to recognise that under this
system the power and usefulness of the Arbi-
tration Court must absolutely disappear. The
use of an Industrial Court, to which recourse
could only be had in the case of an agree-
ment by both parties, and against the decisions of
which an appeal could be made to another body
of inferior standing, would be nil. The only
reason why it should be permitted to survive after
this shearing of its responsibilities would be to


Even the most rigid of Tories are now
beginning to see the natural effect of Chinese
slavery on the Rand. From the Englishman
(India): —
The seriousness of this attitude consists chiefly in the
fact that for various reasons, but chiefly as the result of
the importation of Chinese labor, British immigration
into the Transvaal has ceased ; and as the
British element already in South Africa is either not so
prolific as the Dutch, or becomes imbued with Dutch
proclivities, the future of South Africa almost certainly
remains with the Boers. When we remember their indu-
rated character, and the abnormal length of their memo-
ries, it is difficult to regard the growing evidences of their
disaffection without serious qualms for the future.

The Harvester Trust Again.
The agents of the Yankee Trust which claims
to control 90 per cent, of the world’s trade in har-
vesting machinery are deadly anxious to get a
grip on the remaining 10 per cent., and, as a st; p
to that object, to wipe out the Australian manu-
facturers. The latest move has been the circula-
lation of a “special issue” of an alleged news
paper (which does not seem to be registered
at the Post Office) devoted to lying slanders of
the Australian manufacturers who have dared
to give a patriotic refusal to the offers
of the Trust, and wouldn’t sell out and
thus leave the Australian farmer at the mercy
of the folk who control the Standard Oil concern.
The Yankee Harvester Trust cannot, however,
argue out of the fact that the Harvester is an Aus-
tralian invention, which has been copied in
America and exported thence to compete with the
original inventors, and that it has been made for
very many years in Australia by different firms
under competitive conditions, at steadily de-
creasing prices. There is no reasonable chance
under Australian conditions of the manu-
facture of Harvesters falling into the hands
of a local trust, which would unfairly raise the
price to the farmer. If such an unlikely event
occurred, a prompt remedy would be always avail-
able to Parliament. But as a matter of fact, the
local makers are prepared to guarantee sale at a
lower price if adequate Protection is granted to
them. All the interests of Australia, agricultural
and industrial, are thus bound up with the success
of the Australian Harvester. If the American
Trust succeeds, not only will the Commonwealth
lose a flourishing industry, but the Australian
farmer will be liable to be bled white by the
Yankee exploiters. These trusts and combines
are not run for fun. Their method is invariably
to spend freely until they win a monopoly, and
then to bleed freely.
Comic-songster Bent had a jovial evening down
at Tatura (Vic.) last week, when the locals covered
him with congratulations re the Government’s
closer-settlement purchase of Wyuna estate.
Wyuna is Bent’s “bit of all right” among the
land purchases. But when the Overnewton estate
comes to be talked about, there’ll be no buttering
of Bent. He’ll sing no comic songs to the congre-
gated residents of the districts thereabouts. Over-
newton is going to be a matter for humble apology
or vociferous bluff—probably the latter. At
Tatura the other night Premier Tom was on
a lamentably good wicket. The president of
Rodney shire entertained him at dinner, and the
shire aforesaid is the favored country which
had its irrigation liability wiped off the slate.
Every acre of land on the west side of the Goul-
burn in the Rodney Trust is worth fully £2 more
than the non irrigated land of the east side. If
the Government had a sense of common justice it
Would even now repudiate the sinful cancelling
of that irrigation debt, and tax the improved land
according to its deserts. But as there is no appa-
rent fear of the fatted Rodneyites being made
to do the square thing until the Australian Govern-
ment taxes their land, they can afford to boil over
with enthusiasm whilst burly Thomas plays the
giddy ox. “ Right loud they laugh with counter-
feited glee at all his jokes, for many a joke hath
he.” The glee is not necessarily counterfeited in
the Rodney case. Bent is the sort of humorist
who really appeals to the hearts of farmers.
“L.C.” : Local Government is a grand institu-
tion, but in many Vic. shires the thing is just
bureaucracy on a small scale. Our Council is com-
posed of squatters, large farmers, and two or
three smaller men dependent on the others.
When there is an election, it is merely a sporting
event or the outcome of feudal animosity between
classes. An engineer and secretary are appointed,
or the offices are combined, as in our case; and
the officer runs the show. He is also the valuer,
and has a deep-rooted conviction that for rating
purposes the squattoeracy’s land is the poorest on
earth. He is allowed private practice and various
perks, which bring his income up to £5OO a year
or more. His private practice often takes him
away from the office for the better part of a week,
and his actual working time costs the shire
anything from £2 per day. He is allowed to
charge contractors a fee of per cent, on amount
of contract (maximum, £1) for preparing plans;
these fees, of course, are ultimately paid by the
ratepayers. The revenue of the shire is upwards
of £5OOO, and salaries and office expenses run to
over £5O0 —which sum has to be paid before any-
thing can be done to roads, &c. The councillors,
of course, have good, solid reasons for being
satisfied with their officer, and no complaint,
however well founded, has an “ earthly.” More-
over, the ratepayers have to be satisfied with their
councillors, for few men are foolhardy enough to
oppose the landed interest. Thus reform is only
remotely possible, and the danger of being ousted
a negligible quantity.
