Last week Senator Soapy Gray, of N.S. W., again
told in Parliament the childish old taradiddle that
“New South Wales could sell her railways for a
sum that would more than cover the National
Debt.” Surely it isn’t worth the while of a grown
man, who professes to be in danger of having in-
telligence, to make a display of himself with such
crude boastings. If any co. bought the N.S. W„
railways for a sum large enough to pay off the
State debts, that co. would get rather less than
2 per cent, on its money. Does Senator Soapy
Guay know of any syndicate which wants to in-
vest about £80,000,000 at less than 2 per cent. ?
Also, can he point to any syndicate or company
that ever offered to buy the New South Wales
railways, even at a price that would pay
off half the State’s public debt? If a
company bought the Victorian lines at a price
that would pay off the Victorian public debt that
co. would make, at latest figures, 3 per cent, on its
money, so Victoria should have a much greater
chance of finding the sort of syndicate that Senator
Soapy Gray refers to than N.S.W. has—and Vic-
toria’s chance is just about nil. The kind of syn-
dicate which wants to invest over £50,000,000 at
3 per cent, is about as rare as the sea serpent.
But Victoria doesn't send to Parliament a petty
braggart of the Gray species to bring the State
into contempt and general dislike by his attitudes
of superiority, and that is something to its credit.
It is N.S.W.’s ill-luck that it'should be so largely
represented by Foreign-trade persons like Gray,
whose pomp and brag and airs of patronage recall
the mental attitude of the nigger field-marshal
when he has just composed his new uniform and
goes to review his standing army of two more
niggers.

State Premier Carruthers, of N.S. Wales, has
started early to manufacture a new grievance
against the Commonwealth Government. The
Braddon clause, which provides for a return to
the States of three-fourths of the tariff
revenue, can’t expire for five years yet, and
it won’t expire then unless Parliament formally
abolishes it. Unless it is formally abolished it
goes on for ever. But Carruthers, looking from
five to 500 years ahead, has written to Prime
Minister Deakin to demand that after the Brad-
don clause is abolished the Constitution shall be
altered “ to provide for the return of an adequate
fixed amount of revenue to each State.” In
this matter Carruthers is supposed to repre-
sent himself and various other State Premiers, but
these people evidently haven’t agreed among
themselves as to what is an adequate
fixed amount —at least, they don’t mention
any adequate amount. Presumably, the idea
is that the Federal Government should find
out what kind of a fixed amount will satisfy a
batch of State Premiers each of whom wants the
earth, and each of whom has an idea that
everything is a conspiracy to rob his State for the
benefit of all the other States, and that Deakin’s
failure to achieve this impossibility shall supply
Carruthers with another grievance. It would be
an interesting experiment if Prime Minister
Deakin replied simply that he would be glad to
hear from the State Premiers again when they
had all fully agreed among themselves as to how
much “fixed amount” each State should get, and
intimated that any State Premier who was killed
in the discussion would be buried at Federal ex-
pense. But though this would be a pleasant jest,
it is better left alone. The business of the Aus-
tralian Parliament is to entirely cease subsidising
the States, and to compensate them for the
loss of their tariff revenue by taking over
their debts, their old age pension responsibilities,
and their costly Education Departments. Every-
thing that reduces the necessity for communication
between the Australian Government and the
State Premiers is something gained for the cause
of union.

That vague, shadowy animal known as Pre-
ferential Trade wandered in an aimless fashion
through the Victorian Chamber of Manufactures
the other night. It was first dragged in to the
premises by manufacturer I. Jacobs, and then it
was a proposal that there should be a difference
of 10 to 20 per cent, of import duties, in favor
of all manufactures coming from the “Mother”
Country, with the proviso that the basis and
the guide in fixing all duties in the Common-
wealth tariff should be “the difference in
the relative cost of production in Great Britain
and Australia.” Second manufacturer J. Pender
objected to the shapelessness of the creature, and
suggested that Australia should demand the
original Kingston tariff as against Britain, and
25 per cent, more against every other country.
Third manufacturer W. A. Hartnoll said that
until Great Britain became Protectionist, and had
something to offer in return for Australia’s
preference, the preferential creature could be
nothing more than the shadow of a dream. Fourth
manufacturer J. K. Henderson asked what the
Australian tariff was, anyhow, and what was the
use of it if it wasn’t going to shut out the cheap
English competition, which in many lines was more
dangerous than any other form of competition.
Finally the preferential animal wandered forth
from the Chamber in the following form: —
That this Chamber reaffirms its approval of preferential
trade with Great Britain, subject to conditions giving
adequate Protection to Australian industries ; the prefer-
ence to Great Britain to be up to 25 per cent., and in
all such preference, consideration to be given to the
individual requirements of each industry.
And that is the extent to which the amorphous
creature has developed so far. When Australia*
by putting all outsiders really outside, has built
up healthy manufactures of its own, the pre-
ferential animal may possibly be belted into some
shape or other. And just as likely it may not.

For her last week in Sydney Miss Nance
O’Neil is going to the tomb and bringing forth a
Lazarus every night. Saturday it was “Ingomar,”
hitherto only played at a matinee; Monday,
“ Hedda Gabler ” ; Tuesday, “Marie Antoinette”;
“ Magda” for the Wednesday matinee, and Little
Billee s girl for the evening. Then, for to-night
(Thursday) “ The Fires of St. John,” put on thus
late so that Miss O’Neil may fly to her ’Frisco
boat if Sydney votes it too shocking and
raises a revolution. On Friday Miss O’Neil is
to give one act each from five plays, in-
cluding a “sleep-walking scene,” a “flower
scene,” and a “curse scene.” In the “In-
gomar” production she is Parthenia—a rather
poor Parthenia, failing in the lighter moods, and
getting but one, or at most two, chances to show
off her special tragic powers. But, in the costume
of a Greek maiden, Nance looks her very best.
She doesn’t have to change her clothes all the
evening, and that is good, because no change
could be for the better. Talking about clothes,
hairy Ingomar lets out a secret apparently without
intending to do it. The rather stilted old play is
about the Power of Love. Parthenia talks
a great deal of grandiloquent rubbish on the sub-
ject—the sort of thing you can get for a penny
with a paper pattern and advice on the subject of
cosy corners, and what to do with chapped hands,
and what to say when the Duchess of Shalots
ealls. Parthenia says she doesn’t under-
stand it all, and neither does anybody
else ; but, having quoted “Two souls with but a
single thought, two hearts that beat as one, ” the
hairy heathen and barbarian, Ingomar, lies down
on the grass and repeats it in rapture, just as
the old lady did that blessed word Mesopotamia.
It is all so beautiful, so sentimental, until Ingo-
mar remarks that Parthenia isn’t a bit like his
own heathen, barbarian women—they wear rough
skins for clothes, and don’t do up their hair
nicely, and never go to the massage parlor, or
brush their teeth. Parthenia doesn’t notice the
remark, of course—she’s too busy, like the rest of
the girls who go to matinees, in hoaxing her-
self that the beginning of love is some inscrutable,
poetic thing. But Ingomar had really given the
whole business away. Miss O’Neil’s support in
this piece was rather better than in some
others—John Glendinning’s Ingomar and Mario
Majeroni’s Polydor being specially worth men-
tion. But Gaston Mervale’s Massilian was very
bad ; the boss Greek’s remarks were, no doubt,
highly interesting, but most of the time they were
utterly unintelligible.
* * * *
A good deal of the Ibsen dramatic art is
to pourtray impossible characters in a series of
common-place conversations. The conversations
are “ natural.” Therefore the play is counted so,
though it may be made up of most unnatural inci-
dents and personages. “Hedda Gabler” shows
this art—it is art of an out-of-the-way kind, like
Japanese chinaware and Beardsley black-and-
white—in almost its highest expression. Juaged
by dialogue and trend of incident, the play is an
ordinary picture of domestic life. Really, it is
the pourtrayal of a woman far more savage and
repulsive than most of the villains of tragedy.
Yet Hedda is simply designed—merely the incar-
nation of all the bad qualities and desires of the
modern woman multiplied a hundred fold. She
is prurient, and so gives up her time before mar-
riage to extracting from a young cad in her train
of worshippers detailed information of his vices
(this they call “comradeship”). She is mean
and cowardly, and so delights in the small felini-
ties of middle-class women. She is jealous and
cruel, and doesn’t stop at destroying a precious
manuscript and inciting-an old lover to suicide be-
cause he has shown some response to the reforming
influence of another woman. She is greedy for
money and social position, and is willing to take
almost any means to get them. Withal she lacks
the saving sin of passion, and makes it brutally
plain to her man-friends that she means to remain
“ faithful ” —in the conventional sense—to her
husband. There are scores of women, unhappily,
who have some or many of these characteristics of
Hedda’s. But it is not natural to pack them all
into, one woman with no savor of a redeeming
quality. Hedda is not real; she is a savage satire
on the type of woman which the “new” move-
ment tends to produce, prurient but cold, cruel
but prudent, shrinking from the true work of
woman, but with not brains or character enough
to undertake any worthy work in another direc-
tion. Miss Nance O’Neil gives a reading of
Hedda Gabler which carries with it the fascina-
tion of the serpent. She looks always evil and
beautiful, and there is a shudder in her every
pose. It may not be the Hedda that Ibsen
imagined when he dipped his pen into the slime-
pot, but it’s a powerful and convincing horror.

At Sydney Criterion * ‘ Beauty and the
Barge ” is drawing so well that manager-actor
Brough sees no reason why he should shelve it
for another piece with more brains and less
farce. If even one of the barge hands would
dance a hornpipe in the bar-room scene, it would
make the proceedings more interesting, and this
chronicler would call again. But the public likes
“ B. and the 8.,” so a solitary growler- is neither
here nor there —especially there.

The Palace Theatre will exhibit to Sydney
folks on Saturday and succeeding nights a
“Famous Bio-Tableau and All-Star Picture
Show.” It is to include “authentic ” episodes of
the late war, and if they really are authentic
it will be an event in the history of a lying and
flickersome instrument, which has cheek enough
to present a picture of the battle of Nicopolis or
the battle of Hastings, and swear that the thing
was taken on the spot. Still, J. 0. Williamson
runs the show, and his word counts for much.
It is at least a guarantee that the flicker will be
reduced to a minimum.

“Between Two Women” will be with-
drawn from Sydney Royal after Friday, and on
Saturday “A Woman of Pleasure” is to be staged.
This is an exciting mixture of melodrama and
circus—the circus element being introduced in
scenes showing the relief of Mafeking, the town
which bore up, almost till its last dozen of ■paU de
foie gras, against a savage besieger who mostly
wasn’t there, and amused itself writing de-
spatches to the English papers about its diet of
curried cockroaches and mosquito soup. There
are many humorous possibilities about the siege of
Mafeking—if Mr. Anderson’s co. would be good
enough to show them.

The more you look at the little French
couple who are doing the meehanical-Pierrot turn
at Sydney Tivoli, the cleverer you find them. At
first you don’t notice much but that the man’s
copy of an automaton is excellent; later on you
see what difficult things the automaton does in
the way of gymnastics. The billiard-ball jugglers
and the Diamond Duo continue to use up much ink
in the bills; and the pictures of the Gordon Ben-
nett motor race brought consolation during a spell
of windy days, for they show that there’s plenty
of dust m even the pleasant land of France. And
when your own eyes and ears are full, it’s com-
forting to think that others probably have a
mouthful as well.
** * *
“His Majesty's Servant,” which brought
the Jeffries-Knight co. back to Melbourne notice
at the Princess's last Saturday, is a roman-
tic drama of the gaudy, unconvincing order,
written a while ago, when costume plays were in
vogue and theatrical interest was centred in the
dead past. His Majesty is Charles the Second—
not the matured Charles who gave England a
number of Tottie duchesses and little dukes to
correspond, not the merry personage who sat in
Nell Gwynne’s high-backed chair, but the young
man Charles, with no kingdom to support his
title and pay his bills. The present drama shows
him as a fugitive, going into hiding whenever
General Lambert happens on his track. And
the “servant” of this royal dodger is Julius
Knight, disguised as Geoffrey Mohun, a
glaringly heroic actor of the period. The
Roundheads have shut down on barnstormers,
so there is nothing left for Julius but td come to
His Majesty’s rescue in time of need, and shout
for God and the King when the dramatist appeals
to his furious loyalty. An interval of nine years
between the first and second acts makes no ap-
preciable difference to the gallant Julius, or
General Lambert, or the pair of girls concerned in
the story, Charles seems to age a little, but the
others skip across the nine years as though Time
had paused for them. One of the girls, finding
that her long-winded devotion to Julius is of
no avail, accuses him of having wrecked her
reputation, or words to that effect, on the
evening of the last day when the audience saw
them together. And Julius permits the lie to go
unchallenged, because he has sworn to remain
silent for the sake of the other girl and on account
of the King. Therefore the other girl breaks off
her extraordinary nine years’ attachment to Julius,
and they part in a sad state of misunderstanding
which doesn’t upset the audience in the least.
The audience knows that the innocence of Julius
will come out strong at the finish, and that a
glorified grovel before the anointed King is bound
to ensue.
* * * *
For such stagey-minded historical plays as
“ A. Royal Divorce ” or “ Sweet Nell” this country
has a large, paying public whose sympathies are
easily captured. But the defects in “His Majesty’s
Servant ” are too obvious to admit of it becoming a
great financial success. The love-story is foolishly
improbable; the King, of whom everybody talks so
much, has little to do with it; the dialogue is
sometimes trivial and the situations are forced.
Against these defects the picturesque costumes
and tin helmets of Lambert’s troopers, and the
splendid heroics of Julius Knight are not of suf-
ficient account. The play fails respectably. It
gives the leading man a brave show, and provides
a fairly good part for Arthur Wontner as the
King who conceals himself in sundry places.
Plimmer fills the part of the humiliated Genera


Lambert. The dear “little girl” is tall Miss
Jeffries (a striking instance of misplaced nobility).
Miss Hamer, essentially modern in her speech,
has to impersonate the unreal young woman who
suddenly weeps for her alleged disgrace of nine
years back, and is not cross-examined on the sub-
ject—and there are the others of the cast. Also
there is effective staging. The co. will probably
be seen next Saturday in a revival of one of the
older dramas.

Some new variety items are cropping up
in “The Cingalee,” at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne).
The Gollywog notion has been elaborated, and
Chambhuddy Ram introduces a travesty on
Thurston’s performance that raises a laugh and
advertises the wizard in question. Apart from
these irrelevant proceedings, “The Cingalee” re-
mains as before. Six times a week Miss Mus-
grove dollywogs, but she is spared the trouble on
Wednesday afternoons, when Miss Milne dolly-
wogs very successfully in her stead.

Thurston’s long stretch of last nights at the
Athenaeum Hall will terminate on Saturday, after
which occasion Melbourne will remember him
kindly as a producer of illusions and a deceiver
ever. One is sorry to part with one’s illusions,
especially when there is a girl in the case. And
in the Thurston case there are at least three girls.

** * #
Maggie Stirling’s first concert at Mel-
bourne Town Hall settled all doubts as to whether
the popular ballad singer of six years ago had con-
verted her original Australian voice into a Euro-
pean contralto. Miss Stirling is not less of a
mezzo than ever she was. Her warble shows some
improvement in the production, achieved without
any sacrifice of its natural mellowdiousness, but
now as heretofore the singer is at her best in songs
where a sympathetic enunciation of the words is
of first importance. The selection for her opening
concerts included one or two pieces of foreign lan-
guage—notably the German version of “ Songs My
Mother Taught Me” —which might have been more
fetchingly uttered in English. The concert party
numbers only four, including the accompanist.
Miss Ethel Sinclair, a Melbourne violinist who
came back from London with Miss Stirling, is a
very likeable performer on a moderately good in-
strument, and one Adolphe Borschke, a very
young man of very girly appearance, contributes
solos on the piano. Adolphe shines as a player of
light, frivolous pieces. He has a soft, coquettish
touch when he pleases, and his smile, his com-
plexion, and his sweetness in general suggest that
he should fix up his long hair with a comb and
wear a wreath of roses on his snowy brow.
Adolphe may be described as a peach among
pianists.

The Players (now led up to the breach by
'Vivian Edwards, what time Gregan M‘Mahon is
otherwise engaged) fluttered “Lady Winder-
mere’s Fan ” at Melbourne Bijou on Saturday
evening last. No particular violence was done to
Oscar Wilde’s meaty epigrams and cynicisms,
and, take them all in all, the Players
scored once again a considerable success.
Mrs. Maurice Dudley played Mrs. Erlynne
with an intensity and truth of feeling seldom en-
countered even on the professional stage. Her
only fault is the amateur’s tendency to act too
quickly. Mr. Lawrence Hardinge, an excellent
comedian with a wide histrionic range, was not in
his most congenial element as Lord Windermere.
Also the latter part of the above clause applies
strongly to Mr. D. M. Beere. He played Lord
Darlington, the seductive tempter of innocence
who self-reprovingly relents when his efforts are
crowned with non-success, as though he had been
especially bottled to fill that sort of human jug.
Miss Georgina O’Meara and some of the others
have done better in previous struggles with polite
comedy.
* * *-
At Melbourne Opera House there is a well-
sustained rush of plain, ordinary multitude and a
steady inflow of rank and fashion. The three
magicians, together with the four Jacksons and
the balance of the company, will repeat themselves
to crowded houses for the next two or three weeks,
presumably. For the sake of the principal
“turns,” which cover nearly an hour of space
between them, a patient public deals gently, as a
rule, with the feeblest of the minor “ turns,” but
last Saturday night a lady vocalist got an impolite
reception when she came along to chant a second
ditty. It is a peculiarity of the Rickards show
that the people who might be forgiven for perpe-
trating one song only are invariably engaged to do
it twice.

Adelaide, having spent most of its money
during Show week, was not able to keep up, to the

“ Pretty Peggy.” Also, the piece is too much

running, and Nellie Stewart serves drinks to a num-
ber of local amateurs who appear as students, and
a general atmosphere of Serene Highnesses and
college-choruses and Lager Beer prevails. At the
Tivoli John F. Sheridan bounced through the
part of the footman-bailiff for some nights, but

for his farewell appearance. Frank Clark has
now come back from Broken Hill with Harry
Shine, the Stagpoles, and the rest of his variety
co., for a short so-called “ theatrical ” season of
“ Muldoon’s Picnic.”
Apropos of the alleged idea of the Kaiser
that the Baltic Sea should be closed to all ships
except those belonging to the Powers on its coast,
it is less than 60 years since Britain tried to shut
the Baltic Sea against Germany itself. In 1848,
the German coast being blockaded by the Danes,
the young Germanic Confederation, which then
had no navy, rushed enthusiastically into the
marine store business, and scratched up a fleet of
old merchant brigs and smaller craft, including
row-boats. The following year it put to sea and
challenged the Danes, and then the British
Government had the gratuitous cheek to notify
the Legislature at Frankfort that its flag—the
venerable banner of the Holy Roman Empire—
“being unknown on the seas, vessels using it ex-
pose themselves to be treated as pirates.” The
Confederation was thunderstruck, the little armada
was recalled and not long afterwards sold by
auction. And the colors which were “unknown
on the seas” were those carried by Don Juan of
Austria's ships at Lepanto.
AT POVERTY POINT.
According to a private letter from England the plump
and figuratively pleasing Annette Kellermann is quite a
considerable sensation in the Cold Country, and draws
great crowds wherever she puts in an appearance in her
somewhat scarce costume. On the occasion of an exhi-
bition swim at Ramsgate the onlookers were so enthusias-
tic that crowds waded into the water to capture the
mermaid, and buxom Annette was only rescued after
strenuous exertions on the part of eight stalwart
coppers, who bore away the dripping damsel among
them. Happy policemen! Miss Kellermann, although
she hasn’t full clothing on, and what she has is generally
wet, says that she does not find the country ( cold.
Perhaps she is so crowded that the cool breeze can’t get
at her.
Song words from “The Cingalee” are matters for grave
reflection when one finds them advertised in cold news-
papef print. For instance :
Once a Golly wog loved a Dollywog,
Wog, vvog, wog, wog.
It was folly wog of the Golly wog,
Wog, wog, wog, wog.
Surely nothing worse than that can happen in the way of
comedy drivel. The one thing that can be said in “ The
Cingalee’s ” defence is that this particular rubbish wasn’t
in the original.

It was announced by no less an authority than London
Tedegraph that Miss Carrie Moore would take principal
part in the new musical corned}', “ The Blue Moon,”

At Thurston’s show in Melbourne, the other night, the
magician had the luck to get a singularly smart kiddie
from the audience to assist him in his egg trick, which
depends largely on the little stranger for humorous effect.
Thurston takes the first youngster who is willing to
mount the platform, and as often as not he draws a blank
—a mere stodgy juvenile with no idea of entering into the
joke. This night the casual kiddie was an enormous
success. It turned out to be the young hopeful of
Brightie Smith, of Bland Holt’s co. The infant Smith
has “ been on” in melodrama more than once, and is
quite at home on the stage.
George Elton (son of Billy Elton), lately with William-
son and Musgrove, now understudies Cyril Maude, of the
Hayinarket, London. Alice Crawford, the Bendigo girl,
is also in the company, and appeared with success as
Josephine in “ The Creole ” to Mr. Maude’s Napoleon.
Joseph Gillott, formerly well-known as a musician in
London, is having a comic opera of his composition pro-
duced in Sheffield, England. The opera is said to be ex-
cellent stuff, yet it took composer Joseph years to secure
the Sheffield production, whilst as for the London
managers, he found it impossible even to secure appoint-
ments with them to hear the work. Gillott is a nephew
of Melbourne’s Samuel.

“ 0.K.” : The alleged increase of musical enthusiasm in
Melbourne is not noticeable. Probably there was a falling
off this winter in the attendance at Marshall Hall’s
orchestral concerts, for there never was any overflow,
although the seating accommodation had been lessened
by the Prof.’s device of extending the platform several
feet forward. Some capital chamber music concerts were
poorly patronised. Hugo Heermann passed unnoticed
except by the small-sized crowd who swore his cold,
classic fiddling was worth sixpence a minute in spiritual
satisfaction. And now it seems that the two liedertafels —
the Metropolitan and the Melbourne—that have existed
on their respective owns for 30 years or more, are about to
roll themselves into a single body under the name of the
Royal Victorian Liedertafel ; which means, of course, that
both of the old institutions have been losing subscribers.
“Willbra” : See by The Buldetw that a long-haired
person has been recently exploiting his experiences
in New Guinea. Said gentleman did really pay a
visit to this country, and spent some weeks here,
mostly at sea. So he didn’t have many hairbreadth
escapes from wandering cannibals or wild missionaries.
He has found one of the lost tribes in the Kiwais (mouth
of the Fly River), and must have had considerable oppor-
tunity of observing them, as the Ysabel stops at Daru for
several hours. Apart from their distinctly Hebraic
appearance—quite common in Polynesia—the only points
of similarity between the Kiwais and the Badly-Chosen
People consist in their superior intelligence and inordinate
lust for the Papuan equivalent of woman and wine.
“Hiphen”: I witnessed “Leah Kleshna,” which is to
be played here shortly, in the Cold Country. It is a strong
play with a weak ending-such a kind of ending that,
were it a prize-fight, the referee would stop it and give
the money to charity. Leah Kleshna is the daughter of a
professional thief, and is herself no slouch at the game.
Being a good-looking wench, she has great success in
rifling the rooms of timid and bashful young men, who,
when they discover her, have to request her to leave the
apartment while they slip on their—ahem—dressing gown
before ringing up the police. Leah, however, meets
her Waterloo when she tries to separate Paul Sylvain, a
French Deputy (who is the Tommy Bent of his
country), from his family jewels. When he dis-
covers her in the dead o’ night, she promises him
a funeral with the utmost celerity and a revolver. But
he is undismayed, and starts in to save the soul which he
is sure exists beneath the low-cut evening dress in vvhich
Leah is more or less encased. Then follows an impassioned
scene (which comes near to rant), and finally Paul Sylvain
Bent leaves her alone with the jewels, and tel}s her to take
them or leave them, and to please herself. Thereupon,
the dormant good in Leah is much touched, and she goes
out leaving the ger-littering jewels on the table. They
are discovered there by the villain, Paul’s prospective
brother-in-law, who enters (naturally) by the window,
full of evil intent and wine. He also for some time past
has been trying to save Leah for himself. The grass
being short in his paddocks at the time through losses
in Tattersalls, he takes the diamonds, and when Paul
finds them gone he thinks he has made a mistake, and
calls himself an ass, and brays. The matter is cleared up,
however, and Leah retires to her native home in Poland.
In the weak-kneed last act she is discovered some years
later in the company of bestial-looking Poles,
chasing slugs off a lettuce plantation. Enter Paul
the Spruce, who has meantime been jilted by his fiancee
because of the discovery of some of Leah’s wardrobe in
his room on that memorable night. He tells her that for
him the sun only shines through her front teeth, and
that though he is not a land-agent, he has a bit in the
savings bank. Whereupon Leah retires to change her
clogs, and they start for the Registry-office. The play
ends by the curtain falling.
Dan Barry doesn’t confine his audacious eloquence to
the bush townships. He gives it to them just as strong
when he is touring the outskirts of Melbourne. Witness
this lovely quote fropi a poster advertising “The Parson’s
Nose ” or “ The Parson’s Oath ” at a suburban town hall: —
“ Never before have such immense, delighted and repre-
sentative throngs been seen in the above hall as those
which each week greet our popular performances. To hear
the hearty, healthy, hilarious merriment, and watch the
sea of human faces bright and vital with intellectual
interest and happy enjoyment is in itself a source of the
keenest human delight. The adults present of all ages,
from the bright-eyed maiden in her teens, with her best
boy at her side, to the greybeard of venerable aspect, with
his wife, the companion of his declining years, at his
elbow, all greet our performances with cheers upon
cheers, while to the little ones present our weekly drama-
tic entertainments come as a delicious deluge of delight.”

Ghost, “ O ’Amlet, I will ’arrow up thysoul! O ’orrible!
O ’orrible ! most ’orrible ! ”
Adelaide Theatre Royal closes at the end of this
month to allow of extensive alterations inside. Already
workmen have been pushing on with the improvements
for some months, and at times actors have had to dress
in half dressing-rooms, and hang their trousers on drain-
pipes, etc. At a matinee during the Knight-Jeffries
recent season, plumbers began nailing on the roof just
when Knight was doing a fine bit of silent acting. The
audience was sorry it was Knight who was acting, and
not George Rignold. Henry V.’s Soliloquy to a Plumber
on the Roof would have been worth hearing.
A London danseuse, engaged for minor parts on the
Sydney boards, stood out for several days for a written
guarantee that she would be entertained on arrival in
Sydney by the gaud Mayor and aldermen. Her mother
was a Gaiety Girl, and she had grown up to think that all
Sydney Mayors are as cheerful as Sammy Lees.
A woman who had made a specialty of fasting exhi-
bitions was recently found by the German police in a
destitute state. She lamented that she could not get a
fasting - engagement to provide herself with food !
Melbourne Town Hall will shortly 7 be fitted up with a
boss organ. For vears it had one which was worse than a
treadmill to make music on. The new one is electro-
pneumatic and a marvel of scientific skill. The whole
business is worked by electricity. About 60 switches and
five keyboards give the organist complete control of
the fine instrument. The great feature about the new
contrivance from an organist’s point of view is that
he can arrange four different combinations of stops before
he commences to play 7 and can bring each of them into
operation whenever he pleases by merely flicking on a
switch. The thing is all switch.
Comedian Hugh Ward strikes success wherever he goes.
At latest he was the central figure of a “ Revue ” at the
Olympia, in Paris, and the papers were saying all
sorts of good things about him, with notes of exclamation
liberally interspersed to show that admiration could go no
further. The Echo de Paris remarks tha,t Ward dances
and gesticulates with a “fantasie irresistible,” whilst Le
Journal says that “ Under the appearance of a scarecrow
that extraordinary fellow, half ghost and half man, makes
his appearance in the piece. He doesn’t speak, he gesticu-
lates, he mimics, he dances. Metis quel mime et quel
danseur!” Incidentally the Journal calls a scarecrow a
“ Sara-Orow.” Ward is shortly off to his native America
on a nine months’ engagement.
“T.D.”: Passing ’Arry Rickards in Leicester Square
to-day, after putting in a couple of hours torment at
London Coliseum last night, set me a-thinking. Had
’Arry put on any one of the turns in Sydney or Mel-
bourne, with the single exception of a well-staged scene
from “ Faust,” with Tasmania’s Pringle as Mephisto, the
thing would have been howled off. The Coliseum, with
its revolving stage and a heap of money 7 spent in scenic
effects and chorus and supernumerary, is just a panorama
of good old has-beens, yet it is crowded regularly by the
admirably patient Cockney, who has not ceased to
worship 'people for the magic of their names and the
things they used to do twenty or thirty years ago. Here
were to be seen on the one night Rutland Barrington
(degenerated from comic opera starring to the comedian’s
dotage of dying at the end of a pathetic ballad surrounded
by much limelight), Mabel Love (who may have been
a passable singer ten years ago, but certainly isn’t now),
a wheezy Courtice Pounds, and worst of all, the Gown
Trotter. Last-mentioned lady appeared as Columbine in
the big scene from “ Pagliacci,” and accentuated her
thinness and angularity and very flat feet a hundredfold
in “ tu-tu ” skirts and painful efforts to twirl a la
Genee, of the Empire ballet. Barrington and Brown
Potter beautifully 7 illustrate the seventh stage of that
Being Apart, the mummer ; the male kind always wants
to die pathetically, and show that he might have been a
great tragedian ; and the female kind strives hard to
make her final kick that of an eighteen-year-old coryphee.
David Belasco, the opponent of the American Trust as
applied to the drama, has been say’irig things to a London
newspaper. He prophecies that unless the English stage
takes warning, London managers will be absorbed by
combinations engineered in New York. The complaint
in the Great Fog at present is that the theatres are run,
not so much for the benefit of the performance or the
playgoer, as for that of the middleman, who sits like the
Old Man of the Sea on the shoulders of almost every
manager in London.
The Earl of Shaftesbury, who one time appeared in Mel-
bourne (in “ Lelamine ”) for one consecutive week as a
comic-opera tenor, is now on the directorate of the Royal
Academy of Music. Shaftesbury, married to a daughter
of the Duke of Westminster, is a person to be taken quite
seriously.

could write one, and work it in for yourself, couldn t
you?” I couldn’t decide whether I should be a sailor

Touching the difference between genuine musical prodi-
gies of the Mozart order, and the laboriously trained
youngsters who are mostly the offspring of music teachers,
somebody remarks that “the prodigy ousn ess of the real
prodigy consists in his achieving the greatest results with
the smallest amount of practice, whilst with others a little
is achieved bv prolonged exercises. The prodigy is born
with abnormal executive aptitude, which may or may not
be allied with musical intelligence, but it enables the child
to make the utmost of all he has.” And the marvellous
infant musician is invariably a boy. For that matter, the
onlv wonderful child mummer on record was a boy—
Master Betty—who lived for 80 years after he had earned
a fortune by playing Hamlet, Macbeth, &c. The natural
precocity of girls is limited in its power of artistic achieve-
ment whereas the unnatural talent of the occasional
urchin can hardly be overstrained. Unlike the girls, he
shows no sense of his peculiar cleverness —has no airs
and graces when off duty. Master Betty was just a
bright, intelligent kiddie w 7 ith a prodigious memory. He
learned the longest Shakspeare parts quite easily, re-
membered every thing he was told to do in acting them,
and between the acts he played marbles w 7 ith a mate.
His troubles about being a prodigy ! The boy was not even
as a girl is when she comes before the public,
[n his early manhood he made his re-appearance behind
the footlights, because there was a demand for him, but
finding himself no great shakes he soon retired for good.
“ L.E.” on art: —
Ballarat, the art centre of the universe, lately broke
out with violence over the matter of painting a damp
wall. The president (James Oddie, F.G.S.) thought one
of the Gallery walls should be lined with wood to keep
out the wet; but the secretary, reckoning- both
sides at £l2 a side, thought woodwork would be
too expensive, so he got an estimate for painting.
The president took him before the council tor
insubordination. He had no right to mention either
(1) wood on both sides, or (2) painting. The angry and
ancient president likewise accused the secretary of having
pledged his (the ancient’s) pictures to the Government
while he was away in Fogland. Th's ancient took the chair,
ruled some people out of order who tried to argue w.th hun,
and suppressed people »nd things enthusiastically, till the
Council bubbled over With wrath. The secretary denied
the charge and said he lad nothing- to apologise for. Ihe
aggressively artistic people present spoke their minds as
freely about that wall as ever did the old Athenians over
a new statue by Phidias. Many would have engaged in a
sanguinary civil war rather than yield one inch of wood-
work on that wall that let the wet through. Finally the
Council nased a unanimous resolution that the secretary
had not been guiltv of anything. r lhereupon the flabber-
gasted president said that out of the 100 pictures he had
loaned to the Gallery he would now remove &0 to the
lunatic asylum at Wendou ee. One of the committee men
who had attended that pleasant evening’s discussion fell
down dead before midnight. They fight hard for art iu
Ballarat.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. ivill be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

On the public examination of W. M. M.
Arnold in the Sydney Bankruptcy Court,
it was sworn that Mr. Cornelius Delon ery,
a member of the Public Service Board, owed the
bankrupt £1069 7s. 3d., but that he had com pro-
mised with his creditor by a payment of about
ss. in the £. Mr. Delohery’s Board lately dis-
missed a clerk (who had paid a great deal more
in the £ to his creditors than did Mb. Delohery
to this particular creditor) because he was unable
to make any further payments.
Launceston (Tas.) is too beastly good.
Some considerable time ago the gaol was shut
up for want of occupants, and handed over as a
refuge for old women. Now even the ordinary
“ lock-up ” of the police station is rusting its life
out. Often a week goes by without a single
record. But Launceston is a go-ahead little place.
For instance. Milestone is very plentiful, and for
want of the lawful (or unlawful) stone-breakers m
gaol a chippy little electric breaker has been set
up-

Yes, there is no doubt that the Jap. is

A most complicated case :
St. Vincent’s Hospital (Melb.) art union was at least
successful in introducing- a champion liar to notice.
William Patrick Naughton, the person convicted of cap-
turing £5OO of art-union prizes by fraud, has thusfar made
three statements as to how the swindle was worked.
Firstly he told a detective that he spirited the winning
tickets, in a Thurstonian manner, from certain bundles of
unsold tickets. Then he nullified this unconvincing-story
with another confession to the effect that his ballot-barrel
was a faked apparatus, whereby the winning marbles were
ejected by a secret spring- from a tube where he had
hidden them. Finally, on his day of judgment, the varie-
g-ated liar made a long speech from the dock, describing
himself as a poor, innocent Naughton, who never knew
where the winning tickets came from, but had amiably
assisted a friend (his intended brother-in-law) to convert
them into cash. His previous statements, he told the
court, had been forced from him by the detective, who
promised to “ hush the matter up ” in the interests of
the unfortunace hospital if he (the prisoner) would
made over all his money to the Mother Rectress for the
sake of appearances. After the “liush-up” he was to
get his money back and be discharged without a stain on
his character. This amazing and abortive narrative
seems to have been meant to display Naughton as a
victim of a R.C. detective’s sectarian enthusiasm, but in
its plan of construction it was the weakest of the three
yarns in his repertoire. In giving it an anti-Catholic
tone Naughton may have hoped to get the sympathy of
any Orangeman among- the jury, and perhaps he is
rightly described in the prison register as a Scotchman
and a Wesleyan. On the other hand, it is more probable
that he lied about his nationality and his religion, and
will presently describe himself as a Baptist by the name
of Brown.
» ¥ * m
William P. Naughton, the ballot-box
expert, who was found guilty of getting at the
St. Vincent’s Hospital art union for all the prize-
money, is said to have been mixed up with some
peculiar circumstances in Maoriland. He bossed
the drawing of a Sisters of Mercy art union in
Auckland, and all the principal prizes were
won by the girl he was engaged to. There
were suspicions then, but William P. Naughton
threatened large libel actions, and as no one knew
more than that he seemed to be engaged to a very
lucky girl, the voice of slander died away in the
dim, dim distance. Now the voice of slander is
calling out pretty loudly again.
* * » *
Wild rumors shook Geelong (Vic.) to its
foundations the other day. Bent was visiting an
agricultural show in the Sleeping City, and rolled
out of the train at North Geelong, in company
with a veiled lady, with whom he walked to
Osborne House, which the Government has
bought as a consumptive home, or a summer resi-
dence for Tallbottle, or something. Then Bent
appeared at Geelong without the lady. By
evening Common Report was as big and as strong
as a horse, and smelt like a herd of over-ripe ele-
phants, and all male Geelong was wondering
whether it would be breach of privilege or lese
majeste, or the other thing, to go out and inspect
Bent’s tenant. Some people said she was Bent’s
beautiful and accomplished relative, while others
said it was shocking. And Geelong felt vastly
disappointed when it learned that the Veiled One
was a caretaker, and that she had her husband
with her, and that Bent hadn’t a suspicion upon
his character.
* * » *
A young girl was cremated in Adelaide
the week after the Medical Congress met there.
The crematorium had not been called into use for
nearly a year, and the visiting doctors who wanted
to see the plant in operation had to be content
with the burnt ruins of a sheep. Immediately
afterwards came the genuine call. This is the
first time a woman’s body has been cremated in
Tortoise Town.

A FAMILY
DISASTER.
FATHER:
“ Good
Heavens,
my son,
what's hap-
pening?”
SON: “ Mother's just sacked the cook."
There is nothing in all the squalid records
of the police courts to compare for squalor with
the case in which the leading characters consist of
the Chinaman, and the white woman who is
married to a Chinaman, and the half-caste offspring,
and the kind of white man and woman who
associate with Chinamen. An instance is the case
of Leigh, who wanted £5OO damages from Eynch,
which trouble straggled at great dingy length
through the Sydney papers last week. It became
dismally uninteresting as it crawled along, but
taking a casual glance over it one met constantly
remarks about Mr. Ah Duck, about Mrs. Ah
Duck who was really Mrs. Phillips but who was
“supported” by Mr. Ah Duck, about the man
who. lived by bottle-washing and had been
married by Parson George Hay to a woman
who was out on bail charged with vagrancy,
about the woman who smoked opium and came
home drunk, about the beer that was sent
for and paid for with the drunken man’s
money, about Jimmy Lee the Chinaman, about
the lady who had two drinks and was then found
in bed with a strange, intoxicated citizen, who was
trodden on, and woke up, and proceeded to fight,
about the row in a three-roomed wooden house
with a broken “colonial oven ” in it, and about
multitudes Of small, stuffy details of the same sort.
Exactly how it ended The Bulletin doesn’t re-
collect, but all through it there were beer, and
Chinamen, and three-roomed wooden houses, and
abusive language, and ladies who indignantly
denied having ever been charged with anything
worse than vagrancy, immorality or drink. And
all through it could be faintly heard the voice of
the undesirable alien saying “ Whaffor ? ”
Joseph Ward, of Maoriland, lately ex-
pressed a hope that some member of the Royal
Family would come out to open the International
Exhibition, at Christchurch, next year. Perhaps
a cheap line of royal goods may be obtained ; the
British royal family is so prolific that princelets
are fairly common.
“ G.H.”: The loss of the manyth conse-
cutive hat in a barber’s shop suggests that it is up
to somebody to invent a venomous hat that will
bite anyone but the lawful owner. The teeth in
the hat should be sufficiently poisonous to slay the
thief fatally dead on the spot. Failing that, a hat
that would bark loudly when taken by the wrong
party, and thus show up the illicit hat-taker,
would be an excellent idea. I know
a lot of men who would be will-
ing to put money into a com-
pany for manufacturing un-
stealable hats.
“ Den ” : The stern
mother of invention bears
strange children at times.
Necessity is evidently pro-
lific in Adelaide during
the cold winter months,
In the park-lands all
the trees have been
stripped of dead tim-
ber as far up as a
man can reach,
and stray
scraps of
packing
cases are
snapped up
and fought
over by the
fire - wood-
hunting poor.
Strolling
through the
East Park
Lands t’other
day, I saw a
large able-
bodied man fish-
ing for firewood
in the tree tops.
His tackle com-
prised a length of rope to the
end of which was fastened a
short iron bar. He cast the
iron into the upper branches
of a likely tree and then
hauled away. Sometimes he
got a bite, but more frequently
he cast without result. A cripple
stood by offering congratulations when a good haul
was made, and collecting the hard earned fire-
wood in a bag.

A pompous Sydney barrister with political
aspirations cross-examining witness in a dismissal
case, the other day : “ Did your employer ever in-
timate to you that the diminution of your pro-
ducing power must result in a corresponding de-
cline of your remuneration ? ” Witness: “ Naw ! ”
“ Did he ever give you to understand anything of
the kind?” “Naw!” “Will you kindly tell
the court exactly what he did say ? ” “He said
he would have to dock me dashed wages ’cause I
was gettin’ too dashed slow ; that’s what ’e said ! ”■
SUFFERING UNDER BASE SUSPICIONS.
Professor David, of Sydney University, in a recent
lecture, stated that Australians must needs be over-
dilute, and therefore driven to lean against posts, because
our average temperature is 13deg. higher than that of
England and Wales.
“Now, don'choo go makin' any mistake (hie).
I'm not drunk (hie), I'm over-dilute, I am
Nothin' common about me. Whoop!”
“ T.D.” : I was never much in the way of
meeting ecclesiastical personages in Australia, but
since I’ve been in England, just 12 months, I’ve
been pestered weekly with begging letters from
peripatetic bishops from out there. Apparently
their sole aim in life is to rush round the country and
collect a little boodle for “ the diocese.’’ Opened
yet another letter to-day, this time from the
Bishop of Perth, who is just now invading the
country seats of the British mighty, and collecting
guineas, and presumably having a good time. The
gaitered one wishes me to go somewhere and hear
him lecture, and mentions in large type that he is
raising a fund for the glorification of his diocese.
Needless to say I’m going, and taking the cash
along in a barrow. I came with just the one
object of helping Bishop Riley to float around
among duchesses and the like.
A PLEA FOR SKITE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the backblocks we are breeding- a race little better
than the blacks. . . . The city streets are filled with
stunted, weedy brats ... a race of degenerates.—
Liddell, M.H.R.
A mild, admonitory rhyme
I wish to make in homely phrase,
’Tis of our bright Australian clime
And too-benign Australian ways.
We are a people who have shown
Ourselves to be extremely fit,
Even if we run to skin and bone ;
We’ve got a country of our own,
Although we’ve messed it up a bit.
Then why don’t you
Australians true
Skite, lie, and fight for country as all other peoples do ?
We’ve heard about Australian blow
From fierce John Bulls, who ran with grease,
And heated arguments to show
Their country is God’s masterpiece ;
And meek Australians all agreed
That far-off land they do not ken
Was most magnificent indeed,
And that the men from there exceed
All other makes and kinds of men.
And they’re not slow
To visit woe
On any slanderous foreign cuss who says it is not so.
Australians, “ Awake ! Arise ! ”
Go take your little trumpets curled,
And blow a blast to shake the skies,
And wake an echo in the world.
Let’s raise a big, bold, brazen band,
With Austral sentiment agog,
To whoop ourselves and all our brand,
And skite about our native land
As now we skite about our dog—
To land ge-whiz
On any phiz
That hints this country ain’t immensely greater than
she is 1
The soul is base that will not lie
About his native land, and swear
That dim and bleary is the eye
That does not see all beauty there.
So crack ours up, recite with zest
The splendid story of her worth,
And bellow it to East and West
Of all the lands this is the best
At any mortal thing on earth.
The grand, the great,
The fruitful State
Is wooed with fine unreason, and mad ardor is her mate !
VIC.
EDWARD DYSON.
TRUE LOVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The trustees of the St. Kilda (Vic.) Cemetery are
inquiring for the whereabouts of H. Trueiove, their late
secretary. —News item.
When true love flies,
And Cupid comes to be
Just merely an objectionable kid child,
And all the scented paths of Arcady
Are broken asphalte, dust-and-germ-defiled,
The poets rise,
Throughout the ages with a tingling lyre,
To ask the right direction and inquire
Where true love flies.
Where Trueiove is,
The poet’s passioned plaint
Has been surpassed by infinite degrees
With some suggestions wise, and some that ain’t,
Set by St. Kilda burial trustees,
Agreeing his
Location this time will more likely be
Some fairer spot than their old cemet’ree
Where Trueiove is.
VIC.
C.D.

To be called a returned cohentmgenter is
now regarded by the Melbourne police as a deadly
insult. Panton, P.M., laid it down in Melbourne
City Court (Sept. 18): “I will not have the fact
that some members of the police force were
soldiers who had served in the South African
war mentioned in the Court.” When the faim-
burning hero first returned he was met by Minis-
ters of the Crown and carried off to get beef and
whisky. Later on he was brought back on a
fever-ship, and when he landed he was allowed
to walk home. Now, he takes a back street and
asks only for beer and obscurity, and he has to
pay for the beer. t m *
After all, the birth-mark which identifies
the long lost cheeild and exposes the changeling, is
not a mere melodramatic figment; it does exist in
real life sometimes. At the Sydney Water Police
Court, the other day, a young girl was charged
with destroying property. Her putative mother
gave evidence that, some years ago, she left her child
in custody in England. Eighteen months ago she
sent for the offspring, and received by return post
a girl, but not her girl—the birth-marks were dif-
ferent. However, she took the changeling some-
what to her bosom, but found her uncontrollable,
and now wished to be free of all responsibility for
her. The girl furiously claimed that the witness
was" her mother, but she had no birth-mark testi-
mony to ajlege in support (she had neglected to
observe her mother’s birth-marks), and she was
fined for her offence and left motherless.
The Randlords’ yarn that the imported
Chow slaves would increase white employment
proves true enough. The Transvaal Government
has just issued an ordinance allowing Euro-
peans £1 and exes, for every escaped Chow slave
returned to his owner. Thus is the brand-new
occupation of Chow-hunter started.
• * * *
Bent seems to have been pulling Mel-
bourne’s leg when he flashed out with an announce-
ment the other day that, since the religious folk
professed to be shocked at the running of Sunday
trains, he would stop them all—not only the
trains which carried people away from the City of
Dreadful Sunday to the hills and the seaside, but
also the trains which carried the holy in from the
suburbs to the fashionable city churches. Appalled
at the prospect of getting exactly what they had
asked for, the unco guid, who really only wanted
the country excursion trains stopped in the in-
terests of thripenny, rushed forward in protest.
Then Bent smiled all over his countenance and
partly over his stomach, and remarked briefly
that he had not meant what he said and didn’t
intend to stop any trains. Whereat there was
relief in the tents of the godly, who, on the whole,
would prefer that a certain number of thripennies
should be exported to the country than that the
great mass of thripennies should be stuck up in
the suburbs for want of means of locomotion to the
fashionable city churches.

Did they mean, then, that what they imparted on

lost during the week ? ”
“ G.H.” : Controversy is now raging
amongst the committee which is superintending
X erection of the Queen Victoria statue on Yarra
Bank Melbourne. One portion of the committee
wants to drag an expensive block of Italian marble
overseas from Carrara, and the other and more
patriotic section reckons that the marble obtained
It Caloola (N.S.W.)is good enough to memorialise
any King or Queen who ever drew his or her salary
with royal regularity. Had a talk, the other day,
with a man who is competent to sp ea k, and he
said that the Caloola stone is better than the Dago
article. It contains a larger percentage of car-
bonate of magnesia than the latter, and is, there-
fore, better suited to stand the weather, ihe
professor of geology at Melbourne University has
examined the product of the Caloola quarries, and
remarks that it contains small, hard pins°;, d ,,o'
mice. My authority, who has sculped both the
imported and the local variety of stone, says that
the dolomite pins simply mean that the monu-
ment purchaser gets a more durable obelisk for his
money. Carrara marble sells here at 12s. per
cubic foot; Caloola at Bs. The only solid objec-
tion to the Australian stone (which exists m prac-
tically unlimited quantities near Bathurst, IN.b.V\.)
seems to be that it hasn’t been dragged over 10,000
miles of moisture in a ship, and that the rats
haven’t crawled over it.

Brisbane Hospital got into the Philp-
Leahy habit of owing all round to such an extent
that the State Government had to take a hand,
and, after putting matters straight, brought in a
Bill to deal with hospitals. Whereupon an
“influential deputation ” sought to stay proceed-
ings, which Morgan promised to do if the deputa-
tionists paid up the £6OOO owing before October
Ist. This idea of paying up was looked upon as
simply preposterous, but as Kidston can say
“ No,” and mean it, the deputation is feeling m
its pocket, and hoping that something dreadful
will happen to a Government that intends to pay
its way and make others do the same.
That pleasing exhibition of laundry work
and the ironing competition attracted several
hundreds to Melbourne Exhibition Building for
three or four evenings. Chief interest centred in
the ironing by hand, in the presence of an inquisi-
tive public, of a white shirt and a lady s skirt, for
each of which events a score of entries were re-
ceived, including one from a man, Mr. Arthur
Guy—a police constable, no less. r l his bold
launder had also entered an ironed-at-home white
shirt for competition, but apparently the shirt had
been mauled by some drunk he was arresting;
anyhow, neither he nor his garment transpired.
The laundresses didn’t look a robust lot; the
gas or the steam appears to be unhealthy;
without exception they were white-faced, ansenuc,
weak-looking. Also, one or two had the starch-
chewing habit so badly that they couldn t refrain
in public. The rest were either free of it, or only
demolished starch at home.

A man is not considered
drunk in Queensland if he can say
“ Ipswich ” without the latter part
of the word becoming entangled in
his teeth.
« » *
“Hamer”: Curiosity, the
instinct that makes a man fire a
gun at himself to see if it is loaded,
involved me in conversation with
a long-haiied peculiarity in a liar.
He read palms, and drank what
drinks he could get. He grasped
my hand, and suggested that I
should give him a small coin, which
I promptly didn’t. Then he told
me that the initials W.S. came
to him. “William Smith” was
the only person they reminded me
of, I told him, at which he seemed
pained, and suggested a small coin.
He told me I had had a mother,
mentioned my age within half-a-
eioz 11 years of tlie truth, and told
me many similar facts as an illus-
tration of his occult power, and
again suggested a small coin. I
shouted drinks, and he warmed up
and told me I would be rich and
famous some day, and again sug-
gested a small coin. I promised to
give it to him when I was rich and
famous, but he didn’t seem to be
attracted by the prospect. He left
me for a while, and another man
informed me he had seen him in
Chow Africa, where he was coining
money as a palmist, which seems to
hint that the country that allows
the importation of Chinamen is
more gullible than this on.*. Tiien
he returned to me and hastened
the time of my good fortune, and
again suggested a small coin. So
many suggestions seemed worth
something, and I gave him a small
coin, and left him promising me
riotous good fortune in a few weeks
time.

The party of parliamentarians who left
Melbourne the other day to inspect the Waranga
waterworks took with it Mr. Malan, a South
African Government expert inquiring into Aus-
tralian industries. He was taken to see the huge
Goulburn weir, over whose brim 2,375,000 gallons
of water are now running to waste every minute ;
then on through the Shepparton, Rushworth, and
Murchison country, where irrigation channels are
almost as plentiful as gutters in a city ; then along
the main channel, as big as a river, from the Goul-
burn to Waranga Basin—the huge lake that,
taking in 1,000,000,000 gallons a day, will take 54
days to fill! Malan noted all. this, and, looking
round for the complement thereof, saw only a
casual farmhouse here and there, a few navvies’
huts, a hundred or two sheep, and a few score
cattle. Someone asked him his opinion of the
undertaking. “Very large and good and com-
plete, but—where are all the people for these
lands? The country seems to be a wilderness
And so it is—a howling wilderness of big land-
lords. „ ' .
* * * •
Some more reflections and reminiscences
about St. Peters-the unkempt Sydney suburb
which recently, aud undeservedly, failed to get
itself re-christened by the foolish name of
“Brompton”: —
St Peters is one of the oldest boroughs in Austral):,,
and its church and cemetery are historic. The church
(St. Peter’s) was built 70 years ago on ground given by
Robert Campbell, the first minister being Thomas Steele,
LL.D. Acting for Dr. Steele for a time was the Reverend
Thomas Makinson, who, ill the late forties, ’verted to
Rome, his place as locum tenens being taken by Rev.
James liassall, who died last year near Bnsbane.
Another in the neighborhood was Rev. Charles C. Kemp,
who married the widow of William Toogood. Toogood
kept the Golden Fleece at the N.E. corner of George and
King streets, in the fifties. Mr. Kemp induced his wife to
refuse to let the house any longer as a pub., and
when Mr. Edward Samuels’ lease was up, the City Bank
started business on the corner. The old cemetery,
adjoining St. Peter’s Church, holds the remains of many
notable colonists-the first wife of Solicitor-General
Manning, James Raymond (Postmaster-General) Judge
Josephson, “Smith of Mulgunma,” Thomas Icely, M.D.0.,
Jolui Bibb (an early architect), Robert Haviland Way,
and Mrs. Wise (widow of tbe late Justice Wise,
and mother of B. R. Wise, K.C.) On the Cooks
River road, in the early days, lived many mem-
bers of the colonial aristocracyA. B. Spark,
Leslie Duguid (first manager of the Commercial Bank),
Dr. Tucker, the Talbots, Kerri son James, Unwin (an old-
time solicitor), and others. When the Government
undertook to convert .the picturesque lagoon lying
between Cook’s River road and Botany Bay into Sheas
Creek, and made it a dumping-ground for city debris, the
“ old families ” gave up the locality as a bad job.
Acting on the advice of Grown Prosecutor
Finlayson, the Bent Cabinet last week decided
that, as no criminal proceedings could possibly
succeed against the awful Victorian butter agents,
those typical “commercial men should still be
allowed to pervade Collins-street as of yore m t
tall hat of respectability and the halo of the
martyr. No wonder the Victorian Chamber of
Commerce protested so strongly against Attorney-
General Isaacs’ Secret Commissions Bill. Under
a sound and honestly-administered scheme like
Isaacs’ Bill, or like that of Lord Russell in
England, there would have been no possibility of
a nolle prosequi being entered against certain butter
agents—the only alternative to acquittal would
have been £5OO fine or two years hard labor, or
both. „ ,
George Fairbairn, Vic. M.L.A., on the
American “expert” :
When he was connected with the meat-trade in Queens-
land the company he was identified with was very anxious
to devefop the canned meat trade. They therefore sent
to America for an instructor, and secured °"* ak
year The report which the manager sent soon after his
arrival was that he was a very nice young man, and
exceedingly anxious to learn the canned meat industry
Others could tell a tale of sending across the world
for “experts” who turned out to be poorly
equipped, and afterwards finding that they had
thoroughly capable men at home. Ihe ‘ strenuous
1 fe” led by a good many Yanks seems to consist
mainly of strenuous boosting of their own ability,
with very little capacity to back their brag.
• * *
Madame Maggie Stirling was officially
welcomed by the gaud Mayor of Melbourne with
much ostentation and showers of melted butter in
the shape of eulogistic platitudes, bhe was also
received by the Mayor and citizens of Camberwell,
her native suburb, with ostentation and more
melted butter. The lady bore it all with becoming
meekness, although the difference between the
attitude of mayors now and the obliviousness ot
mayors when she was only an Australian singer
without the English hall mark could hardly have
escaped her recollection. Mayors were not officially
aware of her existence when she figured in public
merely on the strength of her abilities as an artist,
but it is painfully like mayors to endeavor to add
to tlieir importance by greatly patronising the
singer who has won European recognition.

Sleeping and waking, my tenderest care ;
God grant your boyhood he trusting and dutiful !
Learn to confide ere you learn to beware.
Lullaby, lullaby, lullaby-low;
Trials avid troubles before you ;
Mother has suffered and, mother
should know
('Tears of repentance must bitterly
flow),
But surely our God has some
mercy to show
To the son of the sinner who
bore you.

You will get guidance, my
nursling of woe,
Through the prayers of the
sinner who bore you.

will smilingly
Flatter my boy in their
dangerous way;
Teasing, and coaxing, and
gazing beguil-
ingly —
Hiding the feel-
ings they
feign to
betray.
Lullaby, lullaby, Mother’s afraid—
Trials and troubles before you.
Love is a wanton in beauty arrayed;
Old as the hills arc the tricks she has played ;
But, darling, play fair with a soft-hearted maid,
For the sake of the Mother tvho bore you.
Lullaby, lullaby, love is a jade;
Never give woman just cause to upbraid
The dear son of the Mother who bore you.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Queensland has £2,392,701 with which to wean
itself away from the habit of leaning up against the pawn-
shop ; that was the amount of the “cash balance” to
credit of'Loan Fund on June 30. But £1,551,742 of it is
in the Q.N. Bank, and only oozes out at the rate of about
£86,000 per half-year. It is to be hoped that long before
the eight years are over (it will take eight vears for it all
to ooze out) Queensland will be quite cured, and will put
the final payments into the Debt Reduction Fund. So
far that fund doesn’t take up much room in the public
accounts. In Philp’s days, between 1900 and 1904, there
wasn’t a single entry, except a debit—“ Loss on sale of
Government Savings Bank stock, £10.” Philp actually
had the gall to charge that to a fund created expressly
for the reduction of the public debt.
* * *' *
Some Westralian politicians can hardly hold
themselves in when they mention their sinking fund for
the redemption of the State’s debt; it already amounts,
they point out, to about 5 per cent, of the total debt.
But it turns out that two-thirds of the whole lot has been
lent over again to the State. In other words, Westralia
has virtuously put £900,000 or so in the till and said it
would pay its creditors with the money ; and then it has
sneaked back to the till, and taken the money, and built
things that don’t pay with it, and comforted the other-
wise empty till with its own I O U.
* * * *
In the past 12 months Tasmanian railways didn’t
earn quite half of what they cost the country in interest.
Cohen’s bill was £147,019 ; and after paying' working
expenses there was £73,300 left to meet it with. But
Treasurer Stewart scorns the idea that this meant a loss
of £73,719. “ Consider,” he said, “the immense benefits
to the users of the railways from the cheapened cost of
transit.” If a pickpocket stole Treas. Stewart’s watch, it
wouldn’t be enough for somebody to say to him—“ Con-
sider the immense benefits to the thief through getting
a watch for nothing.” “ Yes,”Stewart would say, “that’s
all very well for the thief; but what about me?” And these
“ immense benefits from the cheapened cost of transit”
are all very well for the people who get them without pay-
ment ; but what about the State that can’t give the
immense benefits without payment ?
* * * *
While a few Australasian fire insurance co.’s
have made splendid progress in the last 15 years, others
have remained almost stagnant. Take this list, showing-
net premium incomes of a few of them at various periods :
1890. 1900. 1904-5.
New Zealand .. ..£269,893 £315,284 £420,311
South British .. .. 246,487 215,729 284,402
Colonial Mutual .. .. 88,593 95,253 125,161
Mercantile Mutual .. 27,923 37,235 50,534
Australian Mutual .. 27,870 33,888 36’,088
North Queensland _ .. 30,722 75,310 72,230
Victoria 73,413 82,217 82,276
Victoria General .. .. 10,874 11,324 9,443
Australian Alliance .. 41,779 48,574 54,659
Some of the variations are due to changes of policy. Thus
the South British once did a large foreign business which
it couldn’t watch, and the result was just about as
disastrous as might be expected. It got its experience,
paid for it, and drew out; hence the decline in business
between 1890 and 1900. The North Queensland got on to
the same alluring track, and found a loss at the end of it ;
then it drew back—and fortunately in its case it didn’t
have quite so far to draw. But, apart from these special
cases, this one fact stands out above all others : the
offices that have suffered most from dry-rot and stagna-
tion are those that have had the high-dividend disease
worst. The Colonial Mutual, which has made extremely
rapid progress, laid the foundations for that by going
absolutely without dividends for some vears. In 1894, it
had no reserves at all, and before that it had a
huge deficit. The next four years it made £34,404
profit and paid only £4125 in dividends. At the
last balance its shareholders’ funds amounted to
£171,127, including the paid capital of £37,500. The
New Zealand Co. has in six years put aside £196,152,
while its dividends have been £162,500; and as a result
of thus strengthening its reserves its business is simply
leaping ahead, and taking, profits along with it: The
Mercantile Mutual, though it is doing less in the way of
strengthening its position than some others, has neverthe-
less added 33J per cent, to reserves in 10 years ; and the
public’s business has gone that co.’s way to reward it. On
the other hand, take the last three co.’s on the list. The
Victoria 10 years ago paid a dividend of 401 per cent.,
which mopped up £20,221 of the year’s totafprofits of
£'20,741. Following year it made £17,058, and paid away
in dividends £17,974. Year after that (1897) it made
£lB,OlO and paid £17,974. A saner policy has been adopted
lately, though two or three years ago divs. were again
rushed up to 36 per cent.; but the co. has to pay now for
its old follies, and its premium income remains almost
stationary.- The business is going to offices that showed
that shareholders weren’t their only consideration—that
they gave a thought to policy-holders as well. The
Victoria General is. an even worse sinner ; to pay its 15,
20, and 25 per cent. divs. it has actually had to reduce its
reserves by £5500 in 10 years ; and as it never had large
reserves, people who want to buy insurance take the hint
and buy it from somebody else. The Australian Alliance
isn’t the worst of the bad examples ; but it is pretty bad.
It had opportunities which hardly another office in Aus-
tralia had, and it threw them away simply because it
greedily devoured every cent of under-writing profit, and
left the future with all its possibilities in the way of some
vast conflagration, to take care of itself. And the public
isn’t sure that that is the sort of fire insurance co. it
is looking for. The figures prove up to the hilt that the
worst use fire insurance co.’s can make of profits is to pay
large dividends with them ; in no line does the scoffing of
profits as soon as they show up pay worse—in none does
the reserving of a of them pay better.
The N.S.W. Miners’ Accident Relief Fund,
which began operations in 1901, and is the nearest
Australia yet gets to a system of national insurance
Carried
Ex- to Re- Re-
Dec., Income. Claims, penses. serves. serves
1001 .. £31,906 £6,821 £lOl £24,984 £24,984
1902 .. 39,036 11,096 1546 26,394 51,378
1903 .. 45,559 14,520 1050 29,989 81,367
1904 .. 43,288 17,587 1146 29,555 110,922
Every person working at a mine which employs 15 or
more men is required to contribute ; the Act requires the
employer to deduct 4.jd. per week from the workman’s
wages, and the State and the employers are required to
pay into the fund, another 4Jd. between them in equal
amounts. The claims are made up of weekly payments
(125.) in cases of disablement, a pension of Bs. per week to
widow or other dependent of a miner killed by accident,
2s. 6d. per week to each child, and a grant of £l2 to bury
the miner when he is killed. The expense rate, though ''’
much higher than it used to be, is still lov-, auiounting to
only £2 7s. sd, per cent, of total income. But In'oom-
paring this with the 8 or 12 per cent, absorbed by the
ordinary insurance office, it has to be remembered that
no medical examination expenses have to be paid, and no
canvassing done for business, while the employers do the
whole of the collecting-. The local committees (which see
to the distribution of the relief) get a small allow-
ance; but the central board" of management,
consisting of representatives of mineowners and
miners, takes no fees, as a rule. In all, only £43 has
from first to last been swallowed by this board. The
fund, moreover, receives office, clerical, and actuarial
accommodation free of charge. Granted that it is a most
excellent institution, there is no reason why, in its rela-
tions with the State, it should not be put upon a purely
business footing. These leakages, through indirect and
hidden subsidies, run away with tens of thousands of
pounds a year. The State should charge for any services
it renders this and every other branch. Then, if the
State thinks it can afford it, the direct subsidy-could be
increased. The public would then know where it was.
As the result of constant clamor, the Govern-
ment lately instructed its actuary to make the first quin-
quennial investigation of the fund. The object was to get
higher benefits. The Act said that an investigation
should be made every five years ; but this one was rushed
on within a little over four years. The report recom-
mended that the benefits should be increased 25 pc: cent,
in most cases, 20 per cent, in others. The actuary valued
the liabilities under the old rates at £85,341, and under
the new rates at £106,048 ; the latter would give a surplus
of £14,060 as at 30th April. Valuations of liabilities
under accident policies are, however, extremely unreli-
able (on account of the want of data), and the reduction of
the surplus to this small amount looks very risky.
Note, as to that, the steady growth in the total amount
of claims. Consider also the growth in other respects :

Yet the actuary argues that the tendency is for accidents
to become less. It may be ; but the tendency of men to
go on the fund, instead of risking their jobs by working
when unfit, is obviously greater. And again, as to relief

The increase in the benefits has, nevertheless, been agreed
upon. Well, the only thing to hope for is that, after a
few years, a lot of disappointment won’t have to be
caused by hauling the benefits down again.

But this takes no account of the judgment for £113,082
given against the co., this judgment having been
appealed against, nor of the costs of the proceedings. The
smaller profits as compared with 1904 result from heavier
fire losses. These absorbed 54 5 per cent, of net pre-
miums, as against 39'1 per cent, in 1904, and 45 per cent,
in 1903. The marine loss rate was 68'5 per cent, of net
premiums. The expense rate in the fire department was !
37 per cent, of net premiums; in the marine, 23'3 per
cent. Thus, in spite of the unusually heavy fire-loss rate,
8-5 per cent, of net premiums in that branch were profit;
while, notwithstanding a favorable marine experience,
only 8.2 per cent, of those premiums were profit. In 1904
the surpluses were—fire, 26 per cent. ; marine 7"7 per
cent. Though there are stray years in which marine
business gives thumping profits (in 1900 this co. netted
40-2 per cent, of its marine premiums), the experience
generally is unsatisfactory. Anyway, there is not the
money in it that there is in fire insurance.

* * * *
They are trying hard in Melbourne to patch up
the Australian Alliance Assurance Co.’s troubles. Chief
Justice Madden’s verdict against the co. (brought on
behalf of policy-holders in the old life department) was
for £113,082 and the costs of one of the most expensive
cases ever heard in Victoria ; co.’s capital and reserves
amount to £224,657. The policy-holders are mostly old,
and not particularly enthusiastic, and it has taken a lot of
hustling to carry the struggle this far ; so both sides look
kindly on proposals for a compromise. The matter comes
up at a special meeting of the co. on Oct. 12.

The bonus is 6d. in the £ on purchases ; and that and the

preliminary expenses has to be reduced. The position is
dha-t after two-years’ trading (and it is assumed that it has

holders some of the money spent in floating the concern.
Meantime, the society is drifting deeply into debt.
Eighteen months ago it owed the public, over and above
its resources in cash, “sundry debtors,” and similar
assets, £9159 ; six months ago that total had reached
£12,717 ; now-it is up to £14,380. Certainly all this is
supposed 'to be represented by stock, vacant land, pre-
mises, fittings, &c. ; but any depreciation in the value of
those things will fall wholly- on shareholders. It is too
rapid a rush into debt. The society was formed, on a
co-operative basis, to make the members their own mas-
ters and put them outside the money-power; and it forth-
with throws itself into the arms of the money-power, and
pledges itself to support the money-lender. There, is
a more satisfactory side to the business. The turnover
continues to grow. Receipts have been £42,866, against
£36,364 for the corresponding half of 1904—remarkable
figures for an infant. But if members won’t buy unless
they get their bonus, if they won’t help to build up the
society by leaving a bonus in abeyance for a while, then
the society would probably be better without them.
Then it wouldn’t be so much inclined to rush out and buy
a motor car—it would put up with a wheelbarrow for a
year or two.

The Tasmanian Farmers’ Co-operative Asso-
ciation, Ltd. (Burnie, Tas.) :

Premiums on new shares, £8 .. £8
1904 1096 699 129 259 17
Premiums on new shares, £6O .. 77
1905 1
approx} l249 785 134 230 177
This is run on much the same lines as the Adelaide Co-
operative Society, one of the oldest in Australasia, except
that the latter allows withdrawals of capital at any time,
and the surplus profits are left on deposit, carry-
ing 4 per cent., while in the Tasmanian society
the surplus is represented by bonus shares, which
hitherto have carried 6 per cent. No attempt is made
to build up a formal reserve fund. What appears above
as a reserve'fund is a suspense item, but as it consists
largely of premiums, and is, therefore, to that extent a
reserve, it has been assumed that it is wholly a reserve.
If it is a reserve it should not be called a suspense account,
and if part of it is one sort of thing and part of it another,
the two shouldn’t be lumped in this way. Apparently, it
is called a suspense account because it is thought that a
true co-operative concern shouldn’t have any reserves;
there is a lurking suspicion to that effect in the Adelaide
concern and some others as well. Why it should be so
passes comprehension. A reserve fund is just as neces-
sary to guard against disaster, and therefore just as
necessary in the interests of present shareholders,
as an audit is, or a lock on the door, or
a coat of paint on the premises. Most co-opera-
tive societies know they can’t get along without
a reserve, and the struggle to invent some other
name for it is half humorous and half pitiable. And
if this particular society doesn’t look out, in spite of
its very fine progress the struggle to pay 6 per cent, on a
capital which is being continually watered will land it in
trouble. True, the increases of capital are represented
by balances of profit, but that profit may be in the shape
of “ sundry debtors,” and earning nothing. In four
years the overdraft, etc. (now £1900) has been more than
doubled, and that doesn’t look healthy, for one thing.
