The co. originally paid £3500 for the goodwill of a small
medical benefit society, and £lOOO of this still remains in
its balance-sheet as an asset; this being purely a paper
item it is disregarded in the foregoing table. The actual
additions to assurance fund are less than they were in the
previous two years :
Additions to Funds.
1897 £849 1900 £1643 1903 £1494
1898 851 1901 805 1904 1463
1899 1173 1902 1010 1905 1408
But the bulk of the business is under medical benefit and
similar tables, and it is probable that some at least of
these risks require no large reserves. In the valuation
made at the end of 1904 no liability was reckoned as
accruing under these tables ; and, though any office doing
this sort of business is liable to be struck with an abnor-
mal number of claims in any year, the fact that claims
in the aggregate remain steady gives some support to
the practice of the office. Compared with the size of the
claims, the size of the reserves is therefore not so absurd
as it looks. The unsatisfactory part of the business is
that the funds accumulated in connection with the
regular life assurance, as to which reliable valuations of
liability can be made, are lumped with the funds in
connection with a vast number of fancy tables, as to
which no reliable valuation of liabilities can be made.
Also, this lumping of things makes it impossible to tell
what the ordinary insurer is paying for his insurance.
The lumped expenses have been
Expenses in Proportion to Total Income.
1898 .. £39 16 3 per cent. 1902 .. £52 0 5 per cent.
1899.. 39 13 5 „ 1903.. 52 17 5
1900.. 41 0 8 „ 1904.. 49 16 6
1901.. 48 8 3 ~ 1905.. 50 14 9 ~
This is a ridiculous rate to pay for any sort of insurance—
even the sick-benefit sort. The ordinary benefit society,
which isn’t generally reckoned a model of business organ-
isation, does very much the same sort of business as what
is probably the most expensive class of this company’s
business, and does it for 20 to 25 per cent, of total
income. The 1903 report of the Oddfellows (Independent
Order, N.S.W.) showed a total expense rate of a shade over
21 per cent., which was said- to be above the average.
Either, then, this co. is only doing this friendly-society
class of business at an enormously greater cost than
mutual friendly societies do it for, or the regular insurance
business is costing enormously more than the mutual
office can do it for. Probably the cost is absurdly great
in both cases—which is another poor advt. for private
enterprise as compared with co-operation, either through
the State or through mutual societies.

The insurance account has to cover £432,536 worth of
steamers, lighters, plant, &c. Excellent speed has been
made in building up this reserve. An unknown amount
is written off for depreciation ; the repairs and renewals
reserve is therefore something more than a book-keeping
entry against depreciation which has actually occurred,
though to what extent it is better than that it is impos-
sible to say. Twelve months ago floating assets repre-
sented £503,288. They are now down t0’£432,537, a re-
duction of £70,751; there have evidently, then, been
heavy writings-off: during the year, in addition ’to the
£20,000 or so added to repairs and renewal fund. The
amounts evidently-written off, as well as the additions to
formal reserves, are represented by large increases in
“ freehold properties, wharves,, and shares in other co.’s ”
(£104,103 to £189,655) and “sundry debtors, agents
balances, bills receivable, &c.” (£52,249 to £118"895).
Allowing that the co. is in a fairly hazardous business,'
and that wear and tear is tolerably heavy, still the
impression these accounts leave is that it is doing- remark-
ably well, and that if it wanted to it could easily issue a
much more alluring balance-sheet.

That 14 p.c. dividend looked a fixture (it had been paid
regularly since 1894), but electricity has made a difference.
Ten months ago the co. brought down its price for gas to
6s. lOd. per 1000 ft. ; now it has reduced the price of gas
for power to ss. per 1000 ft. The co. has been writing
off for wear and tear an average of about £lBOO per
annum, equal to about 2 per cent, on book-value ; more-
over, apart from funds invested in the undertaking, it
has £15,565 on deposit. The reserves are therefore a
good deal more real than those of most gas co. ’s. And.
even at the reduced rate of profit, they are still earning
good money; on shareholders’ total funds (capital and
reserves) the past year’s profits work out at just about
7 per cent. Reduction from 14 to 12 per cent, is no
doubt very sad ; but the co. should take heart—there
are quite a lot of people whose money isn’t earning them
7 per cent.

The co.’s capital (£25,000) is a small thing compared with

was put aside to reduce this huge load, but, in spite of
the yarn that legislation is driving capital out of the
country, that particular Oohen declined to accept the
proffered money. So far from waiting for a chance to get
out, he pointed to the bond and insisted upon staying.
The co. therefore has to go on paying interest on the old
£125,000, though it has £7500 out on deposit, besides
(previous to paying the dividend) £3661 on current
account. The trade revenue for the year was £56,680 as
against £62,423 the previous year, which explains the
decline in net profits, notwithstanding that only £1604
was spent in repairs and renewals as against £2148 in
1904. The shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves)
earned £9 14s. per cent. ; that is to say, rather less than
the earnings of the reserves were put to reserve.

This co. doesn’t tread the ordinary paths of profit. Its
business is mainly to keep up the price of beef and mut-
ton ; it gives more than a beast is worth, for the encourage-
ment of the others, and grateful pastoralists subsidise it
for its services. Their own profit comes in the higher
prices that the other buyers are obliged to give ; and as
this co. is practically the pastoralists themselves, they
can afford to go without direct dividends from it. It all
seems somehow an interference with the “ natural law of
supply and demand,” which pastoralists are sometimes
great sticklers for. But, with large resources, it does
some good work for Australia by sending out only high-
class potted goods, &c., and spending money in impressing
on the foreign buyer that Australian goods are good
goods.

presented by stock (and to some extent sundry debtors),
a very great deal has to be taken on trust.

The new electric light installation of the Mel-
bourne Exhibition Cycle Track, formed a great attraction
of the recent “Austral” meeting. The main feature of
the system is the “Ganz” brilliant arc lamp, forty of
which are distributed round the track, suspended on iron
brackets. The light is absolutely steady, soft and
diffused, doing away entirely with the injurious glare
common with other lamps and overcoming the objection-
able shadows in and under the globe. Special globes are
used which, while diffusing the light, do not cut off an
undue quantity and the effect is a light known
scientifically to be the best for all illuminating purposes.
The candle power is more than four times that furnished
by other lamps at the same current consumption. Electric
lighting by the “Ganz” arc lamp is cheaper than any
other form of illuminant, both in first cost, in fixing and
in maintenance. The contractors for this installation
were the Austral Otis Engineering Co., Ltd., corner
Hannah and Kavanagh streets, South Melbourne, Sole
Agents for Ganz and Co., Budapest, Hungary, who will
gladly furnish estimates and expert advice upon similar
or any electrical schemes.*

The Bankers’ Magazine (London) September,
1904:—“ The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
Limited was founded thirty-one years ago, with head-
quarters in the capital of Victoria, and the amount of its
business and of its accumulated funds far exceed those of
any life office established in England since the passing of
the Life Assurance Companies Act of 1870. The rapidity
in the development of this Australian society attests the
energy and organising powers of its administrators.”*
The Wallaroo and Moonta Copper Mines,
South Australia, are making extensive additions to their
concentrating plant. The management are familiar with
all forms of concentrators on the market and no doubt
gave very careful consideration to the various types before
they finally decided upon the Wilfiey Concentrator. They
have placed an order for 20 Wilfiey Tables with the
Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne, sole
manufacturers for Australasia.*

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
' mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discriDtion of cast or wrought iron work.*

+ » * *
“ Janor” : General belief among miners is that
wolfram deposits occur only in granite country ; yet there
is a creek in Gippsland (Vic.) in which waterworn wolfram
is obtained, in association with alluvial gold. Watershed
is entirely composed of sedimentary rocks (silurian).
Creek drifts show no sign of granite, as they probably
would had some pre-existent granite belt been entirely
denuded on the catchment area. Looks as if the mineral
will yet be found in other than granite country.

If official reports are true, East Fingall, Day
Dawn (W. A.), has a possibility of rivalling its big neigh-
bor. Main shaft cut a reef at 413 ft., and followed it
down to 427 ft. This reef is 4ft. wide, of which 3ft. assays
over 3oz. per ton. Some of the people controlling the
show are also in the Murchison Associated, and might
be more reliable. All sorts of gaudy reports have been
issued about both mines on different occasions, but subse-
quent events have not confirmed them. Co. is in 400,000
shares of ss. each, of which 158,415 only are issued, paid
up to 4s. 6d. Concern has such a big bunch of shares in
reserve, it could easily be put in a strong financial posi-
tion if developments warrant. Co. holds 78 acres, so has
lots of ground if the reef is any good.

Hannan’s Star, Kalgoorlie, is, as remarked
before, one of the “strongest” reconstructions perpe-
trated for a long time. Holders of old scrip have only till
October 3 to apply for new scrip, and have to pay up an
assessment of 4s. a share. Of this amount Is. is payable
on application, Is. on allotment, and the balance in calls
of Is. at intervals of three months. From Jan. to
June the co. worked at a loss of £l2OO, which looks
bad enough ; but, during July, loss was £4OO, and during
August it was £5OO, which is worse. Ore has dropped
back to about sdwt., which is quite unpayable. Concern
looks a most hopeless proposition at present, and its past
history is hardly reassuring. Some five years ago a
highly payable lode was reported as having been cut by a
diamond drill bore, but all efforts to locate it have since
proved fruitless. Query is, where did the core come from ?

South Kalgurli (W.A.) is highly promising at
present. It is equipped with two No. 8 Krupp mills and
a No. sas a stand-by. The two No. B’s have been treating
100 tons a day each, and during August handled 6200 tons
for a net profit, barring London exes., of £6OOO. Manager
Morgan reckons that this rate can be maintained, as co.
has two years’ reserves in sight, and the No. 5 mill can be
utilised during any stoppage to maintain output. Ex-
traction is good (93 '6 per cent.), and total costs come out
at 265. lOd. per ton. As co. is in only 200,000 shares,
August rate of profit will allow of divs. of 25 per cent.

• * * *
Norseman (W.A.) is suffering from the peri-
odical water famine. Princess Royal has been obliged to
sack 30 men and Cumberland a less number, because the
dam has given out, and there is not enough fresh water
for boilers. The rainfall is quite sufficient had dams been
sunk to catch it, and through neglect workers and share-
holders have to suffer serious loss. Princess Royal is dis-
carding No. 1 battery, an old ramshackle affair, for a new
plant, and No. 2 will be remodelled. A second rock-
breaker is also being installed. This will increase output
and cheapen costs, which hitherto have panned out at the
ridiculous figure of 555. a ton.
* * # *
Cumberland, Norseman (W.A.), doesn’t seem
to have improved since manager Fernald displaced mana-
ger Burkett. The fact is, reef is far too small and patchy
for a big co., and mine is only suitable for working
parties. Tom Hewitson’s friends, who bought in last
year at and over 10s. per share, must be having an anxious
time.
* * * *
That matter of the rush of mining-law experts
and others to the place where silver chlorides were found
near Orange (N.S.W.) seems to have been quietly dropped.
The story is that the stuff was sent down to the Mines
Department with the request that the prospectors might
be told what it was made of; and that, before word
reached the prospectors, “fly’’people in Sydney either
knew or got a suspicion that the stuff was rich, and rushed
the neighborhood. Did the Department leak ? Presumably
Minister Moore will probe the circumstances. The pro-
spector gets enough discouragement without having to
hump the suspicion that the nearer you stick to the Mines
offices the closer you are to the reef.
* * * *
Two hundred thousand tons of B. H. Block 14
“tailings” containing much zinc have gone the way of
the rest. The crowd that has laid its paw on all these
Barrier dumps evidently has the control of the spelter
market in its eye—or it may be the same Continental
crowd that has had the biggest say in that market for
years. The money (£5OOO deposit has been paid) will
come in very handy to Block 14. The way that co. has
been able to pick itself up out of the dirt, and get new
equipment, without going to the shareholders’ pockets, is
about the most striking thing in recent Barrier company
management. What it was and what it is about repre-
sents the difference between Adelaide and Melbourne
control.
• * • •
Judging by the daily-paper reports, all the
trouble at the Sulphide Corporation, Broken Hill, is over
—as, when the creep occurred, they said it would be by
the beginning of September. Well, for the last four
weeks before the smash the mill averaged 5250 tons of
crudes per week ; present run is barely 2000 tons. And
this is after nine weeks. Estimate made here, that it
would be well towards the end of the year before things
become normal, looks like being realised.
» * * »
British (Broken Hill) mine is rushing things.
To work the almost-untouched northern end an arrange-
ment has been made with the Junction for the use of
MTntyre’s shaft. There is ore in this block almost from
the surface down, including a good deal of oxidised ; but it
is very poor in silver. British has, next to the B.H.
Prop., the largest area of lode country ; but its bodies are
irregular, and much of its sulphides are heavy in rhodon-
ite, the stuff that causes all the trouble at the neighboring
Junction North. Also it owns some of the hottest work-
ings at Broken Hill, and that doesn’t help to keep costs
low. It has to carry 300,000 shares (including 60,000
privileged), and has never been able to be anything but a
very irregular div.-payer, and, in spite of a rather more
cheerful outlook, is still not likely to sail into a front
place and stay there.

“M.C.” : Be the suggestion (8., Sept. 12) that
an export duty might encouiWge Australia to use up its
own dirt instead of exporting it for the benefit of
Germany and Belgium. The' people who blow so en-

True Blue, Wyalong (N.S.W.), from a windlass
beginning', is now about to include in its appointments
rock drills and electricity for lighting purposes. All
brought about by the judicious handling of the wealth
within its four corners. As expected, Naylor’s application
for the Mousetrap lease has been granted, and the lesson
for some people to learn is to keep a good thing when
they have got it by observing labor covenants. Latest
Wyalong crushings of note —Union, 19 tons for 690 z;
Phillips and party, 80 tons for S6oz.
The wail of Chairman Waldron, of the Hercules,
Mt. Read (Tas.), because the miners would not submit to
the wage-reduction the co. wanted to force on them, is
full of holes. The Board wanted the men to submit to
lose £llOO a year as part of “ a scheme of economy pro-
posed to be introduced with a view to endeavoring to
bring the co. into a regular dividend-paying concern.”
Result of refusal was that mine has been shut down for
over six months. At the ver3’ time the Board wanted
this reduction the co. had profits accrued or accruing
amounting to £13,000; it had the prospect of the best
metal market ahead that has ruled for nine years ; and it
had the chance of striking ore in the No. 6 tunnel. To
close the mine has meant pretty well £5OO in depre-
ciation ; but that is nothing when compared with the loss
of prestige to the co. from being badly beaten by the men,
and the absolute cash drift through missing the high
prices for metals. Not content with drivelling about the
check to the inflow of capital caused by “ the power of
the labor organisations and agitators,” the Board sneers
at the outlook for Broken Hill co.s’ disposing of their zinc
residues ; and, as quite 1,500,000 tons of tailings have just
been sold straight out by Barrier co.’s, that gibe is as
foolish as the others.
* * * *
Tasmanians like waiting, and are languidly
watching developments on Gladstone dredge, success or
otherwise of which will have much influence on Tas. tin
ventures generally. There is no reason why latter should
be so ; for if the show paps out well it will not make
others do likewise. As to mine itself, there are only bor-
ing tests to depend upon. Taking these as correct, the
future of mine rests upon the plant itself, which is now in
working order and treating estimated quantities of drift.
There is nothing new in main portion of works; the
special features are the tin-saving appliances and the
boxes themselves, which are used elsewhere, but only for
gold. Trommels seem to work well, classification being
apparently perfect, but several alterations to boxes
will have to be made. Though general opinion is
otherwise, there should be no difficulty in remov-
ing buried timber, as the purchase on the “ lad-
der,” round which the buckets work, will lift
20 tons or more, exclusive of its own weight
(30 tons). This, with an additional lift of 10 tons
with the crane, should remove most trees after drifts
have been shifted by the buckets working under and over
them. The paddock in which dredge was built is worked
out; stated that 60 tons of tin and 150 oz. of gold came
from it (one acre). Under old methods this took 21 months
to work ; present plant should shift same quantity in
eight weeks. Dredge-master Ryan, a practical man, says
it will take about three weeks or less to work out into
paying drifts. Writer has to take office statements as to
vaiue of mine, but believes, as far as dredge is concerned,
it will be a success.
* * * •
The first of the Melbourne-owned Tasmanian
tin mines to come on the dividend list was the New Ruby
Flat. Now follows the New Brothers Home No. 1. This,
though an independent co., has let its mine on a
tribute agreement to the Briseis Co., by which that
concern works it and hands over 48 per cent, of the
value of the output of tin won from drift. Salvation
came with this agreement,.and there seems to be every
chance of divs. flowing steadily, as the worst of the over-
burden—for the removal of which the co. pays—is off,
leaving the drift to cost the co. nothing. Mine has been
at work for years, but since the time when it was Laun-
ceston-owned, when the tin drift was mined by drives, it
has never paid. The problem is how much tin was
excavated then and what the dirt left will average.
Montgomery, ex-Tas. Govt. Geologist, estimated about
101 b. to the cubic yard, and J. B. Mackenzie about 2j-lb.
So far, enough work has not been done to prove which
expert is right. To put it at 51b. might seem a safe esti-
mate, seeing that the Briseis dirt pans out about that.
Doubt about the claim arose from the cold shoulder
turned on the property months ago by the Argus mining
man. Results show that he would have been better
advised if he had been more generous in this direction,
and had used gall when dealing with certain other tin
shows, which deserve slating as much to-day as they did
then.
• * * •
Under the new order, each department of Chilla-
goe 00. (N.Q.) will be worked by its own head. With
Back in charge of the railway, which is by far the most
profitable part of the business—the only profitable part,
in fact —and Greenway as general manager, the pot was
often at the boil. Now Paul Kunze will take up the
metallurgical work, Brander the mining, and Back will
have all the glory of the railway profits. As to those
latter, some people profess to begin to smell rats. Their
idea is that the railway is going to be boomed, and the
profits bloated, until some day soon the State will be
asked why it doesn’t take over such an extremely payable
undertaking.
* * « *
J. Horsburg, lately appointed manager of Mt.
Molloy, one of the two Queensland copper shows that
look like making money for their owners, is a young
Queenslander. First he got into the Aldershot works
(Q.), under Wynberg; a three-years course at Sydney
Engineering School followed; lately he has been under
Greenway, at Chillagoe. Greenway is a good metallur-
gist to be under, provided you don’t catch his craving for
piling up machinery. He, more than any other man, is
responsible for the present system of sulphide treatment
at Broken Hill. It is something of a coincidence that he
and John Warren, the first men to run sulphide mills and
show there was money in them, are each now without a
mine.

Present traction-engine experiment for haulage
of matte, ore, &e., in N.Q., isn’t turning out any better
than its predecessors ; G. H. Blakemore, years ago, tried
it at Einasleigh, and struck just about as much trouble
as he had had with the teams. Next invasion of N.Q.
will be by mules.

On Mills’ United’s improvement, chances of
East Murchison, New Brill. Freehold, and School Reserve
(Charters Towers) brightened. But the local market dis-
counted the improvement up to the hilt. East Mexican
paids were 2s. in Feb. ; now 11s. 9d. New Brilliant Free-
holds have quadrupled, and School Reserves trebled in
six months. There isn’t a mining Exchange in Australia
that picks its bones cleaner than the Towers’ crowd.

Victoria Quartz, Bendigo, chasing the New
Chum Railway downwards in the search for gold, has got
on to the metal at 4105 ft. New Chum Railway got .its
gold at 4224 ft.; but the Victoria is only on the top
of the formation yet, so it will not be far short of
the Railway in the depth of its working. George
Lansell is a big holder in the Victoria, and his 180 mine
is next door. Should the Victoria turn out trumps,
the veteran reefer will have every reason to congratulate
himself on his year’s work. First, the Virginia has got
well on the way to being the leading mine in Bendigo ;
then the Catherine Reef struck its good stone, and now
the Victoria outlook seems to be more than cheery. The
best of it, too, is that Lansell gets his reward not because
of luck, but through sheer, downright grit, and without
any market shuffling such as has brought discredit on so
many Ballarat mines.

“ It is unnecessary to furnish an array of figures
to prove that economy is the watchword of your direc-
tors.” Thus John Leishman, chairman of directors of the
Birthday mine, Berringa (Vic.), gives a slap in the face to
those managements which, every six months, putfigures be-
fore shareholders in such a way that the novice can tell ho w
the money goes. How one so long in mining can cling to
habits and ways of the old school of directors as does
John Leishman is bewildering to his best friends. To
keep investors posted is the watchword everywhere, and
most men who do not do this are quietly shunted.
Apart from this question, the outlook at the
Birthday is none too rosy. The No. 9 level has been
disappointing throughout, and there isn’t too much good
stone left above the No. 8 to beat out, so that the future
now depends on whether good stone can be picked up at
No. 10, or whether parallel lodes, carrying payable dirt,
can be struck. The poorness of the quartz being milled is
shown by the fact that 13,000 tons, crushed last half-year,
only av eraged 3|dwt., and if there had not been some of
the sdwt. dirt to call on results might have been less.
Yet the Board, although, a. Jot of development work is
wanted, and the fljrOspeotppf.the stone improving is pro-
blematical, declares a ffiW of 6d., leaving about £lOOO to
come and go on. Truly Ballarat people love the divs.
A Vic. mining prospectus, issued the other day,
was backed by reports from the local storekeeper, the
proprietor of the local paper, the town clerk, the

more could an investing public desire—unless it might be
the opinion of the butcher'?

The papers lately printed large masses of tele-
gram and comment on the alleged failure of the State
collieries in M.L. ; but they seem somehow to have missed

* • * *
After all his talk of smashing the Melbourne
Brick Combine, after buying enough land for a dozen
brickyards and after getting machinery built and
despatched to the locality of his proposed State brick-
yard that curiously Bent person, at present Premier of
Victoria, last week practically admitted he had been
bluffing, threw in his hand, and let the combine scoop the
pool. Briefly, Bent agrees to buy all the bricks the State
may require up to Sept. 30th, 1906—t0 be not less than
4,056,000—fr0m the combine at 355. per 1000 on trucks,
instead of at 40s. as previously asked, and the combine
agrees to supply the same at that figure and also to
reduce the price to the public generally to 38s. per
1000 for the same period. It is significant that M.L. A.
McCutcheon, the heaviest Tory and anti-Socialist in
the Victorian Assembly, “assisted” Bent in his negotia-
tions with the combine—and that is the result. It is but
a few months since Thomas boasted he could get all the
bricks he wanted at 225. or 235. ; since then a struggling
competitor of the combine offered to supply the four
millions required for the central station at 275. 6d. ; and
the very fact that the combine “ sprang ” ss. to him and
2s. to the general public at the mere threat of business-
like opposition should have indicated to Bent the extent
to which the State was being bled. But no. The chief
apostle of private enterprise, Tory M‘Cutcheon, “ assisted
in the arrangement,” and the chance of breaking up a
greedy monopolistic concern slips through the Bent
fingers. As to any reason why the State should not make
the bricks for its own railway station, just as it makes
the carriages and engines—well, Bent isn’t the man to
try and answer that question.
* * • •
Superintendent Sawers, the Bank of Austral-
asia’s chief officer, retires, and C. R. Cowper, long
marked out for the post, succeeds him. The execu-
tive officers of the “ Asia ” have more responsibility than
those of most banks, for it hasn’t such a thing as a director
in Australia. While Sawers has been chief, the concern,
whose worst feature is that it is mostly foreign and sends
its dividends out of the country that earns them, has
made remarkable progress ; in 10 years the dividend has
jumped from 6 per cent, to 12 per cent., and the reserves
from £809,641 to £1,206,951, and all the time it has been
writing down securities out of profits so hard that
premises, for instance, now stand at only £306,527.
Most of the explanation is that when nearly all the other
banks were losing their heads in the ’eighties, the Bank
of Australasia had for its superintendent the late E. S.
Parkes, who sat glued to his chair like an iceberg, and, in
those giddy, drunken days, was reckoned a glum,
unpleasant person who was missing the ’bus. No great
number of people knew the man, and when he died, and
left Sawers to continue his policy (the national drunk
hadn’t quite ended), it wasn’t reckoned, even among
financial men, that Australia was losing probably the
greatest banker that Victoria, if not Australia, ever had.
But when they compare the bogus profits that the
“great ” bankers of those days made, with the real profits
that Parkes’ concern has made since, they begin to realise
the far-sightedness of the glum man who was supposed to
be missing the vehicle.
Unusual item from Sydney Telegraph’s
•‘Country News”:—“Muswellbrook, Thursday. The
business of the municipal gas-works having lately been
progressing satisfactorily, the council has been able to
establish a sinking fund for the purpose of eventually
liquidating the loan raised for the construction of the
works, which constitute the only liability upon the
municipality.” For any Australian borough to be
practically free of debt is remarkable enough ; for it to be
in a violent hurry to get rid of its little Cohen is astonish-
ing ; but for any body of aldermen in this great loan land
to do these things as a mere matter of common honesty
and sound business is simply stupendous. It might
pay N.S.W. to close Parliament altogether and get these
Muswellbrook aldermen to run things for a while.
••. * *
A, sad story. was told to a few “primary pro-
ducers ” in Melbourne, during show week, in the directors’
annual report to shareholders of the Co-operative Fruit-
growers’ Co. Briefly, the growers had expected the
undertaking to pay from the jump—and, of course, it
didn’t. Calls were unpaid and the eternal bank overdraft
ate up in interest all that could be collected. The chief
object of the co.—jam-making—had been progressing all
right, but the growers would not stand by their own con-
cern and support it till it got a name and a sale ; instead,
they yielded to the blandishments of private jam-makers,
and for the Id. or 2d. extra they got for their cases last
year, they will payback 3d. or 4d., now that their own
concern has ceased to compete. So the co. drops jam-
making and merely continues to act as agent (mostly ex-
port) ; and a co-operative concern which included 60 per
cent, of the orchardists round Melbourne, and which, in
10 years, could have had the whole fruit trade in its
hands, drops that promising future.
A war is going, on in Bananaland. Private
Enterprise is straining every nerve to smash the co-
operative company which controls a large share of the
export of butter. Prices, as a result, are now being paid
for cream which will mean a loss unless the butter realises
110 s. per cwt. on the London market, and no Australian
produce has yet reached that mark. Private Enterprise
has got one splendid weapon at work ; it is seeing reli-
giously that not a box of bad stuff goes to the Cold Coun-
try ; while the farmers, generally a weak crowd when
trying to co-operate, are frequently reminded by the
Government graders that some of their stuff is not fit to
carry the State brand. If compulsory grading did not
exist in Q. just now, the co-operators would have a sorry
chance in this fight.
• < * »
Another alleged swindle is having attention.
Hitherto, when taking delivery of coal, it has been usual
to accept what is known as the “pit certificate” from
Newcastle. The coal comes from the mines in trucks,
carefully weighed at the pit’s mouth, and the mining
people are paid on that weight. The coal is then tipped
straight into the collier, and on arrival, say, at Sydney,
the purchaser accepts delivery on the figures of the pit
certificate, a time-saving arrangement, as it is not always
convenient to weigh the cargo again. A recent consign-
ment did not go so far as it should have done, and the
quantity of the coal was complained of. The indignant
coal-mine owner made inquiries, and it was then alleged
that all sorts of devices were employed by some colliers to
• hide the coal intended to be delivered. Seeing a clean-
swept hold, a delivery note on the pit certificate would be
given, when (part of the consignment being hidden)
the quantity received would be short of what the re-
ceiver was entitled to.
• * • •
Since Collie coal was invented in Westralia no
Government has had any peace. Bigjohn boomed it, and
several Bull co.’s were induced to take up country under
mineral leases and place the alleged coal on the market.
The Railway Department was ordered to give the local
product a show, and engine-drivers were compelled to use
it, but they called loudly for something that would let
them observe running time. Two tons of Newcastle coal,
they said, were equal to three of Collie, besides being
better to handle. Also big claims were sent in by burnt-
out farmers, and the Department had to pay up for using
coal which gave more sparks and smoke than heat. At
last Dr. Jack, formerly of Queensland, was turned into a
Commission and took evidence and made experiments,
with the result that only a small proportion of Collie coal
is to be used in future. A three years’ contract with Zeb.
Lane’s Proprietary Co. was provisionally arranged on that
basis by the late Daglish Government, conditionally upon
an agreement being entered into between the miners and
the co. This has not been done, and there’ll be more
ructions in the future. Zeb. threatens to defy the unions
and carry out the contract with “free labor,” but it
remains to be seen whether the Rason Government will
ratify the provisional agreement of its predecessors. Any-
way, the Collie coal is becoming a national nuisance.

The Sunshine Harvester crowd had the happy
thought of getting 150 farmers together for a smoke-
social after the Adelaide Show, and urging them to fight
the American octopus in the matter of harvesters.
Binders were admitted to be hopelessly in the hands of
the trust, until High Protection conies, but the Sunshine
manager offered to make oath that the price of Austra-
lian-made harvesters would be reduced if sufficient Protec-
tion is given to shut out the Yankees. Incidentally, it
was suggested that Australian manufacturing concerns
should be required to produce samples of war material-
guns, clothing, &c.—with a view to their being ready
with a supply when called on.

Miners’ wages are anything up north. Gympie
pays Bs. 4d. per shift, £2 10s. for 44 hours; Mount Morgan
from Bs. to 10s., 47 hours per week. Charters Towers, £3
for 44 hours; and Ravenswood £3 for 47 hours. Mt.
Molloy, Herberton, Irvinebank and Watsonville pay 9s.
a shift for 44 hours ; Chillagoe, Mungana and Mt. Garnet
£3 for 47 hours ; the Hodgkinson £3 for 44 hours ; and
Georgetown and Croydon, £3 10s. for 44 hours. The
northern gold-miner on the whole has a shilling a shift
the best of it.

sneered at.

“ Petramos ” : The snow that fell in rural
S. Australia recently almost brought along a
little job for the undertaker. A small child ate a
large quantity, and it rushed to his stomach,
causing him considerable anguish. But some
country folk have stomachs of cast-iron. I once
took two pounds of cherries to a station, a little
way north of Adelaide. They were pretty stale
w j ien I arrived, so I gave the whole bag to a boy
who came up to unharness the horses. He had
never seen such fruit before. Ten minutes after-
wards I met him. He showed me the empty bag.
“Finished ’em,” he murmured. “They’re
orlright.” “Where are the stones?” I asked,
looking round for the remains of the two pounds
of cherries. “ Stones ?” he said. “ Stones ? Oh,
you mean them pips in the middle. Oh, I ate
them.” A youth with a couple of hundred cherry-
stones in his interior should be a profound anxiety
to himself and his friends and supporters, but this
one was quite cheerful.

“ Cobbitty ” : Jte “ Elmore’s ” question
about wombats, &c. (B. 7/8/’O5). Out in the
Oberon district, from the Fish River along the
Great Dividing Range to the Mt. Werong gold-
diggings, wombats are to be found in thousands.
They give selectors a great lot of trouble by bur-
rowing under the “ ehock-and-log ” fences, and
knocking them down. I always found that the
length of the burrows depended much on the kind
of country they were in. Have never known
them to go over GOyd. anywhere. The average
length is 25yd. to 30yd. Have often got them by
digging, shooting and trapping—mostly dig-
ging. Used to have a couple, of dogs that were
very fond of hunting them. When they got into
a burrow and started to worry one, I would listen
along the surface of the ground, and find where it
was, and then sink a hole straight down. The
depth was mostly about 2ft., and seldom over 4ft.,
except on a steep hillside. Have known them to
double back in a circular fashion, and so keep the
dogs blocked in, until released—many dogs are
starved to death in that way. The wombat is a
very savage animal when roused, and will stand
his ground against a dozen dogs. I have never
seen dogs kill one outright. A very easy way of
shooting a wombat is to set a rabbit-trap in his
burrow, tie a gun up to a sapling straight in front,
then connect a piece of string from the trigger to
the near-side jaw. I can remember crawling into
a burrow once, when a “ kid,” with a candle and
dad’s greenhide plough-reins, tied together.
These I made fast to a wombat’s leg, but I wasn’t
able to pull him out; so I went upon the hills
and caught old Commerce (an honest mare, in
spite of her name), and tied the plough-rein to
her. When all was ready, I hit the m. re
across the rump. She bolted like a mad
thing down the stony range with a big wombat
bumping behind like a full-blown football. Com-
merce never stopped until she blundered head-first
into an old sawpit and broke her neck. I untied
the reins and drove the wombat home —it seemed
very little the worse for its rough journey across
the selection. I never said a word about the old
mare ; but dad often said “it was a damn funny
thing how old Commerce came to break her neck
in the sawpit! ”
“ TheProoshan” : The
new-chum fossicker is easily
detected by his style of pan-
ning-off wash. He is so
frightened of losing a speck
that he takes as long over
one dish as an expert would
over six. The old-hand
slaps the dish well under
water, swirls it roughly
about, ever keeping the dirt
alive, and, with deft fingers
and the right employment
of water, soon reduces the
stuff to a spoonful. He
loses little or no gold, while
the tyro, afraid of the water,
keeps the stuff dry at the
back of the pan, and, with
his lap, lap at the lip of the
dish, earns much backache
and loses gold (provided it’s
there to lose). A really
good way of disposing of
the last bit of sand is to tip
the dish, allowing the water
to drain off, then to spit the
biggest spit at command
into the very eye of the
gold, whereupon the sand
at once rises free from the
metal, and is easily washed
out of the dish. This an-
swers also with tin.
Irump : Often wondered how foxes suc-
ceeded in capturing fowls when latter are roosting
m trees. An old farmer laughed at my ignorance
the other day, and said that it was done quite
simply. The fox first attracts their attention,
and, having done this, he runs round and round
the tree until the fowls, fascinated by his gyra-
tions, or giddy with trying to follow his circular
proceedings, tumble to the ground.

‘‘Terry” : Travelling from Burkefcown to
the Tablelands with a mate—horses in low condi-
tion and finances ditto—we were offered a Road
Board job to clean out a well; work to be inspected
and_ passed by the manager of an adjoining
station. When we got to the well my mate
would not go down ; and, as he was weakly and I
bulky, I wouldn’t chance letting him lower me.
So we set to work and baled her, wetted the
ground well, and collected a lot of dingo bones
close to the mouth. The white ants had built
nests on the timbers for a good way down, and we
were to clean these off. We thought we were
beaten, when it happily occurred to us to knock
them off with stones, so we pelted away for a day,
and after using half a ton of geology we had ’em
all knocked into the water. Told the manager we
had finished, and when he came over we showed
him the debris we had collected, told him we had
got it out of the well, and asked him to let us
lowe ; r him down to inspect. It was a risky bluff,
but it came off. He didn’t like the idea of being
let down 80 feet, so he passed the work as well
and faithfully done, and paid us ten beautiful
quid. We’ve never had occasion to pass that way
since. [lf “Terry” ever passes this way The
Bulletin will be glad to tell him that it thinks
him a thief for so taking down the Road
Board.]
* * * *
Bishop Gilbert White’s reported dis-
covery of a blackfellow 7ft. high in Carpen-
taria, “proportionately built, possessing, in addi-
tion to a perfectly-moulded form, the strength of
a Sandow,” is quite in keeping with early reports
of the luxuriance of much of the Gulf country.
The aborigines, their trees, birds and fishes have
impressed most travellers by their unusual size.
Captain Pennefather joined “strapping” men,
with “gigantic” palms and “huge” catfish.
There is no doubt that the Gulf countrv will be
found highly suitable for white settlement, despite
the claims set up for yellow labor.

“ Hiphen ” : “ C.H.R.’' (B. 7/9/’O5) says
that a homing bee does not always fly straight.
This is true in the case of a bee that is return-
ing from the club, but any self-respect-
ing bee, full of good, non-intoxi-
cating pollen, returns to his
domicile as straight as the fly
crows. . I fear those gentlemen
who aver that they have traced
the bee in his homeward flight
are gentlemen whose consciences
have run to imagination. The
bee flies as straight and as fast
as a commercial traveller drinks.
The best way, if youwant honey,
is to use treacle.
“ Workington ” : I believe the total in-
ability of the Bill-Jim person to judge distances is
his strongest point. I was recently wayfaring
from ; Moree to Pallamallawa, and, after several
abortive attempts to fix the mileage, lit out on a
20-mile basis, this being the estimate of a stock
and station agent. At Boolooroo, four miles out,
met a teamster and he thought the distance from
there to Pallamallawa was 19 miles. A mile
further on met a weird-looking individual, who
said he didn’t know the exact distance but it must
be or 23 miles. Later, with the village in
sight, about two miles on the starboard bow, a
hardy peasant informed me it was just six miles
from the last mile peg !

“Jimmie Pannikin”: At one well-kept
hotel on the Darling we had two eggs each for
breakfast every morning. Each egg was branded
with a name and a date. The names were
‘‘Auntie,’ “Biddy,” “Speckleay,” “Whitie,”
“ Blackie.” “ Orpie, ” “Leggie,”&c. One morn-
ing the bank-inspector rang the cow-bell vigor-
ously. “ Tell Mrs. Dropogoodstuff that I decline
to eat these two eggs put on my plate.” The girl,
Mulga Mary, carried the rejected eggs out. In-
spection showed that they were branded “ Whitie,
10/7/’O3” and “Biddy, 11/9/’Ol.”
Deeby : Told to me by the oldest
native of Wellington (N.S.W.). The first sheep
station established thereabouts was worked almost
entirely by convicts, kindly lent by the Govern-
ment of the day. The men’s eating room adjoined
the kitchen, their “ feed ” being passed to them
through a ticket-office-like opening cut in the
partition. One day “Cockney George,” offered
to kiss the cook through this orifice, and that
shocked and virtuous female reported the matter
to her mistress. George denied the charge, and
was ordered to receive 15 lashes for his affability
to the cook and 15 more for telling a lie. The
mistress, after the manner of the gentle and refined
English lady of the period, stood by with her baby
in her arms to watch the flogging, and the baby
applauded loudly. In fact it was so much pleased,
and so much disappointed when the show was
over, that George got five lashes extra to keep the
offspring amused. He might have got more than
that, but the infant got tired just about then, and
wanted to go and stick a pin in the cat.

This from an irreverent young woman :
The local preacher is one of a species I can’t get the
hang of, somehow. lam camping in the lean-to at the
back of a country school. The lean-to is misnamed the
teacher’s residence. The school-building is used every
Sunday as a Methodist church ; so I stay in bed and hear
all that goes on the other side of the partition. Last
Sunday a galoot was talking through his neck about the
99 sheep and the one stray. He said he knoo they
’adn’t sheep, but supposin’they was’eife.rs instead. He
asked them, in a crescendo voice, what they’d do if one
of their ’eifers was gone—wouldn’t they go trampin’ an’
trampin’ all day, per’aps through failin’ timbers, an’
burnin’ timbers, an’ scrub, or per’aps through floods?
But nothin’—nothin’ would stop them from findin’ that
’eifer. He then likened his congregation to ’eifers
wandering to perdition. Poor unfortunate South Gipps-
landers 1 The little perdition they’ve got up their sleeves
isn’t worth mentioning. Their only recreation is the
weekly visit to listen to the sky-pilot who drones at them
orribly about ’eifers.
BOUNDING UP CATTLE ON A SALVA-
TION ARMY IMMIGRANT FARM.
S. A. Agricultural Congress has been sitting
on the fox—not literally, for the brute would
bite from underneath, but sitting in consulta-
tion —there being some doubt as to whether
it does not do as much good by killing rab-
bits as harm by killing sheep. Narracoorte dis-
trict council, in the south-east of the State, paid
bonuses on 1915 foxes destroyed last year ; on the
other hand, some sheep-owners in the same dis-
trict advertise that they will prosecute anyone
hunting on their properties. The Congress (almost
unanimously) decided that Reynard is a pest, and
that legislation for his destruction is wanted.

A Chinaman, making for Queensland from
the Northern Territory, was recently lost between
Anthony’s Lagoon and Renner Springs. A police-
man found the heathen having his dinner. A
bullock had got bogged, and John had coolly made
a fire at the rear part of the beast. As the meat
cooked, the Chow devoured it. He had no knife
nor any sharp instrument, so pulled the half-cooked
m eat off with his teeth. Hi-yah !
CONNEMARA CREEK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now, the little one-horse township known as Connemara
Creek
Was the home of peace and harmony—an Eden, so to
speak.
T.iere was only one church-building in this hamlet in the
hush
For the “ West-leans,” and the “ Romans,” and the
11 Church of Ingland Push ” !
Tll ei dno local priest or parson, as the place was much
too small,
And, except the church, no building that was public,
save the “ Hall.”
They’d one butcher, and one baker, and one pub. and
general store,
And one blacksmith (Sandy William), and one constable-
no more !
When there was a Sunday service all the township used
to go
(Made no difference who was preaching), “ just to give the
bloke a show.”
Church was always in the morning, barring when there
was full moon,
And they played “ two-up ” religiously all Sunday after-
noon.
And the bobby used to go to sleep, as bobbies ought to
do
When the law-abiding citizen requires a drink or two,
And a more obliging officer it would be vain to seek
Than the guard of law and order up at Connemara Creek !
’Twas an axiom of the settlers—one you may not quite
condone—
That a man who wanted mutton was a fool to kill his
own ;
But a custom once accepted a community allows,
So they killed each others’ wethers and they milked each
others’ cows 1
And if Jones had fowl for dinner, folks were not aston-
ished when
They were told that Smith or Robinson had lost a Brahma
hen !
But although in moral principles they might be some-
what weak,
They were happy in their sinfulness at Connemara
Creek 1
But, alas, when Sandy William took a trip to Sydney
town,
Though his little wife had cautioned hirn he might be
“ taken down,”
He behaved in such a manner that he wasn’t there two
days
When his cash .was all expended in most questionable
ways !
And he stood before a portrait which he noticed pasted
on
Some large hoarding, and exclaimed : “Why, that is old
Jack Robertson ! ”
But a gentle voice behind him breathed in honeyed
accents smooth :
“ No, my brother ! you’re mistaken ; that’s our general,
General Booth ! ”
MOTORING ON THE MELBOURNE-SYDNEY ROAD.
T. Rand dashing into creek near Longwood (Vie.), on Bent’s
neglected highways.
Sandy William grew repentant, and to Connemara
Creek
He returned with Ensign Blowhard, and in less than half
a week
What had been a peaceful township, and a happy, had
become
Something very like a sacerdotal pandemonium 1
Ensign Blowhard raised a “regiment” and the regiment
raised a row,
And the riot raised the money, which came pouring in
somehow.
It was wondrous where it came from, but it did ! Till it
was known
One fine morning Ensign Blowhard had departed “ on his
own.”
Took the cash? He did most surely. He took every
blessed bean !
Which the clergy all regarded as unpardonably mean !
He was never in the “Army”; he was “pitching fairy
tales,”
And had learned the style from “ officers ” who visit at
the gaols !
But there’s once more peace and harmony on Sunday
afternoon
While the bobby sleeps a sleep that’s something very like
a swoon ;
But though years have flown it’s still unsafe of holy Booth
to speak,
His mem’ry has been buried deep at Connemara Creek !

“ Bowyang ” tells a tale of infamy
In the matter of dog-“ doping ” (B. 22/6/’O5). I
and another scoundrel once owned and ran some pretty
good dogs at country coursing meets. In those days we
knew how to train them, although our knowledge of
doping and betting tactics had not yet matured. A visit
to a metropolitan course convinced us that there was
something at times in running a bye. Next trip we had
wh B, ?° U !u e t ; ou i: ses > and on the morrow had to do
battle for the final. Partner suggested stiffening the dog.
Scientific doping was a bit beyond us, but our candidate
that morning received a generous leg of boiled mutton to
make him heavy, and the senior partner, without entering
into needless details, deputed a hairy mutual friend, also
from the backblocks, to place our available capital at the
best price obtainable on the other canine. But the hare
didn t go long enough to give the boiled mutton
a chance ; and two more - dejected sports never
gathered up the dog that had caused their ruin
At the gate, we were met by a hatless, breathless indi-
vidual, very audibly agitated, but whose general demeanor
s ® e “ led t 0 suggest a possibility. In occasional intervals
of lucidity, he explained that he had in some cases to lav
heavy odds, but he had managed to get the money all
on .-' 'l®, cursed appropriately ; but when he further
related that he had collected and had sufficient bullion
over and above what he had spent on beer, to sink a
whaleboat, we fell upon his neck like one man, and
blessed his dunderheaded greenness. The wink had not
been given to him with sufficient clearness. And if I
hadn t, in that wild, irresponsible moment, leaped a yard
in the air and yelled, “ Hooray ! We backed our own dog
after all, no one would have suspected anything.

“The Prooshan ” on a form of bush
etiquette:—
The portrait album is the great first factor in bush
courtships. It lies on a little home-made woollen mat on
the front-room table. The “ albun,” as it is mostly
called, is treated With as much respect as the Mussul-
man’s Koran, and if little Jim ventures into the holy of
holies While Mrs, Timepayment is on call to get the name
of the maker of the peeanner, and should dare to touch it
with , his paw, young Bill, his senior by three years, lets
fly With, “Kar! don’t you touch the halbun with those
Sticky fingers.” When Amy’s young man that is to be
gets admittance to the front room and has examined
“The Arrival of the First Fleet,” “ The Landing of Cap-
tain Cook,” and other of the fine art works upon the walls,
Amy asks him if he “ kilows any of these,” at the same
time opening the album. She has to lean well over his
arm when explaining Off-side pictures, and her warm
breath, or something, soon leads up to a proposal. But
they never tire of the album, and every night the youth
says, “ Urn, that’s you when you were a kinchin, ain’t
it?’’and at the last page Amy says “That’s the lot,’
After six mouths of this they marry as a rule.

“Brick” : Dimboola (Vic ), which is half
German, was invaded a while back by some of
Henry Lawson’s bush heroes. They sopped up so
much beer that immediate telegrams for more
beer went down the line. Sundry accidents hap-
pened to them. One crawled into the bread-man’s
bakehouse, and went to sleep in the just-set
sponge. Another entered the bootshop and pelted
the boot-man with his own boots. A third met
,one of the citizens, who wasn’t singing “God
Save ” loud enough. He gave him a patriotic
push on the nose, which sat him in the gutter.
But when four of them marched up and down,
singing “To Hell with the Kaiser,” Dimboola
arose and pushed them outside its gates with
much violence. I met them making their way to
Nhill, where Irvine comes from. They told me
their woes, finishing up with “Them there blokes
ain’t got no bowels.” I replied in German. I
learnt eleven new swear words in the next
seconds.
CELESTIAL RETALIATION.
1ST. COCKIE : “ We're Kevin foine say sons the
now."
2ND. C OCKIE: “ Yis, since that Queensland bloke
gave up callin' the weather names it ain't
been bad."
A curious incident occurred at the Kal-
goorlie Criminal Court. A native was charged,
under the name of Weebo, with the murder of a
gin, and another native named Mullud was
charged along with him. Weebo died while the
case still hung fire, and was buried under the name
of Mullud. Then the Court proceeded to finish
trying Mullud under the name of Weebo, and,
finding its mistake, it decided that the matter was
too far gone to be disentangled, and told Mullud
to go away and look for ’possum, and be a better
aborigine in future.

“ WalD.” tells the story of an arbitrator: —
They had drifted apart, they who, for many years, had
tramped together. A few hot words, after a silly quarrel
and a hasty resolve, and they stood, each with his swag
rolled, ready to say “Good-bye.” But neither spoke,
and, after an awkward pause, they turned to go. Laddie,
the dog, who had followed their fortunes ever since' the
day when, while yet a puppy, the two mates had found
him, ran uneasily from one to the other, as they slowly
separated, whining as he went in a dismal fashion. When
100 yards or more lay between the two, Laddie sat upon
his haunches and howled as though in entreaty. Both
men hesitated, turned round, and then moved slowly
towards each other. As they approached him, Laddie
showed a frenzy of delight. The two men reached each
other. One said awkwardly, “D you, Bill!” The
other hesitated a little and then said, “ D-- you, Ben.”
Then they both d d the dog, and once more all was
brotherhood.

“ Veritas ” on cats :
Twenty years ago native cats were fairly plentiful in
North Gippsland, Vic. When lads, we used to go
’possuming, and would almost always encounter a dozen
or more of them. They were very bold and decidedly
cunning. They used to regularly pay visits to the fowl
roost, which in those days consisted of an old log fence
or a pig-sty. Cat is (or was) an animal of epicurean
tastes when variety was obtainable, being particularly
fond of duck. Put three ducks under a gin case one night,
and half-a-dozen bricks on top. Next morning one duck
was dead. The cat’s method was to scratch a very small
hole under the box, kill the duck and suck the blood,
very little of the flesh being touched. I determined to
catch pussy, so set two rabbit-traps, one at supposed
place of cat’s entrance and the other alongside. Second
night found cat secured by the snout in one (the plate of
which I had greased) and by the hind leg in the other.
I have not seen a native cat about here for many years.

“Wiljar”: The newchum wasn’t rheu-
matic, and didn’t intend to get so. When the boss,
prior to crossing the One Tree Plain, pitched a
few sticks into the waggon for billy-boiling pur-
poses, the newchum looked puzzled. “ What
about wood?” he asked. “Waggon,” said the
boss. “Not enough there.” “Be d——d !”
snorted the boss ; “if yer want a forest chuck on
wot wood yer like.” First day’s journey over,
the new chum threw off about 15s. worth of wood
and lit it. After tea he raked away the ashes and
made two beds on the ground—where the fire had
been. “ Going to turn in ?” he interrogated with
restraint. “ Yuss.” The boss picked up one of
the beds and retreated into the gloom. “If I
freeze to death,” he hissed to himself, “ I’ll freeze
game! ”

“ D.H.R.”: I have seen men who could
“ flick ” the head off a venomous snake. Once in
Mungindi district (on Mungindi station) saw a
boundary-rider swoop down on a tiger snake, pick
him up by the tail, flick him above his shoulder,
and that snake’s head left his body with much
expedition. Another time, in the Walgett
(N.S.W.) district, a man (Arthur M‘Cormic),
stockman on Gingis station, was seen by several
to snatch a seven-feet brown snake from his
hurried career, and thus behead him with celerity
and neatness.
“ Ya'rraV” ' _ “Davrick ” (B. 24/8/’O5) is
wrong in asserting that the Australian blacks
have only three known remedies. Though the
niggers of this country are' sadly deficient in l
sanitation, they possess quite a large, unwritten
pharmacopoeia. Some of their herbal remedies—■
and they are legion—effect marvellous' cures l ,
Again, iguana oil (the fat of that reptile boiled
down) is an accepted salve for rheumatism, cold
in the chest, soreness, and bruises. All bushmen
Swear by it, For cuts, certain natural muds
properly used are better than any balsam or
the surgeon’s needle. Saw a Hunter River
black, Known locally as No Nose Tommy,
Chopping a 1 log in search of edible grubs, when
the ax& it/issed the' wood. Consequence was that
Tommy’s shin Whs ripped up; leaving a gaping
wound fully eight inches long and an inch deep,
and laying the bone bare. Stoically he bore the
pain till his lubra went to the river bank—the
locale was Limeburner’s Creek—and obtained a
quantity of black ooze. This she plastered on the
injured limb, then it covered over with blue gUntJ
leaves, binding up tightly with strips of wild flax.
Inside of three weeks I saw my countryman, fit,
well, and desirous of rum, in Raymond Terrace, 15
miles from the scene of the accident.

“ : Why will the half-baked
naturalists who write to the Sydney dailies per-
sist in calling the lyre-bird the “native pheasant”?
Menura, is but distantly related to Phasianus,
which has only one representative in Australia—a
Queensland bird of plain black and brown plum-
age, but otherwise closely resembling P. colchicus,
the typical pheasant. It looks bigger by reason of
its plentiful feathering, but is really so miserably
skinny that it’s hardly worth a cartridge. The
cock-bird’s call, heard oftenest at dawn and dusk,
is a hollow, far-reaching “hoo-hoo-hoo,” which
suggests anything but a bird’s call. Probably
this fowl occasionally moves southwards into
N.S.W. ; and I have an idea that it is its weird
call which recently set sundry aboriginaliars
guessing.

“Vigilans”: Victoria Downs station
(N.T.) musters 60,000 head of cattle on 11,000
square miles of country ; rent.
Is. per square mile. They
employ six white men. Wave
Hill measures 6700 square
miles and musters about the
same. There are no im-
provements or wells. Mr.
Tom Cahill, of Wave Hill,
said in a recent Bulletin
that the white man can do
all the tropical work on these
stations. Exactly. There is
no work in the N.T. or Kim-
berley that a white man can-
not do if you pay him for
doing it. Victoria and Daly
River country is splendid,
and would grow anything of
a tropical or sub-tropical
nature; yet this country is
cut up into “blocks” the
size of Ireland, and held by
syndicates at a bob a mile.
There is a 60-inch rainfall,
and what could not be done
with that ? The stations em-
ploy half-a dozen whites and
scores of niggers, with a
Chow cook. The climate for
six months is ideal; two are
warmish ; four are hot. But
malaria is a myth. Fruit
and vegetables grow without
any trouble. White ants are
a drawback. Cut this land
up, and the country would
make rapid strides. To say
the white can’t work in Dar-
win is bunkum. The station-
master has been there for 18
years without going away.
The proprietor of the Club
Hotel has worked for nine
years in Darwin. He built
the railway sheds —was 18
months working on the gal-
vanised iron roof without a
day’s spell, and knocked out
16 Chows. Pay the white
man, and there is no work he
cannot do in Darwin or any other part of Aus-
tralia. But the mean whites of the North support
cheap Asiatics and say the white man is not
reliable.
“Den”: Here in S. Aus. we call the
butcher-bird the Darwin jackass. Have had one
for two years. Like jack., he loves raw meat and
insects. Also eats mice and sparrows, which he
catches. Is a creature of sedentary habits and
irritable temperament. Emits an annoyed cackle
when strangers approach, and is game for any-
thing that walks or flies. Have taught him to
whistle, which he does clearly and shrilly, but
only when annoyed. He has the run of the yard ;
and he hangs his surplus tucker round on shrubs
and trees —-hence, I suppose, his name—and uses
shocking language when the hens raid his larder.
* X * *
‘ * Tramp ” : Mohammed is getting a solid
footing in some Vic. farming districts. Farmers
are beginning to employ him about the dairies,
and occasionally rent him onion land on third
share. One farmer in the Western District will
never pass Mohammed on the road without offer-
ing him a lift, and he is such a big employer of
black—and cheap—labor that he is already known
as the Sultan of Johore. Mohammed’s bundle is
becoming scarce in the land, and Mohammed him-
self is now often a dairy farmer and runs his buggy
and pair. Regarded in his agricultural capacity,
Mohammed is anything but a hard worker, has
the instincts of a pig, and the principles of a
trouser-button. And his home life ? I have seen
eight of him domiciled in a ten by eight hovel.
They have not yet arrived at the thought of sani-
tation, and the odor that meets one at the door of
their hut is—well, Mohammed.
“ Gorm ” : Have often wondered which is
the fastest flier of our Australian birds. I’m
inclined to place the “green leek” first. The
quail, swallow, and bronzewing all fly well when
in a hurry, but the “ leek,” when cut off from his
mates at roosting time, goes like green lightning.

“Drifter” : Has any bushnian known a
horse’s hoofs to grow again after completely
crumbling away? Know of a horse that was
worked at a Newcastle (N.S.W.) mining pit,
where the ants bored into his hoofs. Eventually
the animal was limping painfully on the mere
shells of his original shale crushers. Owner finally
got him out to a swamp padd'gck towards
Waratah, where it was his intention to shoot
him, but his heart failed him. Later the
horse was seen crawling and picking the grass
across the semi-marshy places, and about five
months after, when his owner happened to
wander out one day, he was astonished to see his
crippled gee-gee trotting festively round with
some young’uns that had been turned into the
paddock. Investigation showed that the hoofs
had grown almost perfect again. The animal is
now racing round Sydney in a commercials
buggy.
* * * •
‘ ‘ Elmore ” : There are many curious finds
made in wool stores. In one season (1900) a man
in the wool business told me he found 15 pocket
knives, four dog-hooks, a coat, two hats, three
coins (highest a 25.-piece), a dead lamb, flat as
Saturday’s beer, and a pair of shears. Needles
and palms can be found in nearly every bale, and
rocks and lumps of iron, such as camp-ovens,
broken up, are not uncommon.
BUSH PHILOSOPHY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you’ve been and made a blunder,
What’s the use of lookin’ blue ?
Laugh and face the chaps around yer
They have done far worse than you;
Don’t let others see yer chewin’
’Cause yer head won’t fit yer hat;
Knock off mopin’, and be doin’
Anything but whip the cat.
Lord ! it makes me feel quite fishy
When my mate harps on the “ Wish he
Hadn’t been and blewed his money
On that little burst he had.”
And the theme of every sentence
Is a forty-pound repentance
For the paltry three-and-tenpence
Spent in beer that made him bad.
Put away those priest-made notions
That “mankind was made to mourn”:
That “ we’re only here to fit us
For a brighter, happier morn.”
This is all the life we’re sure of ;
Laugh, and chance the life elsewhere ;
Our first duty on the earth is
To be happy while we’re there.
So, when you’ve been getting beerful,
Don’t sit whining, glum, and tearful,
Keep your pecker up ; look cheerful.
Paste this maxim in your hat:
“ When your sin’s worth only tenpence.
Don’t waste pounds in vain repentance ;
Keep your eye on this short sentence :
‘ Life’s too short to whip the cat.’ ”
8. A.

SALVATION BOOTH’S IMMIGRANTS, A VISION OF THE FUTURE.


The sorry dyspeptic, submerged in his gloom,
Who feels most dejected and fit for his tomb ;
The poor hypochondriac who wanders in woe,
With no one to speak to, and nowhere to go,
Whom no consolation nor comfort can please,
Who maintains he’s a martyr to every disease,
|Let them aid their digestion and keep their blood pure
And recruit upon Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*

the Tooth family, headed by Sydney’s old brewer
friend, .Robert Lucas, are fighting the church
authorities over a memorial window to certain
deceased Tooths in Cranbrook Church, Kent. So
far the fight is against the brewers, but the 17
Teeth are resolved to see it out.

their usual wreath of bay laurel and daffodils this
year to the grave of J. A. Hartley, killed in Sep-
tember, 1896. They had the wreath inscribed
in affectionate remembrance of their great
chief.” Few Government officials are remem-
bered in that way for nine years after their death
but Hartley was an exceptional man.
The wealth that three rich dead men left
behind them was formally announced in Mel-
bourne last week. James Aitken, of Tooralc, left
£53,281. John Russell M'Pherson deserted £60,000
and Colin William Simpson abandoned £8534 in
Victoria and £173,065 in Hew South Wales, the
latter representing station property in Riverma.
Premier Bent, of Victoria, once admitted
that he was in the wrong. The event happened
so long ago that no one remembers the exact
thing that he was wrong about. Anyhow, Bent
had just discovered that he had put his foot into
it, and held forth in strenuous language. “I’ve
been a fool, he shouted to a friend 9 “a hignora-
inus, that’s what I am—a hignoramus. And, by
thunder, sir, you can spell it with a capital H.”
Ex-N". S. Wales Justice W. J. Foster,
whose name was pointedly mentioned during the
debate on the Judges’ Pension Abolition Bill, was
in January, 1831. He came to Australia in
1852, took to farming, and then studied for the
Bar, and was called in 1858. He was appointed a
Crown Prosecutor in 1859, and held office till 1877
when he became Attorney-General in the Farnell
Government, with a seat in the Legislative Coun-
cil. In 1887 he was Parkes’ Attorney-General.
I his office he resigned because Parkes broke faith
with him, as he did with Edward Butler in the
matter of a Supreme Court Judgeship. On Feb-
ruary 14, 1888, he was appointed Supreme Court
Judge, which appointment he resigned on De-
cember 17, 1894, after less than seven years’ ser-
vice, As a Judge he drew nearly £lB,OOO salary •
as a pensioner, £19,565, or a total as Judge and
pensioner of close on £38,000.

William Henry Moore and Frederick Garling, who arrived
in 1814, were given £4OO a-year each as a subsidy. Garling
became Clerk of the Peace, with private practice ; and
Moore, in 1826, became Acting Attorney-General during
the absence of Saxe Bannister. He was also Crown
Solicitor and Coroner. But the litigants in the teens of
last century were numerous, and the court, which con-
sisted of Mr. Barron Field, admitted one James Norton
to practice. Norton had come out as clerk to a female
convict ship, and having had a legal training he got into
practice quickly. The firm he founded still exists. Next
to him came George Allen, who arrived in the colony at
the age of 16 years, and was allowed to practise at the age
22 - , The bond ’ solicitors were George Crosslev
Edward Eagar, and Michael Robinson, who had a large
practice before and after the arrival of Moore and Garlino-.

A certain young Queenslander is a striking
example of the indomitable energy occasionally
met with m the true Australian type,
.porn of humble parents, he first engaged
m the Mines Department as a clerk.
He was sent to one of the mining towns.
After a year or two he asked leave to sit
•4 • University examinations. While
Brisbane he explained to the office
that he had obtained an engineer’s cer-
tificate, and asked permission, while in
the city, to go up for examination as a
mine manager. He succeeded at the
University exam., and also got his mine
manager’s certificate, provided he put in
a certain time under ground. Quite
recently he was admitted to practise as
a barrister, and he is now studying bac-
teriology—on a salary of £9O a year !
He may not have made much money so
far but for energy a red ant and a bee
and a circular saw don’t compare with
him.

W. F. Schey, autocrat of N.S.W. unem-
ployed, is a Londoner, of warrior ancestors, and
has been just on 30 years in Australia. He is 48
years of age, and one of the fat men of the day.
Narandera (N.S.W.) Argus has a political
history. It was once owned by N.S.W. Minister
for Lands Ashton, and Victorian Opposition
leader Prendergast served his time on it, and
afterwards bossed the shop.
That brawny, capable Scot, McGregor,
who long frizzled as a Governor in New Guinea
and West Africa, now freezes in Newfoundland
his personal pull in the Colonial Office being still
weak. His 10 years’ hard work in Papua entitled
him, as he thought, to a Governorship in Queens-
land or South Australia, or some other healthy
climate, but men with more influence and less
brains got the billets. In acquiring a good
governorship woman has more influence than art,
science and literature combined.
“F.”: Kindly let me correct an error
re the M‘Arthurs (B. 21/9/’O5). Capt. John
Macarthur, of the old “Rum-sellers,” is a dis-
tinct personage from the other Australian
M‘Arthurs. The rum-seller was of Scotch descent,
born at Plymouth, where Governor Bligh said his
father kept a tailor’s shop. He (John) had sons,
but none of them left a male descendant, Mrs.
Macarthur-Onslow being the sole representative!
The other M‘Arthurs are North of Ireland Wes-
leyans, and in the softgoods trade.
Labor-member Lunsford, of Queensland,
died lately at Cunnamulla, whither he had gone
to breathe some of the far-western air, in the
hope of getting relief from lung troubles. Duns-
ford’s heart was in the cause of Democracy, and
he attended so closely to his Parliamentary duties
that he did not once miss “prayers” in the
Assembly during his membership. He was a
prominent fighter during the great shearers’
strike, and in recognition of his merits Charters
Towers made him its member, and returned him
regularly as long as he lived. Altogether he
represented the golden town for 12J years.
The wine and spirit business in which the
late W. E. Sparke, of Sydney, made his fortune,
stood upon the same spot for over three-quarters
of a century, and only recently the ancient build-
ing made way for a new structure. It stood in a
lane opposite the Victoria Markets, and was
founded by Richard Brownlow, who had kept the
Canning Tavern in King-street. Most of the
wine and spirit merchants of that time had evolved
from the public-house. After Brownlow come
Dan Egan, Walford and Sparke, and D’Arcy and
Co., in which Mr. Carson, the Sparke legatee, has
an interest.
Decided by medical observation in Mel-
bourne that there is nothing mentally wrong with
P. A. M‘Anulty, ex-taxing officer of the Supreme
*-Vurt—the man who refused to be sacked.
M Anulty s curious actions were, presumably,
the result of a too acute sense of grievance *
his race s capacity for nursing a grievance
is notable, and a too tenderly nursed grievance is
apt to become as a snake in the bosom. Under the
circumstances, the Victorian Government would
take the dignified course if it treated M ‘Anulty ’s
case with a big leaning towards mercy.
Ned Hawker, the new president of the
S.A. National Ass, is a great improvement on
lawyer Moulden, M.L.C., who preceded him
Hawker is a Cambridge M.A., a lawyer, and a
squatter; but now, having travelled through the
mining districts of North and South America,
and of Spain, practises as a mineralogist. He sat
in the Assembly long ago, but was defeated last
May for Adelaide. He is a son of the late
Speaker Hawker, and a grandson of Admiral
Hawker. His wife is a daughter of Chief Justice
Stawell, of Victoria, and is herself a University
graduate. Hawker is acknowledged by his
strongest opponents to be a courteous man and a
scrupulously fair fighter.
Yarrurn ’ : Talking to a retired coastal
skipper, last night, he told me that, among other
things, the late Ed. Hargraves, alleged first dis-
coverer of gold m Australia, had presented to him
by the merchants of London a case of silver plate
valued at over £5OO. The old skipper says he
took it from a ship in Sydney harbor, and
delivered it to Hargraves at Noraville, near Gos-
tord. Ihe late Queen, the ancient also informed
me, gave Hargraves a cheque for £500: but I
fancy that s a “ fairy.”
“ Den ” from Adelaide :
I stood on the kerbstone watching the Eight Hours
procession, t’other day, while the crowd cheered Premier
Tom Price who marched with his brother unionists.
Suddenly an agitated camera was clicked near my ear.
“ Bai Jove, who’s thet fella thej'’re making the noise
about ? ” inquired that which worked the kodak. I
turned and beheld a Willie boy, evidently just off the
mailboat. I told him “ thet ” was the Premier. “ Ah,
premier of the labah union, and thet sort of rot, Ai sup-
pose,” he remarked. I assured him no ; Premier of the
State, the top-notch man of the country, and a stone-
mason by trade. “ Oh, thet’s too rich, old chep,” he
said. “Ai wasn’t born yesterday, yer know.” Some of
the bystanders corroborated my statement, and then the
Willie stood;and gasped. “Is thet so?” he murmured.
“ And can’t you get a better sort o{ a johnny than thet
for your Premier ? ” I assured him it was the best we
could muster, and there had been worse, much worse.
He looked grave at that. Then he? said : “ What’s the.
fellah’s salary ! ” I told him. “ Dem it all! ” he pried,
“Ai think Ai’ll stop here for a while. Ai’m not doin,’
anything much just npw,, you know ; jest strollin’ round,
and thet sort of thing. Did you say a thousand pounds?
Bai Gad ! Not a bad little place to live in either. Ai;
stay.” So Price’s reign will be short. That
Willie is after his job.
About an explorer :
The late Ernest Giles tackled exploration work as far
back as 1861. In that year, he was out run-hunting on
the Lower Paroo, and went over a lot of country in the
Albert district. Panton, the veteran Melbourne P.M.,
went to the same part of the country on a similar mission
in the following year, and did a perish before he got back
to the settlements. His rations were exhausted, and his
torse thoroughly done up, when he ran across a cave in
the Scrope Range. Entering' it, he found a swag of stores
carefully done up in little bags, with a note requesting the
tinder to help himself. The note was signed “E. Giles.”
Pan ton did not broach the stores, but took a turn in his
belt and pushed on to Menindie Lake, which he reached
late the following night. He saw a small fire, and thought
he had struck a blackfellows’ camp, but found a tent with
Giles and a companion inside—waiting for rain. They
had a, nestful of swans’ eggs, and Panton got outside five
right away. They all camped there for several days, and
so a lifelong friendship was commenced. Old Baron von
Mueller took a grtat fancy to Giles, in whom he discerned
the makings of a great explorer, and sent him out (at the
baron s expense) to the Macdonnell Ranges. Tietkens ac-
companied him, and the baron was so pleased with the
results that he equipped them for another trip to the Mus-
grave Ranges. They pushed right on to the W.A.
boundary, where they were confronted by desert country,
which they could not penetrate for want of stores. At
any rate, they traversed three-fourths of the route after-
wards taken by Bigjohn Forrest, when making for the
overland telegraph-line from the W.A. side. The intrepid
Giles next made what he termed “ a forgotten expedition ”
from Eucla to Port Augusta. His splendid exploration
work met with a poor reward. To Brer Alick Forrest an
imposing monument is erected in the public park of
Perth to mark his services as an explorer. Giles, who
performed far better work under far harder conditions
rests m a lonely grave in a Westralian goldfields cemetery,
already forgotten by the men whose fortunes he helped to
build.

Iceberg Irvine, from being a sort of fetish
in Victorian politics, has gradually sunk out of
sight until now he has become a back number.
The Augus resuscitates him on every possible
opportunity, but its “Mr. Irvine says so” is a
matter of no public interest. Irvine is still on tap
at Women’s League meetings and other political
dolls’-housebusiness ; but his “deliverances” are no
longer “great,” and members saunter out to the
bar when he commences, just as if he were not
the -“calm, strong equity lawyer” of Victorian
politics.
