Only as far back as 1902 the following
question was asked in the Queensland Assembly:
In view of the fact that prickly pear is now proved to
be valuable both as food and drink for stock, and likewise
as supplying- fruit and vegetable to man, will the Minister
for Lands see that this valuable plant is protected ?
Griffith and Allan, Patent Attorneys of
Sydney, have opened a branch of their business in
Melbourne—(Broken Hid Chambers, Queen-st.),
under the management of Mr. H. P. Keoah of
Melbourne.* 8 ’
Everybody has noticed how vastly Tommy
Bent’s politics have improved of late. But everybody
does not know that said improvement dated from Thomas’s
first visit to Daly’s reconstructed Turkish Baths, Royal
Arcade, Melbourne. Thomas breathed fervent blessings
on the improvements in the Baths, as he lay smiling on
the marble slab. Since then the blessings have returned
to him and sweetened his political career. What’s the
old adage about one clean politician being nearer to
godliness than a whole row of Wesleyans ?*
An interesting adjunct to the laundry show and
ironing festival in Melbourne was Buckley and Nunn’s
exhibit of the shirt making industry in full stitch, where-
by the utter absurdity of that ancient superstition, the
imported shirt, was intended to be brought right home to
the dullest Tory understanding. To see shirt-making
carried out on a B. and N. scale by a smart lot of girls—
undoubtedly smarter than the ordinary underpaid white-
workers of the Cold Country—is to be amazed at anti-
Australian prejudice which continues to provide a market
here for foreign starched-goods. Getting the material
direct from the looms under an easy tariff, the local
manufacturer can make for stock, or cut to order, a better
shirt than the higher-priced imported article. The
manager for “Buckley’s,” discussing the matter from a
retailer s point of view, reckons that the waning demand
for English-branded shirts deserves to be killed by a
prohibitive duty, even in the interests of the infatuated
purchasers, without regard to the interests of the local
factory. It employs a hundred or two hundred “hands”
to run a business that supports manufacturing industries
in which many hundreds of other “ hands ” are employed
and if the shirts and things made on the premises didn’t
command a profitable sale among the unprejudiced
majonty of the shops customers they wouldn’t be made at
all. This exhibit of linenery at the laundry show was an
appeal to the reasoning faculties of people who still
prefer foreign goods for their foreignness, and throw
money away in the indulgence of preposterously
unpatriotic fads.* * J

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
__. Sydney, September 26, 1905.
My Dear Arini, —
There’s something wrong with Australian
seasons. I want a Commission appointed to
lnquire-into the Inscrutable Ways of the Weather.
Ihe w inter was mislaid and spring acted as its
locum tenens and was a failure. Now, winter of a
most disagreeable kind has been found when it
wasn t wanted and spring is out of a job. The
owh for all his feathers, is a-cold-so what of the
poor bird in a transparent blouse ?
A recently divorced Sydney pair are showing
symptoms of remarrying each other. The fair
complexioned interloper still builds her hair, preci-
pice-fashion, above her forehead and is again in
bydney, but her name is now cinders so far as
concerns the man who forfeited so much for her
society. His wife is a legatee under a recent rich
wdl. The majority of men should marry—or re-
marry. By persistently remaining single a man
converts himself into a permanent public tempta-
tion. bo a notorious epigrammatist says. There
are some men who are not public temptations, but
they are few.
The Frank Gardners are still about Sydney and
were at some of the race meetings. The lady’s
appearance rather disappointed the rubber-necks.
Ihey expected someone “Frenchy-looking,” a
phrase that, as used by the majority, means comi-
cally operatic in get-up. Mrs. Gardner looks
a rather pored English gentlewoman. The
husband is crazily fond of his two-and-
a-half-year-old daughter. Whilst on the race-
course one day last week, a telegram informed
him that the child had cut a special kind of tooth,
or a tooth had cut her, or some such. Anyhow
the magnate fled off the course and up to
bunny Corner, near Bathurst, where his child and
her nurse were staying. (By-the-way, he has
mining interests also in that neighborhood.) Con-
cerning Carrie Swain : An Australian student in
Bans mentions in a letter to me that the clever
ex-artiste keeps up great style, and that she is
to-day apparently a more affluent person than
Jt rank Gardner himself.
Mr J. C. Williamson has bought the late rich
manbparkes mansion, “St. Monans,” in Eliza-
beth Bay, for £BOOO, and will go to live there
some tune next month. The impressario has now
three town houses on his mind. A mountain home
tvas sold some time since. In the few years he
owned it J.C.W. spent two days at that mountain
home. Ihe morning of his first and only visit he
was looking into the purple distances, and speak-
ing of the peace and restfulness of the atmo-
sphere, so far from telegrams, and ’phones, and
ink, and worry, when a boy on horseback
invaded the holy hush with fourteen mes-
sages that had arrived per wire, and a demand
for 14s. porterage. That sort of thing went on all
day till the messenger and the horse were worn
out, and for fear of having a dead boy on his
hands, and having to bury the horse, Mr.
Williamson fled back to the city* “ St* JVlonans ,5
where the rich old celibate lived and died—he
never travelled further than Sydney Heads—has
a water frontage, beautifully laid out grounds, and
the glass palm-house has the novelty of a
swimming bath with dressing rooms in its centre.
An auction sale of the late Mr, Sparke’s furniture,
marbles, and bronzes is going on to-day. The
fresco work on the ceiling of the dining-room
shows fruit-laden grape vines, with birds
anf . butterflies against a background of
rolling opalescent clouds. One of the curiosities of
the sale is the old-fashioned cedar bedstead, with
carved postsand velvet-brocade hangings, used by
its late owner to lie awake on while he wondered
what to do with all his wealth.
The Sparke domestic staff couldn’t have had a
very gay time. All had to be in by 10 o’clock,
and at that hour the gates were locked. To the
housekeeper, who had been with him for 23 years,
deceased left a life income of £250 a year. To his
butler “who had served him 14 years and had
never broken one article of all the valuable cut-
glass entrusted to his care ” he willed merely a
sum of £2OO. Yet surely he deserved more. An
every-day butler would have dropped that amount
on the floor and broken it inside two years.
Concerning those “Egyptian” tablets that the
archaeologist at Leland Stanford Uni. has hustled
out of the museum as unfit to lie under the same
glass case as other old cuneiform things: A
bydney doctor has photographs of some of them,
also he has two old Roman coins, one of which is
stamped with the head of Constantine. Coins
and tablets were, you may remember, sent on
from Melbourne to California. A Melbourne
man, who claims to be a Mahatma, had
these and scores of other such objects “sent”
to him. That is, they were brought by means or
forces not explainable to science—just poured on
the table when called for, as the ceiling might fall
on an ordinary individual. Mr. Stanford, the
late millionaire’s brother, who lives in Melbourne,
was a profound believer in Mahatma Guppy.
I hat s not his name, but the real one is just as
English and unromantic. Now it turns out that
the late Mrs. Leland Stanford’s visit to this
country was due to a desire to meet Guppy and
see his materialisations and collections of “sent”
coins (no gold ones amongst ’em) and billet doux,
written by Nebuchadnazzar in his salad days.
Curious thing that Americans—people who
have such a large allowance of common sense,
such a strong affinity for facts, and so great a
m arithmetic and tinned meat —are so
much given to spooks, Mahatmas and such soft
stuff as is fed to chickens. They couldn’t be easily
had over a deal in wheat or an oil-well
spurt, but mediums and Dowies have a walk over
them. (As to Dowie, I suppose we ought to feel
flattered that an Australian could take in so smart
a people.) Perhaps, like the old Athenians, the
Americans just like to run after what is new;
perhaps it’s a back swing of the pendulum—from
extreme hard-headedness to a slight softening of
what is inside the head. Certainly if the newness
does it, Guppy was new. At one seance he called
tor nsh, and a wet live bottle-nosed shark, wrapped
in sea weed, dripped on the table immediately. If
he had only demanded that Nicholas Willis be
produced on the instant!!
Miss Florence Young arrives in Sydney before
Ghristmas. She joins the Repertoire Co. Aus-
tralia is very glad that Florence and New York
didn t cleave to one another till death did them
part.
Cinnamon-complexioned soprano-voiced Fiorelli
will give a concert at Goulburn on Oct. 6. She
has a sentimental longing to sing in the town
where her mother was born. Captain Eden-
oorough the singer’s grandfather—was the origi-
nal owner of the station now in possession of Mr.
Harry Chisholm. Mr. Southwell will be pilot to
the little co., which will also include pianist
George Boyle and tenor George Grimm.
Violinist Stahl has acquired a young, spring-
chicken sleekness since he took to hen-farming.
In between the Chopin nocturnes he goes out to
catch eggs and pullets at his place at Belmore.
.Look at the back of his little cart any day, and
you 11 see beaks, feathered heads, and sinuous
necks poking out.
Horse-owner Stead, after the race had been to
his swift, took the four ladies who accompanied
mm and his daughter from Maoriland into
Hardy s the jewellers, and bediamonded them.
Miss Stead chose a diamond necklace—others
favored pendants. Spring and autumn race-
meetings are good seasons for the jewel-seller. A
horse wins and a woman is gemmed.
talking of jewellery reminds me of a funny
little circumstance. A partner in a well-known
Sydney firm was staying at Medlow Bath, and at
his wife s request sent some of her jewels to
Sydney to be put in his safe. “Give these to
onookcr, he said to the genius entrusted with
their care. Now, Snooker was one of his man-
agers, also Snooker had that day, or the day after
married a wife. “He was almost overwhelmed
by the magnitude of the wedding gift,” he said,
but he bore up bridegroomfully and presented the
sparklers to his bride !
Ethel Barrymore, the American actress, is to
marry an Englishman, one Captain Harry
Graham, an ex-guardsman and now private secre-
tary to Lord Rosebery. He has published a
book ot skittsh verse, “Misrepresentative Men.”

Lord Northcote’s newly-appointed A.D.C. is re-
la-tod to this author of clever conceits.
The Akgus MacKinnons (she’s a charming little
Scotswoman), who were at the Wentworth Hotel
for race-week, have gone back to Melbourne.
Ihey and the Septimus Millers “have taken the
whole house for the Autumn race-week.
The wife of a N.S.W. doctor was at Marienbad
during King Edward s last visit there, and she
writes that the King and the English press were
very indignant over the way the crowd jostled
®; n d stared at his Royal Highness. “ But ”
the Sydney woman writes, “if he did not want to
be stared at and chased, why did he dress in such
a remarkable way ? He wore a green Tyrolese
hat with a feather, a blue suit, tan boots, and a
flyaway red tie. I was one of the starers, but I
did not know till next morning that it was his
Majesty of England I was helping to annoy. I
thought it was the latest thing m musical comedv
potentates.” J
Next Saturday evening, the Symphony Orchestra
(Professional Musicians) will give a concert at the
Lyceum Theatre. Occasion will, in a sense be
specialised by the first appearance here of ’two
Queensland singers, who have come to reside in
Sydney and to enter its professional musical life
They are Misses May and Edith Collins. These
girls, who have sung considerably with Brisbane
Liedertafel, have uncommonly good contralto
vcnces. The elder will warble the Flower Song
{f° A m Faust,’aaM the younger Goddard’s
Angels Guard Thee. Later in the programme
the sisters will join m a duet with full orchestral
accompaniments.
Some day, when I get a nib that suits me I’ll
write a treatise on “Bird-lime.” It won't have
anything to do with ornithology or glue in a
literal sense. Man will be the “birds!” woman
the lime. Meantime I m marvelling over a ques-
tion that is on the rack in the “Women’s Page ”
of so many weekly papers-the question of how
li. ii \ w ° man “ ak ® herself attractive to men.
Shall she try to be like some woman in real life
who is confessedly attractive, or like some charmer
in a book ? Should she be silent and sympathetic,
or talky and entertaining ? Is she most
charming to the male when she? trXg
to make him think that he’s her god or
when shes cold and independent? If s he’s
better educated than he, hadn’t she better pre-
tend to be more ignorant than she is ? One
prophetess tells her girl readers that men do not
care for women who forsake the place “ that Pro-
vidence has assigned them in the home.” Another
asserts that men will make catherine-wheels of
admiration before the girl who refuses to be
dependent, and goes out to earn her own vittles
p and 'P ainte d muslins. The weeklies of
this Commonwealth are strong on these cribs
from Home Fakes, and such papers. Does
any paper ever get a rustle on itself and
tell men how to make themselves attractive
ai i ?i en ? fording to Coghlan and Dr
Arthur there are 80,000 more men than women in
Australia. These would surely be glad of hints
on hen-lime. As a matter of solemn fact, girls
are becoming curiously indifferent to marriage.
They are remaining single rather than marry
other than their accurate mate. But no girl with
a clever mother need remain single if she desires
to marry. A woman with a real turn for society
strategy can always manoeuvre a daughter into
matrimony, where the girl’s own efforts would
fail.
Sydney’s big red lamps are mostly alight
again after a short interval of extinguishment,
caused by the Adelaide Medical Congress. Drs.
Worrall, Chisholm Ross, Jarvie Hood, Arthur, J.
B. Nash, H. Marks, Foreman, Brady, Bowker,
Rennie, Armstrong, and Chisholm went down to the
city which is described by the wife of one of them as
giving an impression of “ clean prosperity.” Mrs.
Arthur, Mrs Bowker, and Mrs. Chisholm accom-
panied their husbands, and the whole medical
visiting crowd was nearly smothered under the
hospitality that would make it eat when it was
not hungry, drink when it had no drought, and
dance when its legs were already sagging with the
burden of much enjoyment.
Lady Northcote continues her good work of
booming Australian manufactures, and at latest
had ordered some special Australian design dinner-
ware from Bakewell s pottery, N.S.W. Since the
lady gave a hand to help Australian tweeds along,
Vicars, of Marrickville, is faced with the necessity
of having to either double his factory or give up
blanket-making, and confine hisattention to tweeds.
I hope he will do the former. I don’t see why,
having been a good Australian in an Australian
tweed all day, the citizen should have to turn in
and sleep between foreign blankets.
Mayor and Mayoress Allen Taylor, and Layton
of the Well Fitting Clothes, will pack their
candle-boxes and go to Melbourne for Cup week as
guests of Mayor and Mayoress Pleasance. Al*
ready, too, preparations are in pickle for the
evening party the Taylors will give the Jerseys
towards the end of November.
A marvellousiy beautiful picture—one that
would make you dream out loud —has been secured
by our Trustees of National Art Gallery. “On
the Roofs,” by C. Hoffbauer (a painter whose
name is unknown to Sydney artists), shows a night
phase of wealthy New York life. Two men and
two women, all young, are seated around a supper
table on a roof-garden. All around them from
countless windows of towering buildings rains the
subdued radiance of the electric light, deepening,
in the darker places of the picture, to a gem-like
pallor. One of the women billows with sun-
rayed white chiffon. Only the nape of her
beautiful neck shows, and her hair is
fair under a big black hat. Opposite her
muses the other woman in a misty-pink gown and
white feather hat. Both men, smoking cigarettes,
lean well back in their bamboo chairs. The com-
position gives the sensation of windless coolness
after a fiercely hot day. The “air ”of the figures
suggests lassitude, and one of the men has turned
his lean, clean-outlined face with a movement as
of conscious courtesy to some remark of the pink
woman’s. There is an entire absence of “ working-
up ” in the composition. Go close to it and the
texture of the canvas shows clear through the
figures faces. 1 hree little dabs of golden yellow
amt among the table appointments make, at
a distance, exquisitely indicated champagne
glasses. The silver and crystal on the table turn
jewel-like under the gleam and sparkle of the elec-
tnCf' ,- Yet ’ in spite of sky-scrapers and dynamo-
made lights, and indicated city smoke, the sense
of mystery, the rest and charm of night are pre-
served around the group.
Royal Art Society’s show curled its tail last
Saturday night. Then it went out of business for
another year. The “curl” was announced as a
Bohemian Night,” in aid of the Children’s Hos-
pi j V ne tj l6 Bohemians went collarless,
and they didn’t drink beer out of jam-tins or paper
_ So it was the new Bohemianism—not the
old land in shiftless boots, and uncertain pawn
tickets, and midnight revelry where the tallow
candle weeps in the bottle. The presence of
Chief Justicess Darley was a guarantee of the pro-
ceedings being kept up to Kosciusko’s chilly level.
Mrs. Harry Henderson (Miss Maggie Knight)
has started a school for dramatic training and
voice production in Sydney. Her husband, a
well-known Aucklander, was till lately manager
of a big shipping firm here. He fell into very bad
health and has gone to Vancouver. Miss Knight’s
long and successful stage career has well qualified
her to teach her art.
/-WAT I ®! interviewer who tries to get Miss Nance
O Neil on to thistle-down topics will labor in
vain. She does not look on life as being mostly
froth and bubble, and her mental storage has
nttle room for personalities. With a Why and
Whither expression in her blue eyes, she talks of
1 hingsand gropes among the stars, feeling after the
psychological instinct of Sudermann and Ibsen, the
dramaturgists of whom she is fondest. Pressed for
something autobiographical she rather reluctantly
shows views of her historic place on the Merrimac.
JLhe homestead is mentioned in one or more
American novels. There are two lakes on the
k.nd, and the house looks a great square palace,
borne of the_ views show the owner feeding the
deer or walking with the dogs or riding astride.
All looks as if the lady had struck oil. Then-
well, there’s “Magdalena,” the blue-eyed Persian
cat, a bad-tempered but favored beast, that the
tragedienne has with her at the Australia. Also,
there s the fact that Nance wears neither hair-
frame nor corsets.
Sydney trains and trams are covering their
nakedness with gum boughs and bunting in honor
of the Railway Jubilee. But not one bears
7 intimation that the people at the back
of the decorations owe eighty-three millions, and
that even the first shovel of earth that was
turned for the first railway 50 years ago is still
unpaid for. And how many generations of worms
must have fattened on that old, old security in the
interval!
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
On one of the New Hebrides, cats were
introduced to destroy the rats, which ate young
cocoanuts. The cats were afraid of the rats, but
destroyed all the birds.
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 25, 1905.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The State Exes, have been guesting at Winter-
Coolie’s extensive station. The squatter, who is a
widower, uses his wealth without fuss, and it was
only by the merest chance that the Talbottles,
happening to select Hamilton for a jaunt, found
him with a palm spread in greeting and hospi-
tality. What time Mr. W. Cooke lounged on the
cushions of the House of Reps., he employed a
monocle with which to inspect brother babblers—
an aid to the vision these same heartily disliked.
Miss Ivy Clarke had her final fling in short
frocks and flying locks, last week, when she hosted
it at a limited knickerbocker party. The damsel
is to be seriously ordained a grown-up in Cup-
weelc, which is a favored time for “coming
out.” So many people are “ out” at the end of a
Gee-Gee Carnival.
Mrs. Wesley Hall has been taking a little notice
again. The neat little widow isn’t making any
profuse display of her riches, so Toorak gets the
notion that she must suffer from dyspepsia or
something. Nothing of the kind. Mrs. Hall is a
robust little party, but the society whirligig
doesn’t attract her. That’s all about it.
Retired Judge Casey has the festive flag once
more braving the bottle and the lees on St. Hilda
esplanade, the pensioned Solomon having just
returned from Colombo. Within ten days of his
returning he put up a record of seven dinners and
lunches at his mansion, and still the table squeaks
(the modern table never groans) with cates set out
with hospitable intent.
Irish crochet is Miss Nellie Stewart’s latest fad.
Turquoises, cameos and diamonds are neglected
for the collection of crochet. A skilful worker has
just completed a dress in crochet for Nell. Mrs.
Brown-Potter, by the way, had a costly collection
of Irish crochets.
Rupert Clarke has been holding the top box at
the Princess’ of late in company with Julius
Knight who, by a merciful social law, can’t flaunt
his person in butcher blue tailorings of evenings.
Typhoid has added a slight air of pensiveness to
Knight’s heaviest posings.
Since Mummer Osborne took Miss Maud Jef-
fries in marriage to his clan, they have been
indulging in much praise and pretty avowals
of delight. An actress in a family of much
sheep and sinew is such a novelty. Even
the Maddens, distantly related, are" gushful.
Mrs. Osborne takes all the fuss with a distrait air
and a touch of tired unconcern. Her accustomed
outdoor pallor gets never a touch of powder or
rose-dab. This undiluted, blameless, refined
pallor is admired as a courageous and noble art of
beauty in itself. The lady who stars as a beauty
on the boards is deemed brave to show up in the
fierce light of day minus the dabs that delude.
Many a sweet frank girleen has gone over to false
eyebrows and a moveable glow of cream and roses
just because her friends dubbed her “beauty,”
and she felt she had to try and live up to the
reputation even if she had to be a Chemical Flora
to do it.
After her Philharmonic tootle next month Miss
Eileen Castles is to keep her rather metallic voice
in pickle till the end of this financial year, when
she takes up her travelling kit to trip it with the
Royal Comics. This third aspirant of the boomed
family is a somewhat lighter edition of sister Amy
as to appearance and manner, and, of course, she
is more stage-struck. Amy, the founder of the
family, is likely to be the last to arrive on the
operatic stage. The eldest brother, now in the
Agent-General’s office in Fogtown, also itches, ’tis
understood, to be footing it in the Williamson
ranks, and expects a call in due course.
A Holy City bachelor who runs a motor and
skates like _an ice-nymph is regarded as the
accepted suitor of a coy little theatrical star,
fairly new to the footlights and the grease.
Miss Vida Goldstein, the other night, ran a
corroboree at the Austral Salon, to which all the
ladies of the Political Association flocked in
assorted clothes. Senator Dobson and Rep.
Hughes furnished the pros and cons of a Union
Label debate, Miss Vida as referee. The Tas-
manian Senator overstepped the time limit, and
the perky referee promptly pulled him up and de-
livered him over to the caustic tongue of Hughes.
Another globe-trotter returns with the verdict
that Australian theatrical shows are up to any,
and past many, put on in the more important
London play-houses. The theatre-goer in ques-
tion saw “ The Cingalee ” in the Fog City, and he
prefers the Australian production, principally
owing to the melodious chorus. The voices ot
our cherubim, he says, sound ever fresh, hopeful,
and joyous. “ There is an exuberance in the very
quality of the voices of a comic opera chorus this
side that is not to be found in even Italy, Ger-
many, or France ! ”
While on voices—Miss Mary Conly, the comely
soprano who warbled herself into matrimony and
travel, has been spending three years in close
study under the evergreen Madame Minna
Fischer. No cables have loomed in the press
anent the triumphs of this little Mary, but recent
files disclose the fact that at several concerts in or
about the City of Fog, Miss Conly was pro-
grammed by the side of Ada Crossley, and praise
was apportioned both in equal terms. I saw one
criticism that spread most of the marmalade over
Miss Conly. yhe is the matronly-figured young
Melbournienne of whom it was said at the time of
the first Castles boom that she was better worth
booming than the lucky one.
Managers Tait have had some troubles with the

The enthusiasm that has been moving Madame
Maggie Stirling to little hysterical outbreaks of
laughter and sob-strugglings culminated at the
Camberwell home-coming, when she fell on the
unprepared shoulder of a short young local
violinist and pressed a motherly, or elder-sisterly,
kiss on his top curl—-a modest little curl that had
never been kissed before.
Miss Stirling (whom everyone talks tenderly of
as “ Maggie”) appeared for her introductory con-
cert at the Town Hall in fine oyster raiment, with
a slip-panel down the front of crystal splinters
that illumined the rest of her. More of the splin-
ters, in glistening heaps, bordered the corsage,
and were as a foil to the high and heavy white
shoulders. The Dowager of Cliveden sat straight
up in the forward pew, with the large Stirling
bairns on either side of her own lambs —a spectacle
which evoked much sympathetic approval.
Miss Ethel Sinclair has grown plump since she
sailed off from this port with the Clarke scholar-
ship, her aspirations and her sweet-toned fiddle.
She made a re-appearance at the Stirling concert,
and was warmly received. But her style is not
that of a captivating gadder or artful traveller.
She is simply stout and energetic, with brain
to back her fiddling and a saving dash of tem-
perament.
There was a big roll-up to Toorak House last
week for the Show of Stitchery, set going for the
impaired gentlewomen by Mrs. Edward Miller.
Folks went in party rig and carrying chatelaine
purses for emergency purposes. Sovereigns
passed hands briskly as soon as the Work
Association delegates started to deal. Before
the golden noon was far advanced all the
leading exhibits were labelled “ Sold.” Mrs.
Miller took a regal view of the bazaar havoc
in her blessed mansion, garbed in committee
black, the glace of which broke into a lace diver-
sion here and there with encouraging effect. One
of the most summery of smart frockers among the
guests was the red-haired Miss Brodribb, in cream
with one of the approved tag-and-tear silk boas.
An Adelaide correspondent writes :
The W.C.T.U. is having its annual useless pow-wow in
the Holy City. The Union is divided into as many de-
partments as there are mortal sins. During the year Mrs.
Stiggins prowls round with a oat’s-eye lantern, and the
“bloke "who tells sweet nothings to his Sal in the city
parks is disturbed by the aforesaid glimmer on Saturday
nights. She invades bars, and thrusts a pile of uninterest-
ing literature between the thirsty man and his beer.
Annually there is a great corroboree. The head of the
“ Morals ” branch retails her record of timely interrup-
tions, and the chiefs of the various departments recite
their deeds of squalor. These people make a plain citizen
tired and ill.
Yiolet-picking has been a midwinter amuse-
ment. And it’s not quite done yet. There’s a
nursery out Malvern way boasting a boundless
outlook of violet beds. For a small church coin,
one gathers as many blossoms as is possible with-
out contracting a backache.
Claude Bantock relieves the toilsomeness of
looking funny in comic opera by bantam raising.
He’s a fowl-fancier of renown and understood
his fame as a chicken-hatcher, the other night,
when a prize bantam was handed up to him over
the trombone man, in “ The Cingalee.” But surely
there should be a veto on this sort of thin,
aggressive nonsense. Otherwise the action of the
drama will be presently delayed whilst Lauri is
presented with a milch cow, or a tame crocodile, or
a real joke, or some other strange animal. I
know a man, by the way, who speaks feelingly
of these things. Years ago, when he was young
and harmless, some villainous friends banquetted
him and gave him the best part of an alleged
gold mine in scrip. When the 14th call happened
he realised that it was the only way they could
get rid of a dead investment—a very dead one
indeed.
From the way the massage parlors and Turkish
baths are being patronised, it seems that the local
she intends to be quite clean for the summer. “Let
me be clean ” ! is a common male cry, but till re-
cently it hasn’t been echoed with enthusiasm by the
second sex. The rush to the Turkish t’other day
was more than the attendants could cope with, al-
though the baths are available for women every day
of the week. In the hot-house of highest tem-
perature simmering Venuses, run to flesh, prome-
naded, recounting to one another the story of
their gradual avoirdupois, and swapping anti-
fat diets. Between the steady showers of perspi-
ration, other elderly Junos exchanged family
histories and maternal sighs.
The Norman Bayles have come back, covered
with dust and glory, from a motor-spin through
the other States. It hasn’t yet transpired
whether the beauteous Bayles conveyed any
specially-canned cornflowers on the tour to keep
up a supply that would furnish him with a fresh
buttonhole at every port of call. Hardly possible
that he went for more than one day without his
blue blossom.
The Dante Society is agitated now to fever
point over the coming of an Italian Princelet in
early November. But his nibs comes for the Cup
carnival, and it will probably be “his troubles”
about Dante.
Eloquent silence, or the brief word that has a
more biting snap, are the only comments passed
upon the arrangements of a Federal dame, who
divided her friends into two sections, feasting the
better half one day, and the lesser on another
occasion. The lists are being discussed, and those
set down in the second-hand lot snort violently.
The recent show of Purr, Yelp and Twitter
suggests that the cult of pussie is of a rather
plebeian order, that doggie is a fashionable
craze, and that the feathered chirper is the coming
pet. The Stonington dame’s whistling exercises
at the cage of Pretty Joey have set the suburban
Miss tuning up her surplus breath to do likewise,
and she is now practising on the chooky-hen with
great success.
Curio-dealers and crest-gatherers have lately been
up on the door-mat of the Grand waiting on P. S.
Mitchell, the interesting bachelor of the Table
Top squattocracy. He has some fine curios,
among them miniatures dating back pretty well
to the Flood, or the megatharium, or some other
old time. Rare ivories are another strong feature
of his treasures.
The second annual street collection in Adelaide
for the Children’s Hospital brought in £555
—£s2 less than last year. The wife of Chief
Justice Way (with a fine “pitch” at the corner
of King William and Rundle streets) was top-
scorer with £5B; a fashionable doctor’s wife
second with £5O ; and the Mayoress next with
£49. One £5 note was taken, and £75 in gold and
paper, but most people simply laid in a stock of
small change for the occasion. There were 9000
threepennies garnered in, and a staggering weight
of 12,409 pennies; also, 1816 frugal souls got
down to halfpence. There were no farthings.
Hebrewdom in Melbourne gathered at the
Bourke-street Synagogue, last week, to consecrate
anew the building, defiled by the rude hand of
the Board of Health. Feminine Jewry, perched
in the galleries outside the congregation, showed
a gallant display of furs and diamonds. One
Chosen daughter, who hung over the clock in
a rakish black hat, had planted “just there”
a diamond gaud that caught a ray from every
gas-jet, and the jets number 120. Melbourne
Jewesses are pretty, as a rule—some of them very
pretty. Mayor Pleasance sat in a place of honor,
trying to look comfortable with his hat on in
church. (That Jewish custom is a good one ; with-
out it the proscribed sex would see from the gallery
only a published surface of bald pate.) Chief-Secre-
tary Sammy Gillott pulled his velvet collar round
his ears and looked the most venerable Abraham
of them all. He gladdened the heart of Israel
with a gift of 10 guineas. Little Dr. Maloney
came late in a slouch hat, and slid into a vacant
seat beside Alderman Pratt, M.L.C., who remained
as starched a Tory as was possible under the circum-
stances. The consecration business was a mysterious
ceremony that looked like one of Prof. Spencer’s
Centralian biograph-films. Rabbi Abrahams
headed a procession of nine Jewish gentlemen,
who marched round the church seven times, carry-
ing sacred totems wrapped in calico bags. At the
same time a fresh and powerful choir made
melodious remarks in Hebrew. Handsome
Miss Schlam sang a sweet sacred solo later
on. After more ceremonies in Old-Testament
language, Rabbi Abrahams mounted the white
stone steps of the Ark, and Gentiles waited
anxiously to see him slaughter a goat, or a
bullock, or some other animal. But it seems
that business is long out of date. The
daughters of Israel in the gallery did not
waste their glances on plump, big-voiced Father
Abrahams. Rachel and Leah (as of yore) are now
talking of an unmarried stranger from a far country
—the new priest who began his duties in a seaside
suburb only a week ago. He intoned one of the
prayers at the consecration, and at every third
word his large, dark eye was raised, to send a
thrill through the gallery.
A pack of fashionables saw the start of “ His
Majesty’s Servant” on Saturday, and used their
glasses with feverish anxiety on the convalescent
mummer of the legs. He showed a close cut of
thatch which caused the thought of the senti-
mentally - mashed to hark back pensively
to the time when Julius’ pulse was alarming.
There were four acts in the piece, and at
every curtain-fall Julius was bellowed for and
appeared several times to bow his shaven head to
the gushers, most of whom were of the she-variety.
Miss Jeffries had only to look the Puritan Maid,
and she looked it in two or three changes of
raiment, that of pearl satin being of most
picturesque account. Miss Florence Hamer
displayed a great depth of chest and a col-
lection of light little liquorice ringlets in her
role as semi-royal villainess, also some gems
of dazzlesome quality. Her nervousness took the
edge off half her devilry. The mummer herself
admits to being the most nervous subject that
ever followed the game. She tells how her knees
collide in trembly stage moments, and her hand
has to clutch some of the scenery to save her
words knocking each other out of all recognition.
The rotund Thomas Bent had a look in at “ The
J.P.” on the farewell night, and shook his side of
the circle with his rolling laughter every time the
giddy honorary magistrate bumped down the stairs,
in a drunken somnambulist act, or was cold-
shouldered by the cheeky little widow of his affec-
tions. Thomas Bent likes his farce with the sauce
thick, and regards it with his working specs,
lodged high on his bump of candor.
A little dazzler of Melbourne origin but
Harborville court interest makes her first appear-
ance before Princess’ eyes in the ranks of “ His
Majesty’s Servant.” Though rather small-
featured for a great effect, she shines as the
pick of the Court beauties.
Mrs. M'Cracken, who died last week, was one
of the great old identities of early Flemington.
Her fine residence looks across from a belt of elms
at the equally fine eyrie of the Maddens, and both
properties represent big interest and big wealth.
The old lady enjoyed an income of £BOOO a year,
and lived a rather strenuous life to the last. Out
of ten or eleven children, only one survived —
Alec, the sport and brewer, who is one of the big
property men of the Glenroy and Essendon dis-
tricts.
That kilted curiosity—Jacobite Napier—who for
years was a familiar figure in this State, is on his
way back to Melbourne after a period of resi-
dence on his native heath. He brings an eligible
daughter from Scotland, and will look once more
to his Collins-street property with a purely busi-
ness eye. The inveterate Scot doesn’t allow his
vast money interests to suffer from the propaganda
of the Fiery Cross, or the declaration of Mary the
Somethingth as the real monarch of Britain.

A N.S.W. Equity Court case heard the
other day ought to make several people uncom-
fortable. It was another of Tom Slattery’s little
affairs. While acting as solicitor and agent for
Mrs. Scanlon in the investment of her Tyson
money, Slattery had accepted £2OO from one Kin-
nane, who sold five Rand wick houses through him.
Now Mrs. Scanlon sued Kinnane to get the money
back and to have the sale-contract rescinded ; and
the Chief Judge in Equity made the decree asked
for, though Mrs. Scanlon is to get back not £lO,OOO
but £9ooo—the odd £lOOO being apparently some
sort of compensation to Kinnane for standing out
of the chance of selling his property during the
five years since the Scanlon sale was made. It
wasn’t, in spite of this decree, a particularly bad
case either. In fact, the Court said that Kinnane
believed Mrs. Scanlon agreed to Slattery taking
his money. If the Court rescinds a contract made
under those circumstances, there shouldn’t be
much doubt as to what it would do in a case where
the second commission was admittedly a secret
affair—a bribe, in fact, given by the seller to the
man whom the buyer was paying for looking after
his interests. And if the seller could afford to pay
the buyer’s agent something, it proves that he
could afford to sell for less. But the morals of the
thing haven’t been in question all along ; the inte-
resting fact at present is that the Equity Court
has made an appearance at last in defence of
morality, and has fallen heavily on the secret com-
mission by squelching the bargain of the man who
paid it.
Tommy Bent and his Ministry were only

Sydney Telegraph, in its new “Ladies’
Page, ” prints this from a London paper :
Australia depends on money borrowed from England,
and the interest of that money is almost all spent in Eng-
land. The D’Arcys and the Wilsons do not spend their
money in Queensland and New South Wales, but in
Grosvenor-square. No country can thrive under such
conditions. No colony, in fact, has ever come to much,
or ever will do so.
When the Telegraph prints things like this,
what prevents it advocating forthwith the obvious
remedy—an absentee-tax ?
“T.D.” : However the monopolist English
press, and the syndicated Australian variety,
struggle to conceal the fact, there is a growing
uneasiness in the minds of British statesmen and
leaders of political thought as to the wisdom of
helping the Monkey to swing on the top branch.
I have lately been in the way of meeting a good
many front-rank politicians and writers, and the
subject of Australia has inevitably brought them
round to the prospects of that far-away land when
the Jap has quite finished with the Bear, and has
sufficiently rested himself to take on some more
fight. This fact in itself shows what fears are
shaping themselves, and even the more Tory
minds are willing to admit that the question of
Jap interference with British interests somewhere
in the Pacific is bound to crop up. And then
Australia will have to look out. As a former
junior member of a British Cabinet remarked to
me the other day, “ You have, of course, an
essentially Japanese industry in the pearl-shelling,
and that fact alone might at any time provide an
excuse for a conflict, which is really sought for
other reasons entirely. Of course I’m not thinking
of to-day or to-morrow, but it doesn’t take many
years or many months to alter an international
position, and the more Japanese you have in
Australia the bigger chance there will be of
eventual trouble. By all means keep them out,
and by doing so you’ll probably be saving some
future British Government a devil of a lot of
trouble.”
“Pot” on the subject of Bent, Boyd,
bricks, coal, locomotives, &c. :
I am not an admirer of Victoria’s Premier Bent, yet I
cannot but sympathise with that curious compound of
humanity at times. Bent has ideas of starting State
brick works. Boyd and others of that ilk condemn thi»
as an interference with “private enterprise.” Boyd wants
his coal used on the State railways to encourage “ private
enterprise.” If Bent proposed to run his own State coal
mine Boyd and Co. would again cry out like a cat on the
roof that “private enterprise” was being grievously
molested. The Employers’ Federation (which includes
the aforesaid Boyd), in its annual report (Argus, 22/8/‘O5),
denounces the State brick works as being a means of provid-
ing “ Government stroke ” billets. The Royal Commission
report on locomotive construction at the Phoenix Foundry
and the Newport State workshops finds the cost of
making all engines to be £477 less at the Government
shops, of which saving half (£238) represents economy in
wages. Now if Boyd and other employers are honest in
their objection to Bent interfering with “ private enter-
prise,” why do they let their annual report say that the
“Government-stroke” brickworks are objectionable?
Their “ private stroke” should soon beat the other kind.
And what on earth is wrong with the private stroke in
loco.-building that it can’t keep up with the State stroke?
And if the State workshops can save £lB,OOO or £20,000 in
building £150,000 worth of locomotives, there is a danger
to Boyd that it may try if it can’t make a similar saving
by running its own pits to provide coal for the engines
when built. And then it may cease to subsidise Boyd’s
“ private enterprise,” which isn’t really private, being
subsidised. Then Boyd will probably take a fit, and it
w'ould be just like him to go and have his fit in Bent’s
electorate.
The Chinese love of a flutter was more
than satisfied by the cheerful gamble between
Lionel Terry and Chun Yung, in Haining-street,
Wellington (Maoriland), last Sunday night.
Chun didn’t know he was gambling for his
life when he stepped forth into the smellful
thoroughfare, and his luck was right out.
Lionel Terry, a young man from Eton and Oxford,
had travelled and seen the evil wrought in every
part of the wide British Empire by the inflow of
the colored alien. He had brooded over the
matter, and written a book, entitled “ The
Shadow of the Empire,’’ in which he strove to
arouse his fellow white Britons to the
danger of the taint. But the Briton didn’t
seem to get any wiser, and Terry, who had
become, apparently, a monomaniac, decided that
something drastic must be done to arouse public
interest in the matter. So he wrote to M.L.
Governor Plunket that in order to draw attention
t) the evil he had determined to kill the first China-
man he met in Haining-street on Sunday night. He
had no personal malice against any colored brother,
and didn’t single out any individual. He left it
to the Fates to decide who should die for the
Empire’s good, and Chun Yung walked along,
and was promptly shot. Then Terry went home,
had supper, passed a good night, and after break-
fast next morning called round and gave himself
up. Now his book will probably sell like ripe
cherries.

“ There are two anti-Australian parties in
Australia—the Stinking Fish party and the Dirty
Bird party. The Opposition belongs to them
both.”— Premier Morgan in the Q. Assembly.
The exact words which Wi Pere, the Maori
M. used in finishing his great speech in the
Barliament at Wellington were “Ko to helle te
l’akeha.” He said this twice, and then smote the
interpreter on the nose and stalked out.
When a S. Australian civil servant was
promoted from the Audit to the Attorney-Gene-
ral's Office, the other day, the public learnt that
his new salary would be £2OO per year. It also
heard that lie had been in the service 23 years.
Good old John Stinson writes to the
Sydney papers proposing that £5,000,000 should be
borrowed to open up the North Coast districts of
N. Is there any blessed “reformer” in all
N.S.W. who doesn’t want to begin his reform by
floating another big loan?
Sudden Solomon is moving in the S.A.
Assembly to put the Northern Territory under
oiler to the Federation. The offer should be
made, and promptly accepted. With the N.T. in
bis locker, Prime Minister Deakin could talk
immigration far more freely than now.

dropped and the tariff duty, to come into effect on
the establishment of the industry, substituted.

“ W.” wades in to spoil another fellow’s
joke with dreary particulars :
In Tiib Bulletin (14/9/’O5), in a paragraph concerning
Speaker Coles of the S.A. Assembly, you remark that he
“ adjusted his blown horse hair.” Be it known unto you
fehat the S.A. Speaker does not wear a wig. Neither is
there a mace nor any functionary in knee breeches to
hang round and look pretty connected with the
Assembly. There is no legislative body in the Common-
wealth which has less “pomp and ceremony ” about it.
William M‘Millan, importer, the gentle-
man who stayed in the Australian Parliament till
ha had got the tariff fixed up, as far as possible, to
destroy Australian industries for the benefit of the
importing business, and then resigned and left the
country to its fate, lately addressed a meeting at
Manilla (N.S.W.), and, according to the daily
Eaper report, “ spoke disparagingly of those mem-
ers who entered Parliament with the. object of
self-elevation.”
It is a rather re-
markable circumstance
that Canada, which
hardly produces any
silver, is allowed to
mint its own silver
coin, to retain the
profits, and even to
repudiate the responsi-
bility of keeping its
own silver coinage in
repair; while Australia,
which is a vast silver-
producing country,
hasn’t managed even
to get the right of
coining 6d. for itself.
Also, A ustralia pays
£200,000 a year towards
the British navy and is
only reviled in Eng-
land for its pains,
while Canada pays
nothing, gets a far
better defence, and is
almost grovelled to by
the British person.
The Owe’Sullivan
built a railway from
Culcairn toGermanton
(N.S.W.), and occasion-
ally it is found more or
less worth while to run
a train along it. The
Australian Govern-
ment wanted the train
to carry the mails ; but
the State asked such a
price that the Aus-
tralian Government
hesitated, and while it
hesitated a private per-
son j umped in with an
offer to carry the mails
oftener, and as rapidly,
for about half the
money; and so the
train crawls along three
times a week, and a
coach whips up beside
the track every day,
and often does the dis-
tance in as good time,
and sometimes better. If there hadn’t been a
coach handy the Post-office would have had to pay
the exorbitant price demanded by the State ; and
then when the figures were published, the State,
in the person of J. Carruthers, would have made
crushing remarks about Federal extravagance.
The S.A. Legislative Council is girding up
its expensive loins to throw out the Price Govern-
ment’s progressive land-tax scheme. But it is in
an awkward position, for if it rejects the pro-
posed graduated tax, which only charges |d. in
the £ up to £5OOO, it will be necessary to per-
petuate the existing rate, which is Id. right
through. Then the man with £5OOO worth of land
will be bitterly angry at losing the chance to get
rid of half his tax, and the “ producer” will want
to throw the hard, bitter turnip of execration at
his M.L.C.
Senator Playford’s statement that the
Government “wanted to get a High Commis-
sioner, but they did not know whether they could
succeed,” has given rise to a good deal of discus-
sion among Federal members He could hardly
have intended to suggest any real difficulty in
securing a Commissioner, considering the number
of candidates in the field. One idea is that the
Government is pledged to Bigjohn Forrest, and is
prepared to drop the measure sooner than yield to
the opposition freely expressed re his appoint-
ment. If that is so, better no High Commissioner
than Bigjohn Forrest, but The Bulletin can’t
credit the yarn.
Some important matters crop up when
men are making laws for the government of a
country. The other day, in the Tasmanian
Assembly, it was decided that a Bill should be
brought in for the protection of the homing
pigeon. It seems as though it would be more
profitable and practical to talk about the reasons
why Tasmania’s population is decreasing, and
why departures from the island have exceeded
arrivals pretty consistently for the last 45 years,
than to discuss Bills for the protection of pigeons
or the establishment of homes for superannuated
canaries—Bills which will probably be thrown
out, in any case, when sent to the Loft or House
of Dodery, where those who ought to be angels
roost.
The mentally-unwieldy Landlords of
Victoria put up a great fight over the sacred rights
of property, which they chose to imagine the
State had attacked by ensuring—by the Water
Bill —its right touse its own water. Metaphorically
they got them into the last ditch and wallowed
heavily and pounded the mire of illogical argument
with much puffing and spluttering. Finally
Manifold quoted scripture—“ Cursed be he who
removes his neighbor’s landmark,” but Attorney
General Davies was full weary of obstruction by
this time and flung back the retort, “ But Moses
commanded a redistribution of the land every 50
years,” and there was no more scripture.
Sample of debate from the House of
Representatives :
G. Wilson (speaking on the Commerce Bill) : “ The Bill
is not an effective one.”
Webster : “ In what is it defective ? ”
Spence : “ Hear, hear 1 ”
Webster : “ Show us the defects.”
Spence : “ Hear, hear.”
G. Wilson (with the air of a pompous parrot): “It is
the business of the Government to put before the House
an effective Bill.”
Spence : “ Just so 1 just so ! ”
Webster : “ Hear, hear ! ”
It is a very simple job to be a great statesman
when you come to think of it.
The High Court has had a fling at the
land laws of N.S.W., and has despairingly
declared, by way of obiter dicta, that “it is im-
possible to construe them ; you have to find your
way through them.” And a thorny way it is.
The N.S.W. land enactments have grown up like
a blackfellows’ camp, higgledy-piggledy. Every
Ministry has had a member anxious to leave his
footprint on the dust of Time (and, incidentally,
to secure his seat; and, in some cases, to secure
an even more tangible reward) by passing “a
comprehensive Land Act ” ; and the final result
is that the land law is so comprehensive as to be
incomprehensible. Yet it would be quite a
feasible thing to codify all the conflicting and con-
current statutes into one Land Act, and ask
Parliament to ratify that and sweep all the old
lumber away. But there is only hard work and
no prospect of profit or credit in such a course.
Therefore it isn’t adopted.
POLITICAL PATRIOTISM.
In re some recent criticisms by Australian Parliamen-
tarians of Australian people and Australian laws.
AUSTRALIA : “At least I might he spared this
kind of thing at the hands of my own servants
in my own House.”
Sydney D.T., Sept. 25 :
A Temporary Supply Bill on Tuesday in the House of
Representatives will afford an opportunity for some
general criticism. . . . The House has had sufficient en-
lightenment now on the slipshod methods in which the
Government’s business is prepared to make any schemes
for the restricting of debate and suppressing criticism im-
possible. .
Thus is the wild Wilks, with the flowing Smiths
(Pumpkin and Bruce), and the clamorous Conroy,
and Kelly and co., urged to get up and obstruct.
One great trouble and permanent misery
of the Australian Government lies in the fact that
it is hardly possible for it to propose a public work
in any State without members from at least two
other States objecting because the work isn’t in
their State, and, as every work must be in some
State, it is difficult to do anything without bitter
parochial howls arising. It is hard to imagine
anything that is more likely to pay 20 per cent,
on its cost than the proposed telephone from Mel-
bourne to Sydney, yet even that roused Senator
Matheson, of Westralia, to complaints about
“expensive luxuries” ; and he found three other
aggravated persons in the Senate to support him.
Nearly a century ago a N.S.W. bench of
magistrates struggled with
the trading difficulty that is
at the bottom of the present
union-label controversy, and
solved it in a way. Union-
ism, as we now know it,
didn’t exist then, but there
was roguery as we now
know it—light weight, adul-
teration, and so forth. The
magistrates decided that the
best way to make sure of
catching the fraudulent
maker—the man originally
responsible was to force
him to put his label on each
article. The following is the
regulation they drew up in
regard to bread :
That each and every baker shall mark each and every
loaf he exposes for sale with the initials of his or their
respective names.
That regulation was signed by Ellis Bent,
Alexander Riley, D’Arcy Wentworth and S.
Ford, and was approved by Governor Macquarie
in June, 1810.
Queensland has constructed a railway to
the imaginary line which divides Banana]and
from N.S.W., and the work comes to a sudden
stop a few hundred yards from the Tweed River.
Permission has been asked from Carruthers to
make a short extension at Queensland’s cost, in
order to save bother and expense to the
N.S.W. dwellers on the river in hauling them-
selves and their goods from the water to the rail-
way. But Carruthers, who insists that the Tweed
River trade must be dragged all the way to Syd-
ney, has refused. An extension of this line would
greatly enrich the N.S.W. people up North by
giving them access to a market close at hand, but
it wouldn’t benefit the traders in far-off Sydney,
and trade must be dragged to Sydney even if it
should burst on the road.
President Moore, at the annual meeting of
the N.S.W. Employers’ Federation, cursed the
Arbitration law, and said that what they wanted
was for “ masters and men ” to come together and
put the settlement of their industrial troubles on a
“solid foundation.” Well, haven’t they? How
much more solid than the law of the land do they
want the foundations to be ? Or do they want the
foundations so solid that one party or the other
can slip away when it reckons that it can get
better terms by fighting than by staying and
arguing—as the Broken Hill mining co. ’s did 10
years ago when they tore up the “ solid ’’ agree-
ment, and told the men that if they wanted any-
thing they could fight for it ? The beauty of that
sort of “ solid foundation ” is that it’s there when
it isn’t wanted, and it isn’t there when it is wanted.
Henniker Heaton, the rich Australian
absentee, has again been circularising this country
about the desirability of a penny post between
Australia and the land where Henniker spends
the money that Australia supplies him with, and
where he hopes some day to get a title and become
Lord ’Enniker. But the loss to Australia, if it
complied with Henniker’s request and supplied
Henniker with his desired advertisement, would
be about £250,000 a year. The said £250,000 a
year would be much better spent in paying off the
3| per cent, bonds which bind us to England than
in forging the new bond of a penny post. As a
matter of fact, Australia wants less communica-
tion with England rather than more. This country
is in much greater need of manufactures of its
own than of a penny post to enable it to order
tweed and chiffons from J. Bull.
The Victorian Cabinet nearly split the
other day. Minister Swinburne was pushing
through his Milk Supervision Bill, under which
those local bodies which have persistently refused
to carry out the law, by keeping dairies clean and
by prosecuting people who poison the public with
dirty and diseased milk, are to have the power of
conniving at wholesale murder taken out of their
hands. Irving and Livingston proposed as an
amendment that they should have another chance
to wink at wholesale murder so long as they
“promised to reform.” Swinburne said “No”
with the emphasis of a long thin man who meant
business. Bent said “Yes” with the hesitancy
of a short thick man who was half-persuaded.
Then there was a hurried adjournment of the
debate. When the Bill came up again it proved
that the cause of Bent, Irvine, Livingston and
Dirt had gone down badly after a fierce private
discussion, and Swinburne was on top.
The S. Australian Railway Commissioner
gave away another part of the show in his annual
report, issued the other day. The net revenue was
said to be £536,530, or £3 19s. per cent, on capital,
“ which means that, after paying working expenses
and interest on capital, £67,800 was carried to the
credit of profit and loss”-—and that surplus was
promptly collared by the Treasurer to make his
surplus with. Then the Commissioner cheerfully
knocked out both surpluses with this remark: —
I regret some portion of this sum was not appropriated
in the further reduction of the amount due to capital for
condemned railway stock.
In other words, the balance-sheet is faked, con-
demned rolling-stock being included in the assets;
and if the honest thing were done, and the rail-
ways were charged with the things they wore
out, then there wouldn’t be any such railway
surplus at all, and the Treasurer wouldn’t
have a surplus either, and the need for the pro-
proposed heavier land-tax would be greater than
ever. It’s a shocking thing to reflect that all S.
Australia has to show for its alleged surplus is
several cwt. of rust and numerous tons of rotten
timber.
The N.S.W. Government has at last fixed
up a contract for the supply of locally-produced
steel and iron. It will cost a little more than the
imported article, but Carrutbers reckons the
extra cost is fully justified because—
This means the establishment of the largest manufac-
turing industry in the whole of Australia. The complete
figures will be submitted to Parliament in a few days by
Mr. Lee. Taking the prices of the last five years, with
freight included, the tender works out an increase in the
rates to the extent of between 6 and 7 per cent, but
against that increase there are many good relative
advantages. The chief one is that there will be more
work for the railways in the carriage of raw material to
the place of manufacture, and the freight on that alone
will amount to about £140,000 per annum. Of course
that will not all be profit, but a portion of that is the
profit to the railways, over and above working expenses.
It also means more employment on the lines. Perhaps
too optimistic statements were put forward some time
ago as to the effect of the establishment of this industry
in the direction of employment. It has been said that
work would be found for 11,000 men, but I don’t think
the industry will give employment to so large a number.
It will, however, undoubtedly mean work for thousands
of persons, directly or indirectly.
All of which is very good Protectionist argument,
though what a Freetrader like Carruthers is doing
with it heaven only knows.
THE DISUNITED UNION.
The State Premiers are said to be uniting to oppose the
appointment of a Federal High Commissioner in London.
Most of them want to retain six Agents-General as at
present.
The National Ass in S. A. shows some sign
of weakening in its antagonism to an extended
franchise for the Legislative Council, and at the
recent annual meeting the platform was modified
and the “no surrender” flag hauled down. Ex-
Lands Minister “Turncoat Bill” Copley suggested
an educational qualification, but blatant leaguers
protested that ‘ ‘ parsons and school teachers are
the worst enemies of the propertied classes ” and
that some of the worst Socialists “ are highly edu-
cated men.” So the intelligence test was promptly
rejected. The Ass doesn't want intelligence ; it
wants bray.

“ Yarrum ” : JRe drink, and the craving for
it. T’other Saturday at Catherine Hill Bay, I
met a young miner whom I’d known at Mt.
Kembla. He was stepping out for the pit for all
he was worth, although there was no work there.
Had had a drunk on pay-night (Friday), and he
explained that he was anxious to get into the pit,
and get a sniff of it to kill the craving for the
“ cursed derrink ’’ that was gnawing at his vitals.
He hadn’t the price of two drinks about him, so
we went in together. Since then an old miner
told me that there is “something in the atmo-
sphere below that stifles the desire for liquor and
steadies the nerves,” and acts on the system same
as a pick-me-up.
‘ ‘ Stony ” : There is in Hobart one place
at least where the traveller can put up, at hotel
rates, if he doesn’t care to go to a hotel. But,
unlike the hotels, this house provides an extra
which doesn’t appear in the bill. A Melbourne
man slept there one night, and, during the even-
ing, among the soap and candles and towels and
things in his 6xß, he came across a Bible, which he
supposed had been left behind by the previous
occupier. After breakfast, next day, when about
to leave the palace, he took the book downstairs
and handed it over the office-counter and ex-
plained. The manager regarded him for a moment
with a grieved and reproving eye, and then said,
as he reached across for the stranger’s money,
“ There is one in every room, sir.” And sniffed.
“ Pot Luck ” writes :
Public school teachers’ shifts in N.S. W. seem to be con.
trolled in a haphazard sort of way that would indicate
that the Department is very much out of touch with the
individual conditions of its officers. Frinstance, a teacher
with lung weakness pegs away for years with a petition
for removal to a drier climate, while at the other end of
the district is a man who hates a dry climate, who holds
the same certificate and class of school, and whose peti-
tion to be removed to some place like Fogville is yellow
with age and venerableness. Doctors’ certificates count
as nothing, and when at last both sufferers are pretty
well decomposed, the exchange is made. But it comes
quite by chance, and three weeks and five days of the
summer vacation are allowed to pass before the parties
are instructed to take charge respectively on the following
Monday. Then comes the rough-and-tumble of auction
sale, to say nothing of the sacrifice in cash caused by
having to clear out at a moment’s notice and leave
matters for the wife to fix up. Shifts should he effected
during first week of the vacations.

A bad taste in the mouth is the result of con-
centration or precipitation of fumes arising from
a sour stomach, while sleeping, forming a coating

more frequently experienced when waking in the
morning after a restless night. The mouth feels-
clogged and the throat thick with phlegm. You
expectorate, but you cannot get rid of the taste.
You have probably eaten some indigestable food;
before retiring which has become putrid in the
stomach. Cleanse your stomach at once and save
further complications. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills will thoroughly cleanse the stomach, promote
digestion, and tone the system. A couple of
these pills taken occasionally before retiring will
regulate the system perfectly, and prevent a bad:
taste in the mouth in the morning.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The V.A.T.C. handicapper has practically
tipped Marvel Loch for the Caulfield Cup by allotting- too
weight to the N.S.W. mare for the Toorak Handicap and
asking her to concede pounds to several nags that will
have to give her a considerable lump in the longer race.
There is, for instance, Sweet Nell, a mare with no higher
credentials for staying power than Marvel Loch. Nell
was handicapped to give her 71b. in the Cup, but the
more recent estimate puts her 41b. below the other lady.
Warroo is another one that looks well in the Cup on the
Toorak Handicap weights.
• • * *
Seaport, the unluckiest of Victorian horses in
his own country, has been winning races at Singapore.
Change of climate, or change of company ?
* * » •
S.A. Supreme Court has upset the astonishing
police-court decision, whereby a street betting case was
dismissed because the prosecution had not “proved"
that any betting took place. (The remarks sworn to were,
“To invest on the handicap,” “What price Whirl?"
“3 to 1," “I’ll hive. ss. on”—and eventually “Whirl
won.” “One pound, is it not”; and payment of the
pound. But no expert evidence was given as to what
this talk meant!) The Chief Justice refused to believe
that this was “ merely an eccentric act ” on defendant's
part, and claimed that Judges are entitled to use the
knowledge they possess as men of the world.
* » * #
Maoriland horse-owner Stead found that if
N.S.W. horses were not quite so fast as his horses, the
Income Tax Commissioners were capable of getting off
the mark at a great rate. They hit the Canterbury
Bportsman for a dividend on the £3570 he won at Rand-
wick during the recent meeting, and also for a dividend
on his loot made at the same meeting last year, when
they were too slow and let the great man depart without
asking him for 6d. in the £.
* * • *
Justice Edwards’ bitter antipathy to the tote
was responsible for the light sentence of 12 months passed
on Percy Dawson, accountant to the Auckland (M.L.)
Racing- Club, who got away with £2937. Judge Edwards
made more remarks about the machine in delivering
judgment than he did about the prisoner and his crime.
He said that Dawson “ might have lived an honest,
respectable life but for that curse of society, the
totalisator. This legalised machine enabled the public to
gamble all the year round, every day and all day. Meetings
were multiplied simply for the sake of gains which were
derived from that instrument of depravity. However,
it was prisoner’s duty to be faithful to his employers
carrying on a legal, if immoral, business and to abstain
from stealing their money.” If Judge Edwards knew the
law he would be aware of the fact that race meetings
aren’t multiplied for the sake of gains from the tote. The
number of tote permits was fixed by Act of Parliament
years ago, and country clubs that have sprung up since
then have to do without the machine. And these are the
meetings which are a perfect Sheol owing to the horde of
bookmakers and welshers, and guessers, and pick-pockets.
A tote permit means quietness and honesty, and the
ejectment of undesirables from the paddock.
* * * •
“ Classic ” races do not give much encourage-
ment to the owner of a good colt in S.A. George Ander-
son, who won this year’s Derby with Torah, and the
Morphettville Plate, for 2-year-olds, with A 1 Forkan,
received only £346 10s. for the two events.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby team is back from Maoriland,
mostly in splints. Swannell, who, when he played in the
British Fifteen, was counted a rough player, apparently
struck something harder than himself and had most of his
features in a sling; Burge and Murnin resembled
’eroes—blokes who had won the Victoria Cross and other
baubles in the shambles ; and everyone had his pet injury
to nurse and his score of scars to show. They are very
“ willing” in Maoriland, where it is considered bad form
to squeal when the man you tackle smashes you on the
nose.
• • • *
As far as sport is concerned, the winter has
gone. Football is dead, or would have been but for the
unexpected inability of Rozelle and Balmain “Soccer”
teams to decide the destination of the Gardiner Cup. A
strenuous struggle ended in a draw, both teams scoring a
couple of goals. The referee ordered 20 minutes extra
play, ten minutes from each end, to decide which of them
should take the utensil, and each team attacked in turn,
only to be met by an invincible defence, so that when the
whistle blew they were still equal. They will meet again
next Saturday and endeavor to settle the matter. Any-
one who wants to see a really pretty game of football will
take a circle seat at Epping ground on Saturday, for
neither Ruggers nor Austral-rulers play their particular
game half as well as the “ Soccer” crow-d.
*.* * *
Football in Melbourne last Saturday was re-
stricted to a League final, an Association semi-final and
the Inter-State Schools’ match—Petersham (N.S.W.) v.
Clifton Hill (Vic.). Latter was played on the M.C.C.
Ground prior to the League match, and proved almost a
repetition of last year’s farcical game, when the same
Petersham beat Albert Park. The trouble is that the
Cornstalk teams are half-a-head taller and about a stone
and a-half heavier than the Vies., consequently no com-
parison of play is possible. There was this year
a restriction that all players were to be under 17,
but as all but one of the Victorian boys' were
under 15 the stipulation didn’t equalize matters
much. A weight-limit would work better. Game
ended—N.S.W. 3 goals 8 behinds to Vic. 2 goals 3
behinds. After that Fitzroy tackled Carlton for the
League final (Collingwood’s “ challenge ” final with the
winners transpires next Saturday), and up to half-time
the two gave an exhibition of fine, fast, strenuous ’ball—
the board then showing ’Roy 5 goals 3 behinds to Carl-
ton 4 goals 6 behinds. During the interval ’Roy
apparently came to the conclusion that it would be best
to make things sure out of hand, and it piled up 3 more
goals and 3 behinds in 15 minutes, the ball being within
shooting distance of Carlton’s goal almost the whole time.
Though the blue-and-whites made several violent efforts
after that, they never raised the score within gunshot of
the others’, who ultimately wound up with 11 goals 6
behinds to Carlton’s 6 goals 9 behinds. Ground was
packed, 26,000 persons parting with £938 —two-thirds of
which goes to the League’s funds.
At the East Melbourne ground some 12,000
people paid 6d. a head and got vastly more for their coin.
Here Richmond and “ Ports ” were arguing who should
meet North next Saturday in the Association final, and the
discussion was distinctly willing. The two teams
notoriously love each other like Heathens, and a lot of
extra police had been laid on, but when Richmond, in the
first quarter, kicked 5 goals 5 behinds to nothing,
Ports got properly aroused and waded in with vim
—also with elbows, shoulders and feet. At half-
time the figures were Richmond 6 goals 6 behinds
to Ports’ 4 goals 2 behinds, but immediately on
resuming the former potted two more goals. Then
someone’s elbow collided with an opponent’s eye, he re-
taliated viciously, there was a “ scrap,” and, before any-
one could fully grasp the circumstance, a dozen players
were letting out at one another right and left, and half
the crowd had swarmed over the pickets into the melee,
and was giving gratuitous advice, interspersed with
prods, bangs, and kicks. It took the police, the umpire,
and the more reputable supporters a good 10 minutes to
sort things out. The game after that was flabby and dis-
cursive, and nobody seemed to care much what happened.
Result was: Richmond, 11 goals 8 behinds ; Port, 7
goals 6 behinds. It was a nice, friendly game, the only
unusual circumstance about it being that no one attacked
the umpire.
* * * *
At the final for the junior Wairarapa football
championship between Martinborough and Tuhirangi
(Maoriland), one supporter of the Martinborough team
offered a mounted deer’s head, valued at £5, for the first
player who crossed the opponent’s line ; and a Tuhirangi
barracker went one better with the offer of a free gift of
“an acre of land ! ” The Tuhirangis won the day, and
Bradstock, a young schoolmaster, gained the land.
A compromise has been arrived at by the dis-
putants in the question of forming' a Board of Control to
regulate international cricket, and the returning Aus-
tralian cricketers will find on arrival that the old order
has changed. N.S.W. is to have three places on the
Board, Vic. three, and Queensland one. Of the three
Victorians, one is to be appointed by the M.C.C., and two
by the local association ; and as the influence of the
powerful Melbourne club would probably have been
strong enough to secure a seat for its representative any-
how, the contending parties seem to have squabbled
violently over a small margin of difference. The S.A.
Association is the party to fall off the ’bus.
* * • •
The fact that the Australian XI. is returning,
like the cows of the legendary Brown, in various quan-
tities and at irregular intervals, makes it very evident
that the team doesn’t expect to be received with fire-
works. However, like fevers and other things, it has
served its purpose, and the failure of the team at
the test matches hastened on the formation of the
national Australian Board of Control, which will
probably change the method of selection and
root out the old gang that was so largely influenced
by personal friendships. A few years back Eng-
land declined to send a team to Australia owing
to the paucity of bowlers. The absurdity of send-
ing this crowd to Britain without either a slow or
left-hand bowler was patent to every cricketer. But the
boodle was there waiting—what else mattered? Contrast
the return of the first XI. with that of the present team ;
no public reception for this crowd—no bells ringing,
unless, like the Duke of Plaza-Toro, they ring the door-
bell themselves.

» * * *
Australian Eleven manager Laver, the man who
is never without a smile, is an instance of a bowler reach-
ing his zenith at a time of life when most leather jerkers
have lost their fire and spin. He is an enthusiastic
wheelist, and having invested part of his share of the
swag in the latest thing in automobiles, will bring a
couple of the team surreptitiously across the continent, so
that they can reach the safety of their homes without
being too enthusiastically welcomed by the disrespectful
push.
• * * *
Grade cricket starts in Sydney this coming
Saturday, while the juniors are already in the field. The
great, overshadowing North Sydney crowd has split into
three, Gordon and Middle Harbor having broken off and
started for themselves. North Sydney will not loom so
largely in grade cricket this year. Of the six inter-State
matches, three will be played at Sydney Cricket Ground—
the Queensland match on December 15, the South
Australian on January Bth, 1906, and the Victorian on
Jan. 26th. The other three matches will find Queensland,
Victoria and S’Australia battling against the Cornstalks
in their own capitals.
* * * *
George Giffen has been made an honorary life-
member of S.A. Cricket Association. The veteran was
inclined to refuse the compliment, on the ground that it
must mean that his playing days are over, and he doesn’t
feel ready for the shelf yet ; but he was assured that the
only intention was to do him honor, and that he can play
till he is a grandfather if he likes. Another honorary is
W. O. Whitridge, who took an active part in forming the
association in 1871, and still bowls lobs in suburban
matches.

“ Wiljar ” : Was it really that fooling on board
(B. 31/8/’OS) that lifted Newland’s jaw off its hinges, or
sunstroke, ptomaine-poisoning, and strained-back that
incapacitated Gregory? Dreams are unreliable, but in
my dream I noticed not one Australian fielding. The
Englishmen hit fours. A toad-like Shape bowled respect-
ful “ under-armers.” Nobody raced, bat in hand, along
the pitch. The umpire, Cohen, said it was un-British,
and unnecessary. When England was tired scoring,
everybody adjourned for lunch ; and then Cohen, in pro-
posing the toast, said that the proceedings were highly
satisfactory, and the new loan would be granted as
desired.
