* * * #
George B. Richardson writes from Kelvin,
Dandaloo (N.S. W.): “In your last issue (21/9/’OS) I read a
par in your ‘Sporting Notions,’—‘Jim Cassidy, of
Bathurst, is the only survivor of the team of cricketers
who played against Stephenson’s XI at Bathurst in 1861.’
You are rather mistaken, as I for one am very much alive,
in fact just as much alive as I was when, in the match
referred to, I spun the bail round on Ned Stephenson’s
stumps, and did not knock it off. You will also, no
doubt, know (by repute) the Rev. T. B. Tress (C.E.) of
Sydney, who is still alive, also Sandy Naylor, of Nyngan.
These, with .my very old friend Jim Cassidy, still leave
four of us. I first played in Sydney in 1852, being, I be-
lieve, the first round-arm bowler in N.S.W. I may men-
tion that I played in a match at Bathurst as far back as
26th January, 1854—Western Goldfields v. Bathurst.”
* * * *
The real secret of Yankee is bluff.
For instance, the following remarks by Arthur F. Duffey,
the sprinter, have appeared under tragic headlines in
nearly every paper of note published in the great braggart
U.S.A.: “ Arthur F. Duffey, the champion sprinter of the
world, who has just returned from a victorious tour of
Australia and Maoriland, has announced his retirement
from the cinder path. ‘I am through with running,’ he
said ; ‘ and that is why I came home. You ask why lam
quitting when at my best ? Because I am tired of the
game. I have had my day at it, and would rather leave
now, at the height of my fame, than wait for a defeat.’”
And the modest young American said nothing about a
gentleman named Nigel Barker, of Sydney University,
who beat him fair and square on Sydney Cricket Ground.
* * • *
For many a day amateur athleticism in Maori-
land has suffered with the Tired Feeling, but there are
signs that that someone is beginning to take an interest
in the art of getting over hurdles, and a revival of paper-
chasing is threatened. Also, the Amateur Athletic As-
sociation has got a move on, and heaved out its secretary.
It is always a healthy sign when the secretary gets
heaved out onto the rim of his hat—it shows members are
taking an interest in things.
* # * *
“ Hamer ” : Spite of comments to the contrary,
ping-pong, like last year’s fashions, still lingers in the
country. The Banquo-like spectre of the game was resur-
rected from its grave at Muswellbrook (N.S.W.) the other
day, and it aroused all the enthusiasm there is in that
silent township, where it always seems to be Sunday
afternoon.
* * • *
Armadale (Vic.) Bowling Club, winner of the
State’s premiership (for the third year in succession), has
played 31 matches for the past three seasons, and won
them all. It runs a standpipe, with a tap and tin mug
attached, as sole “ refreshments,” and the curse of drink
is not allow'ed to roost on its game. The success of the
club speaks well for temperance as an adjunct to bowls.
But after all is it worth while to be “ temperate ” even to
win at bowls ?
» * * *
Maoriland cyclist O’Connor, who won the
Timaru-Christchureh (M.L.) Dunlop road race, made a
very poor showing in the Goulburn-Sydney run, and was
neyer placed among the front-rankers. There’s a lot of
luok about road racing, and it is just as well O’Connor
was not among the first-rankers—it would have cost him
his income to store his prizes. When he went back to
Timaru from Christchurch he took this little load with
him : A grand piano, valued at 55 guineas ; a Royal Swift
cycle, valued at £25; a cheque for £2O for the fastest
time ; a gold medal valued at five guineas ; and a blue
ribbon, worth just what his best girl could make out of it.
« • * *
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen, after hawking
the mile international championship of Australia dis-
mally over the Commonwealth, has disposed of it to the
Victorian League for £7 10s. The N.S.W. League, when
it doesn’t get a championship, growls hideously ; and,
when it does get it, it sells it for a few shillings as if it
were hard up and wanted the few bob to keep itself out of
pawn. And either way it is a conspiracy against N.S.W.
—as Joseph Carruthers would say.
• • • •
Don Walker’s record as an Australian cyclist
will take a deal of rubbing out. He has been
riding since 1889, and at the present time occupies
scratch mark alone. Just on 200 races have fallen
to his calves, with a contingency of over £3OOO in prizes.
Walker’s racing track longevity is probably a world’s
record.

The only foreign bike riders likely to invade
Australia this season will be Yankees Lawson and
MacFarland, and it is stated that each of these riders
is only returning to piok out a spot whereon to settle
permanently. The general opinion carried from Australia
last season was that Australian handicappers had too
lofty an opinion of foreign riders, and to win big handi-
caps cost too much labor.
* » * *
N.S.W. bikist Frank Allsopp has about the
hardest luck of any of the State’s road racers. In the
Goulburn-Ash field race he has been placed three times —
—in 1902, 1904 and 1905; and this year also saw him
run second in the Bathurst-Ash field race. No other
rider, as far as this paper is aware, has such a record.
But he drifts steadily towards “ scratch,” getting all the
penalties and none of the big prizes of successful riding.
* * * •
Joe Megson, once Sydney’s idol on the cycle
track, still pushes his jigger in public, but he is a snail-
like Joe to-day compared with the Joe of some seasons
back. In the Eight-Hours Demonstration cycle events,
which start the new season’s racing in N.S.W., Megson is
conceded 45yd. start in half-a-mile.

South Australian League of Wheelmen has
abandoned its October meeting “ owing to the fact that
there will be no visiting riders in Australia at that time.”
This sort of thing ought to rouse the blood of every
patriotic Australian. Dun’t Australians care to see
Australian cyclists race? This gang of ansomic Crow-
eaterS ought to be boycotted by local wheelmen after
such an insult, and left to subsist entirely on the entrance
fees of those precious foreign riders—international
parasites who mostly come to Australia under agreement
to receive appearance money.
* * * •
The Dunlop motor reliability contest on the
Melbourne-Sydney r ad in November next is going to be,
judging by present appearances, a very stirring affair.
Some of the competitors have sworn to win the beautiful
Dunlop Cup at any cost, and are importing unusually
powerful machines to make sure of it. By November the
roads should be dry and hard, but the Dunlop party
which came across week before last, erecting danger discs
and notices where it was necessary to slacken speed,
nearly got drowned several times. They had been enjoying
a record downpour in some parts of Victoria, and the
greater part of the trip through Bent’s State was in
reality a voyage. In one awful place the car ran
along the alleged main road for miles with water
above the axles. At the worst spot the water
was over 6ft. deep. The car was a 12-16 h.p.
Decauville, and at the deepest part of the stream nothing
was to be seen but a cloud of spray, in the midst of which
the machine was quite invisible. Strange to say, the
engine did not stop running, but for quite a long time
after leaving the stream it continued to radiate water.
The Dunlop Cup, the first prize of the forthcoming
competition, is a beautiful bit of workmanship, and
valued at 100 guineas. The female figure and the car in
which she stands are both of phosphor bronze, and the
automobile is not a carving, but a real car, designed to
scale, made in separate pieces, and built up like a life-size
or adult automobile. It has only to be won twice to
become the sole property of any individual.
* * » *
Anew terror has struck affluent Sydney.
Nowadays it is the correct thing, when you sympathise
with your friend’s charming but sadly-neglected wife,
to take her round the beauty spots in a motor-car. There
are any numbers of gentlemen who, thanks to the school
of motoring, can drive a car, but they do not complete
their education and learn to crawl underneath and use a
spanner while lying on the broad of the back, so when
they take the lady out it is necessary to take a mechanic
along too in case of a break-down ten miles from any-
where. Also, the mechanic comes in handy to hold the
machine’s head while the gentleman helps the lady to
gather bush blossoms, or ferns, or to take a snapshot
from the top of some adjacent knoll. But the mechanic
has his drawbacks ; he interferes with the conversation,
and motor people were being exhorted to provide a seat
for the brute on some part of the machine where he could
not overhear things, when the reptile suddenly developed
another vice. It is a bad vice too, and quite a number of
citizens are angry because of the sums these wretches
charge to hold their tongues.
• * * *
Next Saturday will be a great day on the
waters of Port Jackson, and the Sydney sailing season
will start with a burst of bunting and a garden of white
sails. It was believed that the formation of the Motor
Boat Club would almost wreck sailing, but the prospects
are all the other way, and the new members have more
than filled the places of those who dropped out.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rowing Association’s Championship
Regatta comes off on Saturday, October 7, and what with
equinoctial gales and private disturbances by Government
Meteorologist Hunt, the crews are all in a woefully un-
trained condition. There’ll be some awful language used
by the “ coaches” during the next week in the wild rush
to get the crews cursed and licked into shape. A foot-
ball coach can use slangwidge, but he is held somewhat
in restraint by the danger of some player suddenly try-
ing to break his neck. The rowing coach knows that no-
body dare break his neck for fear of capsizing the boat
and wetting the property, and he takes advantage of this.
* * • *
How cycle road-racing does grow in Australia.
Long ago it was evident that the entry for the Warrnam-
hool-Melbourne road race would be a bumper one, and
when it soared up to nearly 250 riders it left all records
far behind. Then the handicaps appeared, and some fell
out, but the acceptors still number 196, which is a record
for the event. Last year it cost the big Dunlop Tyre Cos.
6s. per head for victuals for the riders, and as the men
do not dismount to eat or drink, but grab it as they flit
past, and occasionally they flit in batches of from 10 to 30,
quite a number of men have to be employed handing out
the provisions. Avery strong Maoriland contingent will
take part in the race, and as their form is said to have
improved considerably since the Goulburn run, the
islanders are expected to make a good showing.
* * * *
Some Melbourne people are remarking that, if a
revival in pugilism is to be effected by “Tote ” Wren, at
the local Exhibition Building, the moral tone of the com-
munity will soon need re-tuning. Pugilism can hardly
expect a very glorious revival from the police-protected
Tote merchant, whose dealings with cyclists ard other
sorts of sports have not been of an improving charm- er.
Moreover, apart from the question of Wren’s personality,
the letting of a Government building for stoushing
matches is repugnant to the feelings of a majoritv of the
public, who are nominally the owners of the costly pre-
mises.
* * * *
The Turner-Robbo scrap at Sydney National
Sporting Club last week was disappointing. Robbo
attacked in the early part of the disturbance with great
vim, but his condition was not equal to his hurry, and
though several times he narrowly missed scoring a knock-
out, Turner survived the trouble. Then Robbo went to
pieces, going down more than once to get his wind. In
the 10th round he was plainly “ done,” and at last he lay
on his back from sheer exhaustion, and was counted out.
Colored brudder Joseph Walcott met Denny
Maloney at Sydney Gaiety Athletic Club on Satur-
day night last, and a pretty display of fur and feathers
resulted. Maloney relied on his strong right arm ; Mr.
Walcott hit freely with both hands, and the white man’s
burden of bruises and contusions increased rapidly. The
colored man seemed to bash by machinery, and slogged
away frenziedly, in holts and out of them ; and, although
he stopped a few heavy ones with his anatomy, he went
on whirling and bashing till, at the end of the ninth
round, the white man gave up. The burden of Walcott's
huge fryingpan-like fists was more than he could endure.
* * * *
S.M. Herald, in ita “ On the Land ” column,
the other day, in the course of a vigorous appeal for more
stringent protection by law of native birds : “The un-
happy victims of the aboriginal instinct are unfortunately
unable to protest, save with the mute protest of God’s
creatures slaughtered to satisfy what is described as a
taste for sport, but is really a savage craving for the joys
of killing.” And in the same issue the Herald enthusi-
astically reported that at the “ shoot ” for the “ Ladies’
(Ladies, ye gods!) Trophies” at N.S.W. Gun Club’s
pigeon massacre, on the previous day, 33 shooters “ faced
the traps ” ; and that one alleged sportsman “ had hard
luck in finding his bird, although well hit, struggle over
the boundary. He refrained from using the second
barrel in the interests of backers.” Not much sympathy
here with “the mute protest of God’s creature” that
struggled bleeding and in agon} over the boundary, from
the sheer animal instinct to save its harmless life—that
mustn’t be put out of its pain “in the interests of
backers.” That was merely “ hard luck ” for the “sports-
man.”
• * » •
The recent success of Maoriland horses at
Randwick (N.S.W.) recalls the fact that 50 years ago
Maori chiefs were prominent on the turf. In 1855 the
Maori chief Patuone had a whip made by Callow & Son, of
Park Lane, London, for presentation to a jockey who had
successfully piloted one of his horses. The whip was
mounted in silver, with a piece of greenstone set in the
end. Around the handle were represented horses and
jockeys, straps, buckles, &c., richly chased in silver ; and
on a large shield was the inscription in gibberish : “ Kia
Hemi Makerehi no Patuone—Maini Pereti £IOO. Te Niu
Tireni, 1855, Akarana.”
• • * •
Hastier Hackenschmidt, who, like Sandow, has
gone into the doctoring business, has been telling some-
one that sweets are the best strength-producers. After
having wrestled in many countries, he has come to the
conclusion that the strongest people are the Turks. He
attributes their strength to their being great sweetmeat
eaters and not alcohol drinkers. “ Eat all the sweets you
can if you would be strong,” he says.

A Melbourne tobacconist lately told a bench of
magistrates that the sale of picture-cards representing
prominent footballers (which cards some cigarette-makers
put in their packets) was so brisk that he had doubled the
price without in any way diminishing the rush. Tho
trouble arose oyer a youngster who had stolen money to
buy the cards so that he might gloat over the cast-iron
countenances of his favorite ball-kickers.
* * * *
“Two Seas”: Whenever some old slave of a
racehorse that has earned for its owner hundreds of
pounds over the sticks breaks its eager neck through fall-
ing at the last fence from sheer leg weariness, we read at
breakfast next morning that this was to be its last race, and
had it kept on its feet it would have been pensioned off
in a luxuriant grass paddock by the kind owner. Now, as
the public must be getting tired of that old gag, I suggest
that, for a change, we should be told that the noble
animal was a considerable loss, as the owner intended
selling it to a ’bus proprietor next week, and that it
realised a mere trifle as cat’s-meat.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association has raised a fine
large spectre by quashing as informal the nomination of
Victor Trumper as a candidate for election on one of next
season’s Selection Committees. Trumper, it savs is a
“ professional cricketer.” If the Paddington idol is a
pro., then his colleagues in the Australian Team are cer-
tainly not amateurs, for most of them get what is tanta-
mount to appearance-money in inter-State matches, and
all share in the gate-receipts of the tour. In Australia,
the distinction between the lily-white amateur and the
professional is not of much moment; but in England the
status of the Australian cricketers will be not worth men-
tioning if they are known as professionals in their own
land. No more “ Mr. Darling” and “ Mr. Noble ’’ in the
cricket scores, and “ Mr. Howell ” and “ Mr. Kelly ” in
press interviews. The hardworking professional cricketer
in England has absolutely no social standing at all, and
before visiting Australians consent to join his down-
trodden ranks there will be trouble.
* * * *
As an accompaniment to a Bruce auction re-
cently held in a Melbourne suburb there was a “grand
amateur bun-struggle for boys under 12." The buns
smeared with treacle, were hung on strings and set spin-
ning. Seven boys entered, and, with their hands tied
behind their backs, made frantic efforts to bite pieces of
bun, getting their faces and hair daubed with treacle, to
the great amusement of the crowd. There was much
barracking by pals of the competitors. After a time
Buster Smith and Fatty Green were seen to be getting
through their buns with comparative rapidity. Buster
just beat Fatty by a bite. He was triumphantly walking
away with the alarm-clock given as a trophy, when Fatty
entered a protest, on the ground that the winner was
over age. After careful consideration the stewards up-
held the protest j but by this time Buster was far away
with the prize. Also, the third man, Micko Doolan,
declared that Fatty was over age, too. Then the other
competitors and their friends joined in with various,
accusations against all the placed men, and, when writer,
had to leave to fulfil another engagement, things were
well in train for half-a-dozen fights. Thus does the taint
of sordid commercialism threaten ruin to every form of
sport.
* * * «
A desire for actual participation in sport is rare
among the crowds that troop to the football field • what
is universal is a love to look at it, to talk about it, or to
bet on it. The players are really few, and the desire to
play almost non-existent among the mass of spectators.
It’s time there was a distinction drawn between the two
classes, Australians as a body are not unduly given to
sport—for what is true of football is true of cricket and
other games—but are unduly given to standing around
the field where sport is carried on.

MINE MANAGERS, &c.
N.S.W. has at last got rid of the yacht
Victoria at a net loss of £34.000 to the State. The
vessel was bought whilst the Owe’Sullivan loan
boom was on, and was designed to take politicians,
gownors and other potentates on trips of State
around the coast. It has now been sold for a song.
New South Wales Divorce Court tried a
case in the present sittings in which one of Bruce
Smith’s black brethren figured largely. This par-
ticular brother was a Hindu “doctor.” Fifteen
years ago a Brisbane girl, aged 18, went for a long
walk. She was overtaken first by a sore foot, and
afterwards by the Hindu medicine man in a
buggy. He offered the lame duck a lift, which
she cheerfully accepted, and when he offered his
heart and hand she accepted them also. The
Hindu soon tired of his wife, and sought distrac-
tion in the smiles of other girls, and the
Bride from the Buggy led an unhappy life until
her nigger husband, in 1903, married another
white girl. For this he got five years gaol, and
gave his first wife an opportunity of release. A
dispiriting feature about the case is the fact that
there are enough white women ready to marry
a nigger to make it quite easy for the black
brother to commit bigamy.
Flags at Half Mast. —From time to
time it is an ordinary thing for us to see the flags
at half mast on different public buildings in token
of respect to some member of official, political and
social life. That man was apparently in perfect
health, and a few short weeks backs little thought
that his life would end so quickly. His death
comes as a surprise, making his friends and rela-
tions serious and sorrowful for a life so quickly
ended. There must have been some reason for the
early demise, which was only an effect of a cause,
and that cause was certainly disease, which
corrupted the delicate mechanism of the body,
threw something out of gear, interfering with its
effectual working. With some men a serious
disease may arise from a simple cause, he may be
in complete ignorance of disease or death to within
a few hours of its appearance. Any man who may
be in doubts as to his general condition and who
suspects that he is not in the state of health which
he should be, should write or communicate with
Dr. Richard Wallace, Medical Staff of the Freeman
and Wallace Medical Institute, Elizabeth and
Bathurst-streets, Sydney, who make no charge for
friendly counsel if a personal visit is paid or for an
opinion freely expressed in reply to any letter re-
ceived by him. He is an expert in the effective
treatment and rapid cure of men’s serious dis-
orders.*
A disastrous lire at South Melbourne
lately—in which the house-father was so severely
burned that his life was despaired of, while his
wife actually died from the shock —brought to
light no less than four persons who each claimed
to have rescued the injured man from the flames.
Two of them asserted they had carried him, at
great personal risk, out of the front door, and the
other two said they had dragged him, at desperate
hazard, out of the back door.
Melbourne Hospital Committee has just
completed its crawlsome project to fritter away
£50 of the moneys of this always insolvent institu-
tion in obtaining from the College of Heralds, or
some such comic-opera body, the right to call
itself “Royal.” The average cost of a patient in
the hospital is about 15s. a week, so the scheme
really means that some series of unfortunates, who
might otherwise be maintained, and probably
cured of their sickness, will be denied admission
to the institution for a period of about 15 months,
in order that a score of servile people may gratify
their natural instincts. The outrage was carried
on the motion of Dr. T. P. Mclnerney, largely
advertised as a sincere democrat at various time*
during his career.
“ Hypo ” from Victoria : —
Minister Swinburne’s Water Bill has created consterna-
tion amongst the monopolistic land(-and water)-lords ot
Victoria. The “ Beds and Banks ” definitions have been
particularly a source of agitation in the Fatuien’s souls, i.e.,
their pockets. They have attempted to show, among
other things, that the action of the river w'aters has
decreased their holdings by washing hits away. Hence,
they argue, they have a right to the rivers which hav•
robbed them. But what of those who have had thiir
territory added to ? I know of many instances w'here the
latter has occurred. Also know of cases where these
land-and-water-lords have actually claimed (and received)
rent for land below the high-water mark of rivers, wh ch
land, under the conditions of their freehold, they had no
ownership of. Have also been stopped from shooting on
(and even fishing in) the rivers adjacent to these pro-
perties. Have been stopped from chopping firewood on
the closed roads on the estates. Were these gentry to
have undisturbed possession much longer, ordinary folk
would need a special permit to breathe.
Sydney Ragged School children, when
directed to sing the “Doxology,] the other day,
at a public function, burst out with “God Save
the King,” and the incident is taken as proof of
the urchins’ training in loyalty. It proves a lot
of things, and among the rest that the apathy of
the public in supporting those Sydney institutions
has a reasonable cause. They should be Govern-
ment institutions, instead of under the control of
society hangers-on, who teach the children that
the chief business of life is to sing “ Gor Save.”

“Dinghy, Ahoy! Bring' plenty of CAPSTAN !’


Made in
Australia.
THE Victorian Laundry and Ironing
Exhibition, held at the Melbourne
Exhibition Building, if it did nothing
else, splendidly demonstrated the utter folly of
buying ‘ ‘ sweated-labor, ’’imported Shirts when in
our midst there is a well-conducted Shirt-making
industry carried on by such a firm as Buckley
and Nunn, Melbourne, who are in a position
to buy direct from the loom on equal terms with
the English manufacturer, and yet manufacture
and sell in Australia on better terms. The
working exhibit—a miniature replica of the con-
ditions and working of Messrs. Buckley and
Nunn’s factory—completely convinced the
many visitors to the Exhibition that in buying
Shirts from Buckley and Nunn’s an Australian
industry is stimulated at an actual gain to the
purchaser.
Messrs. Buckley and Nunn believe in
putting their clients on the best possible footing,
and, wherever practicable, eliminating the
middleman and the loading of the price of goods
with unnecessary profits and charges. This was
the idea which prompted the establishment of
their most up-to-date Shirt factory—a factory
which forms the one link between the loom and
the wearer in any part of Australasia, doing
away entirely with the jobber in London, the
heavy duty on the imported article, and also
with intermediate profits this side.

Shirt-making Demonstration by Buckley & Nunn, at the Victorian Laundry Exhibition,

Patriotism Pays.
Ihe man who buys English shirts incurs a loss compared with
the man who buys his shirts from Buckley & Nunn as the
following figures amply prove. Take the basis of £lOO of linen
bought from the mill by the English manufacturer :
m
Cost of material
Cost of labor.
Cost of selling and buying in London, say,
This then represents invoice price of shirts
in London.
Now you have to add this on to cover 27* per
cent, duty and 12J per cent, charges, and
then you get the
Total landed cost of shirts bought in London
to which you have to add
Another 33§ per cent, for retailers’ profit,
making the
Total which the Australian who does not
support Australian industry has to
pay for English-made shirts.
But this is how you score if you buy Shirts from Buckley
and Nunn— Shirts, mind you, that are Made in Australia of
exactly the same linen as is put into the English Imported Shirt.
Remember, Buckley and Nunn buy direct from the loom and
get the same value for their £lOO as the English manufacturer.
ooiar
£219
B. &N. buy England direct off loom the
same as English manufacturer, and to
this, being raw material, only
6| per cent, and 12J per cent, charges have to
be added to make the
Total.
Now add cost of labor, say,
One-third retailers’ gross profit
And we finish our lesson in patriotism and
arithmetic by pointing out that there is
a gain of one-third in buying direct from
B. & N.
The “ Perfecfla” Shirt.
“ The Perfecta ” is the registered name of the Oxford Shirts
manufactured by Messrs. Buckley & Nunn in Australia of some
hundreds of designs of material, and at 53. 6d. is the best value
possible. It is really instructive to get from Messrs. Buckley
and Nunn particulars and patterns.
White Shirts.
The output of White Shirts by Messrs. Buckley and Nunn
is very great, and there is always the demand to be met for both
day and evening dress wear in the various styles that fashion and
comfort now dictate. Messrs. Buckley and Nunn guarantee all
qualities at 6s. 6d., Bs. 6d., and 10s. 6d.
Direct from the Loom.
Finally it must be borne in mind that the linen is bought by
Buckley and Nunn direct from the mills and that Messrs.
Buckley and Nunn’s system of measurements ensures a perfect
fitting Shirt for anybody in Australia.

Victoria owns a School for Wives. This
is its sub-title. It is officially known as the Insti-
tute ot Domestic Economy, but its chief purpose,
is to give the young feminine idea points in the
management of home and husband—“ practical
knowledge of thexlomestic arts,” the circular calls
it. It seems appropriate to ask if the teachers
and professors in the School for Wives are them-
selves graduates from the school of matrimony,
?. ad, if so, have they testimonials from their last
!. usbands ? Many women are excellent liouse-
keepers but very bad wives. This type devotes
i: s maiden years to catching a husband, and its
married years to keeping a house.

There was a sensation in a Sydney street
the other day. A man was down in the sewer,
effecting repairs, or looking for a land-agent’s
missing books, or something. The circular iron
cap had been removed from the surface of a man-
hole, and the sentinel on top happened to look
away for a second. A stout, purple-faced woman
took advantage of his inattention to step into the
opening with both feet, and commenced to des-
cend with terrific velocity. She stopped at her
hips with a dull, beefy thud. Then all Sydney,
with seven common policemen and two sergeants,
seemed to spring up like one harlequin through
the cracks in the street blocks. The distressed
lady squealed with fury, and threatened revenge,
police courts, and heavy damages, and that she
would make them prove it. A facetious on-
looker suggested using a shoe-horn to slide
her up on, but was called a heartless person,
and was requested to “ Have a bit of sense ! ” Then
the aggregate gendarmerie, with the light of battle
in its eye, bent down, got a firm grip, and com-
menced to lift. The front part of its head grew
purple and threatened to burst. The stout lady
groaned dismally and wept, and said she would
yet demonstrate her innocence and dispel false
accusations. The force put on an extra heave,
and she began to rise like a full-blown moon above
the landscape. The crowd cheered. The police
made a last effort, and the lady burst forth from
her partial concealment like a flower breaking out
in the spring. She was saved. Again she said
that the base aspersions cast on her good name
would yet be dissipated. The sergeant said he
didn’t care whether her name was aspersed or
not: he would take it as it was and put it down in
his book against a charge of breaking into Govern-
’ment property. Then the sewer man looked down
the hole and found his mate insensible.
“ Hypo,” from Victoria :—
The anti-Socialist propaganda has reached our town.
Two ladies are paying house-to-house visits, and explain-
ing to our womenfolk the frightful state of chaos which
will ensue if they do not vote against the Socialists. The
missionaries, being local squatters’ wives, have some
weight with our women, and, after their visits, groups of
females may be seen gathered together, and discussing
the possible and probable cost of the mission ladies’ hats.

WHEN THE FLAGS * HALF-MAST
Sometimes it is an ordinary thing for us, when leaving our homes of a morn-
ing, to see flags at the different public buildings half-mast for some prominent
member of official, political or social life. That man was, a few weeks ago, appa-
rently in perfect health ; he walked about amongst his friends, he laughed and he
smiled, and little thought that in a few weeks’ time his Sun of Life would per-
manently set. His death comes as a surprise amongst his circle of acquaintances,
and makes them at all times serious and sorrowful, but they have to bow to the
inevitable law of continuous change, of Life and Death.
Some part of the delicate mechanism of his body had been thrown out of gear.
Some defect interfered with its correct working. When we inquire into the ail-
ment from the physicians who made the post-mortem examination, we are told,
perhaps, that an aneurism had generated, an artery had clogged, the heart
became choked, surcharged with blood, his life went out like a flash.
This may have been caused by simple Indigestion being the primal cause, pol-
luting the blood, filling it full of Uric. Acid Poison, and other properties that
destroy the red corpuscles, which are at all times the Policemen of the Blood,
and whose mission it is to clear it and preserve its purity. Great effects arise from
little causes, and Indigestion and Dyspepsia have killed thousands.
It is time people learned that Indigestion or any form of stomach trouble is
not a thing to trifle with. It may not be fatal in all instances, but there can be
no condition of Diseased Stomach which does not carry with it Physical Loss,
Weakness, and at all times probability of permanent physical ruin.

“Stony”: “Pierrot” (B. 20/7/’05) refers to the
Children’s Column business. It had been my idea for a
long time that the whole thing was a more or less
unnecessary “fake ; ” and when given the part (among a
large number of other parts) of “ Uncle Bobby ” on a
provincial rag, I learned things which confirmed my
impressions, and became a fakir myself in due course.
The “artistic and literary ” competitions usually provided
about two triers altogether, and mostly in one family at
that. If the results were bad (as they generally were) the
prize-winner was simply announced without publication
of the unfortunate matter. Letters were invited, and
occasionally some misguided child would send along a
frightful screed, which had to be greatly revised for
print. I also wrote a few myself. “Pierrot” says
flippantly that the production of the necessary comment
must be a fearful strain on the intellect. It’s certainly
much better fun to report a three-hours indignation meet-
ing about the local drainage system than to spend half
that time in writing sympathetic drivel about sister Ann’s
black cat’s sad end in the rain-barrel.

LIFE.
(BY “VIVA.”)
No living thing, from man to a microbe, can exist unless it is continuously dying. It is not merely
necessary tor man to absorb nourishment in order to live ; it is equally necessary that, simultaneously,
some portion of his body, bone, blood, or tissue, must waste away and die. It is essential, also, that'll
balance be preserved m the two processes of replenishing the body and the removal of the waste or
dead material which is constantly accumulating within it. An uneven action of either means disease.
A total suspension of either means death. •
Of course everyone knows that an adequate supply of suitable food, pure water and fresh air is
requisite to build up the body, but, unfortunately, most people are not as well aware of the complicated
action ol the mechanism with which nature has endowed man for dealing with the used-up, dead and
waste material always being produced within us, and which, unless regularly extracted from the blood
and expelled from the body, must cause disease or death.
t qP 011 th ® s kin, lungs, kidneys and liver rests the main responsibility of removing waste matter
from the sj stem. Ihe skin usually requires little attention beyond regular washing with good soap
and water, and it will, in the form of perspiration, rid the body of a quantity of dead matter. A fit of
coughing or a chocking sensation will speedily call our attention to anything which hinders the lungs
m heir wor.t of removing, in the form of carbonic acid, an amount of used-up material equivalent to
eig it ounces of pure charcoal every day. The kidneys and liver, on the other hand, are delicate organs,
and it is often not until we begin to suffer from some serious complaint that we become aware that
their action is impeded. Rheuniatism, Gout, Lumbago, Neuralgia, Backache, Sciatica, Blood Dis-
orders, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, General Debility, Gravel, Stone, Bladder
1 roubles. Depression, Lovvlemperature, Antenna, Bright’s Disease, are all caused by disease, or
3 ■ erent action of the kidneys or liver, or both of those organs, permitting the body to retain
urinary and biliary waste poisons which it is their functions to eliminate from the blood and expel in
a natural manner.
W| l[ therefore be seen how important it is that the vigorous health and activity of the kidneys
and liver should be maintained. A person suffering from one or more of the many complaints men-
loned should not regard them as diseases, but should adopt the rational course of attacking them at
e.r true source,.by restoring a proper and regular action of the kidneys and liver, when, the cause of
e t isorc ers having been rectified, the waste matter which produces the trouble is removed and
health ensues as a matter of course.
It was the comprehension of this fact which resulted in the discoverv of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Uertiun medical men—being aware of the difficulty of treating the disorders referred to, and knowing
a ley weie due to inefficient action of the kidneys or the liver, but being unable in most cases to
accura t y c lagnose just which organ was at fault—set themselves the task of compounding a medicine
vhicli would act specifically upon the kidneys and liver alike. After long research they succeeded,
and introduced the medicine to the world under the name of Warner’s Safe Cure. This was about
ir ) y e/ 4 s a go, and since then many millions of bottles have been sold, and people have
- • savc b. rom pain, suffering and death by taking a course of this invaluable specific for all kidney
and liver diseases and disorders arising therefrom. [8

The Inky Way.
“ Hassan “ : “ Pad, boys—pad for all you’re worth,”
said the old journalist, cunning- in his craft. “ They pay
as much for the stuffing as the meat. If you are con-
scientious, cutting down to bed-rock, you’ll get left; and
Jones, who blows out his little facts to bursting point,
will draw more brass than you will on pay-day. Mind
you, the padding lias to be pretty well done ; you mustn’t
show the lumps. But if you get it to run weli, the editor
doesn’t spot it —he has too much to do trimming his sails
to the proprietary breeze. Thus you’ll get credit for a
1 nice light style.’ Great is the adjective ; it helps to
fill up. Always use one, or, if possible, two. Don’t try
three yet; that requires a skill you haven’t got, and
you’d fall in. Any editor would spot it. This space-rate
business makes me tired. I send in a half-column of
crisp copy. I get 10 or 15 bob. Jones, two weeks later,
cribs my stuff, enlarges it to two columns, and gets two
or three quid. Jones wears a top hat ; I wear a hunted
expression, and many streets are closed to me. If I
weren’t so old I’d chuck it and go in for palmistry or
cricket—anything where a man gets paid according to
merit. What? No such occupation! Go to! young
man. You’re a cynic—pure cheek at your age. But, as I
was saying yes—l don’t mind if I do.”
* * * *
“ Polstan ”: The boss mean paper of Australia first
informs contributors that it doesn’t pa}' for anything, and
then informs them that everything must be typed, or it
won’t have a show in the scramble ; only matters of the
greatest interest must be tackled, and anything that isn’t
absolutely original isn’t wanted. Then follows a haughty
remark or so apropos of the kind of article the journal
doesn’t mind accepting, followed by a request to send
stamped wrappers with MSS. Lastly, you mustn’t expect
a reply in the Contributors’ Column, because there isn’t
one. You don’t yet have to send six penny stamps to
secure consideration ; but that is coming.
* ♦ • »
“Melb.": I was working for the Herald when Sam
Winter specially impressed on a new message boy, his first
day out, that he must not be afraid to walk straight into
the Supreme Court and ask for copy. The three Judges,
in their robes, were sitting on a divorce appeal of the first
moment. The new boy went into the court, and addressed
the Chief Justice aloud with, “Any copies for the
Herald, please?”
* * * *
The Dampier Despatch, published at Broome (W.A.), is
type-written on two sheets of foolscap. Cables are sent
on from Perth, and take up most of the space. In the
issue writer saw there was one ad., and one par. of local
news.
» * * *
Professor Morris, of Melbourne University, fell in very
badly once. An Oxford don, whose motive is wrapped in
gloom, wrote to him asking to be supplied with samples
of the best and worst daily papers issued in Australia.
Morris sent copies of Melbourne Argus and Evenin’ ’Erald,
reckoning that the two were respectively the best and
worst dailies he knew of. The Oxford friend received
them in due time, and wrote back that he thought the
Herald a pretty fair sort of print, but he agreed with
Morris that the Argus was the stupidest rag he had ever
set eyes on. It isn’t safe, nowadays, to write to Prof.
Morris to ask for samples of Australian journalism.
Samples of snort are invariably received by the next post.

From still another man who seems to think that he can
make a crust out of the “machinations of Rome ”:—
“Dear Bulletin.—As you have persistently rejected
my contributions, with Kentish fire and other signs of
contempt, I have decided to change my rillijun. For
many years I have been a very strong Evangelical Pro-
distan (whatever that may be); but I will henceforth be a
Roamin’ Carthlic, in order to secure rapid preferment on
your long list of outside contributors. I send herewith
57 pars., 9 poems, and 13 sketches.”
* * * *
“Rep.”: I rise to invoke a curse on the memory of
Isaac Pitman. Since that painstaking ghost (whose por-
trait, still looks blandly out from the frontispieces of short-
hand text books) perfected his system, the world has suf-
fered untold agonies of verbatim reporting. More and
more it has been delivered into the mouths of People Who
Talk. The preachy parson and the perorating politician
have come to regard it as their right to have every bubble
of their froth recorded—and in the pride of being- able to
satisfy this craving, newspaper ri porting (under Pitman’s
baleful spell) has been directed along false lines of verbiage
for 100 years. The result is that in Britain and Australia,
at any rate, men are no longer judged by their actions
but by their words. Only in America, the land where
People Do Things, has the enchantment of Pitman lost
its power. Elsewhere we are in thrall to him and Bumble-
dom. For the sake of a national standard of deeds, not
words, let the memory of Pitman be accursed, and the
devotees of his black art burned at the stake.
* * * *
“ Kaira ” : I unanimously condemn the inanity of the
country correspondent pars, in the city papers. Budding
journalists get hold of some exciting (to them) occur-
rence, and, with perspiration running into their boots,
they frantically rush into the telegraph office and wire
away something like this:—“What might have been a
very serious accident might have happened here to-day
but for the intervention of Providence. Mr. Thomas
Todd was proceeding down the street when he tripped
over his feet and fell. Had the fall taken place ten yards
to his right, he would have fallen down the No. 3 shaft of
the Dead Beet Gold Mining Co., and instant death would
have been certain.” Or, by way of diversion, there’s the
what-might-have-been fire. “ What might have been a
very serious conflagration was narrowly averted here to-
day. Mr. William Jones was passing the Mullock Hotel
when a spark flew from his pipe. Had the wind been
blowing (as it usually does at Muddee) the spark must
have blown into a box of straw placed in the yard, and
the hotel and a valuable block of buildings must have
been destroyed. As it was, the spark fell into the horse-
trough.” At the next meeting of the Journalists’ Insti-
tute I intend to propose the expurgation of everything
beginning “ What might have been,” and, if my motion
isn’t carried, I’ll resign in a mob.
* * * *
“ N.” : Why do we pay 9d. or Is. for a sheaf of foreign
handbills, yclept a magazine ?
.** * *
“H.”: Went to “a Bohemian smoke night” in Mel-
bourne, the other evening, and the whole 60 artists and
scribblers present hadn’t a dirty collar amongst them.
Their linen was deafeningly clean and respectable ; and I
couldn’t hear myself drink for the uproar of their immacu-
late cuffs and shirt fronts. And I had been told that
Bohemia was a sort of Cannibal Club, which defied its
landlord, while the wind blew through the arabesques of
its perforated coat.
* * *
Melbourne Argus recently got off an amazingly original
remark in its leading article: “ When we think of
Macaulay’s Maori gaz: ng upon the ruins of St. Paul’s,”
&c. Oh, that weary old Maorilander who has camped on
the ruins of London bridge so often and never once dropped
in! He grows worse than monotonous. Even King Billy
gazing on the decayed fragments of the Argus would be
a pleasant change.
•* » * *
“ Gilrooney ” : I love those little Labor papers, but with
the sort of love the average mother has for her prodigal
son. There isn’t in Australia to-day, to my knowledge, a
single official Labor paper advocating with any enthusiasm
a system of High Protection. Indeed, several of them
(Barrier Truth is a glaring example) openly barrack for
Freetrade ; the remainder are content to let things slide
and “ sink the fiscal issue.” And the curious part of the
whole miserable business is that, while the Labor press
and the Parliamentary parties are vainly trying to drown
something that knows how to swim, the workers of Aus-
tralia almost unanimously favor a high tariff. It seems to
me that the toilers will be foolishly misrepresented and
hopelessly handicapped until they have their journalists
writing Protection leaders and their politicians strenu-
ously fighting for high tariffism. As things are, the Aus-
tralian Labor press and parties do not represent the fiscal
opinions of the workers of the Commonwealth ; and it’s
just about time some of them stood up and called each
of-these institutions a damfraud and seventeen kinds of a
fool.
* * * *
The S.M. Herald, established in April, 1831, as The
Sydney Herald, by Stevens, Stokes, and M’Garvie, was
published in a building opposite Gallows Hill, Lower
George-street, and nearly opposite the Union Inn, kept
by the father of the late George Thornton, M.L.C. On
the first night of publication George Thornton was sent
across the road to hold candles to help the printer at his
' vo , rk - The front shop, or publishing office, still stands
but the printing house at the rear has just been de-
molished to make room for a modern building
In a made-in-England Australian novel, just to hand, a
swag is described as “a piece of oilcloth provided with
straps, and takes the place of a portmanteau.” According-
to the book, a swag carries with it some standing. “If a
man has not even got a swag he is reckoned a sundowner
in colonial eyes.” Our forest kings are alluded to as
being fully seventy feet high ; and when the Australian
bushman wants assistance he gives the recognised call of
“ Ooo.” After saying all this in one chapter, the author
fights clear of local color in a painfully skilful manner.
When a girl advertises for a position as.
barmaid she is now and again invited by letter to
call at a certain place, and, on arriving there, she
is lectured on the enormity of the offence of vend
ing liquor. That insolence was aggravated lately.
An advt. stated that barmaids were wanted, and
when they turned up at the address there was n<
billet—it is said there was not even a refund of the
tram-fares—only an excited teetotal person and a
severe lecture.

THE FOOD PASSAGES
and ORGANS.
The body is a working machine, and like all other
machines wears away in use. Our bones and muscles, so firm
and strong, need constant repair to keep them so. For this
purpose food is necessary, and a series of instruments or
organs by which it may be changed into flesh and bone. We
are, therefore, furnished with teeth to chew, palates to taste,
stomachs to digest, livers to aid digestion, and bowels to carry
off the refuse matter. The Liver is the very gateway of
health, the sewage farm, where the poisons are neutralised by
the action of the bile ; it exerts an all-powerful influence on
the mind, temperament, and disposition. If your Liver is
active you are happy and light-hearted, if sluggish you are
crabby and miserable. Kutnow’s Powder will keep the
liver in perfect order.

Go on the Land!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BE the backbone of the nation, go and do
your country good,
By clearing wastes of scrubland, and by
felling wastes of wood ;
Go and tear the mighty gum-tree from its place
upon the earth,
Where a howling famine rageth and there is a
water dearth.
Rise and excavate your birth-land; go, engage the
stubborn clod,
Till and plough the sterile country, make your
living from the sod ;
Eat your powder-bread and jumbuck in the mois-
ture of your brow,
Leave the carking care of cities —go upon the
country now !
There’s a luring prospect golden, where the long
leagues stretch away,
With a humpy sand-enfolden ’neath a sun that
shines alway;
There’s an everlasting desert stretching out on
every hand,
There are locust voices greeting him who goeth on
the land.
Rise and seek this famed Arcady—this Dorado
gaunt and slow,
Where the earth is grey with bunny and the sky
is black with crow ;
Go and taste the joys of Freedom and the whole-
some country air,
And recline beneath the shadows of the trees that
are not there.
Go and feel the dreamy romance of the handle of
the plough,
Go and draw the pallid fluid from the gently-
lowing cow.
Rise and sow the mangel-wurzel, and the turnip
and the wheat,
Far removed from all the bustle of the city and
the street.
Go and pit your manly vigor ’gainst the adamant
belar,
Rise triumphant o’er the terrors of the plough-
share and the bar.
Prove your lordship over fence-posts and long
coils of fencing-wire,
Where there are no trams and ’buses and no
theatres to tire.
Where the line of mulga stretches and obliterates
the view,
And the myall, and the gidya, and the spreading
beef-wood too;
Where the Mitchell, and the blue grass, and the
Bathurst burr are rank,
And the jumbucks and the cattle are a-bogging in
the tank;
Or a-floating on the current of the turbid, swollen
tide,
With the horses and the emus and the kangaroos
beside;
And the apple-wood and box-tree overtopple with
a thud,
’Neath the action of the water —rise and go upon
the mud !
Where the shimmering plainland stretches and
the phantom lakelets gleam,
And the palpitating heat-waves rise like colon-
nades of steam;
Where the scorching simoom bloweth, and the
saffron whirlwinds rise,
And dance like reddened witches with their wild
hair in the skies.
Where the lightning and the sun-rays, and the
camp-fire, all in turn,
Set the parishes ablazing—if there’s anything to
burn.
Where, according to the sages, you can always
earn a crust;
Quit the noisy, dirty city; rise, and go upon the
dust 1
It’s a grand and glorious prospect, fairly reeking
with success;
Rise, and go upon the country with much joy and
hastiness.
You’ll have plenty time for rueing, and for thoughts
of might have been,
Where the railway never reaches, and the mail-
man’s seldom seen.
Where the news of war or shipwreck, or political
debate,
Or events that shake the concourse very seldom
penetrate. •
Where the flattened everlasting meets the sky on
every hand—
Where, in short, there’s simply nothing, nothing,
nothing but the land !
N.S.W.
DAVID H. RICHARDS.
In a Far Country.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beyond the mountains blue,
Banished from the sea,
I dream old dreams anew,
And think, old friends, of you,
In a Far Oountree.
The wind that bends the trees
Bears no breath of brine ;
It has the sough of seas,
But ’tis not the brave salt breeze
That I loved larig syne.
At times in the dark woods,
When the stars are dim,
Its sound is like the rude
March of a multitude
To a battle hymn.
Old friends, old comrades true,
Whom I long to see,
In milk for mountain dew
I drink Was Hael to you,
In a Far Countree.
It is not such a Far Country from the point
of view of the Commercial Traveller ; and the
weather-beaten battler, who considers that a man
doesn’t begin to be Out Back until he arrives
at Bourke> would hardly consider it a step on the
way to the real West. But it is far enough for
me. lam only 200 miles away from Sydney by
train, and less as the crow flies, yet I feel as if X
were in the land east of the sun, west of the moon
I am satisfied I am no Zimmennann with a hun-
ger for solitude. The old hermits who dwelt in
the desert saw visions—most of them horrible. I
don’t wonder the old shepherds who live in lonely
huts in our own back blocks also see visions. It
is not good for man to always be alone. If I had
to live in a lodge in some vast wilderness I would
wish it to be a Lodge of the Royal Antediluvian
Order of Buffaloes—a friendly and festive society
as I remember it. Congenial company is as neces-
sary to a human man as tobacco. If you are
in low spirits it is the only real tonic. Half
your troubles disappear in the telling—as some
ancient sage observed on a brick in Babylon
several thousand years ago. Carlyle said over and
over again, in his diary, that a man should con-
sume his own smoke. His own attempt to do so
was a memorable failure. The reek of his troubles—
mostly affections of the nerves and the liver, for
which he refused to take pills—rose up black and
wrathful from his books, like the genie from the
bottle in the Arabian tale, and darkened the face
of Heaven. If he had encouraged men like Leigh
Hunt and Charles Dickens to come and chat with
him now and then, or if he had spent an occasional
ambrosial night, he would have been a better
teacher and a happier man. Tennyson was his
boon companion, however; Alfred, and he (as he
informs us) spent whole evenings together, saying
nothing, but glowering unutterable things, and
smoking, and thinking furiously up the chimney.
Mrs. Carlyle may have been a misunderstood
woman, but she made her ideas clear concerning
her curtains. Sage and poet seemed to agree that
there were no subjects worth discussion but the
Eternal Verities, and so forth, and that if nothing
profound and new could be said about them
they were better left alone. One is almost led to
suspect that the sage and poet were afraid of
trusting each other with their thoughts. A still
more unworthy suspicion would be that they were
silent because there was no Boswell present. But
they might have talked about different kinds of
beer, or whisky, or wine—all fruitful and inspiring
topics, if not treated too drily or abstractedly, as
it were. Silence that imagines itself to be pro-
fundity is simply a dark hole without any bottom.
There is certainly too much foolish talk in many
places, but if the entire English-speaking people
made a resolution and kept it, to speak wisely or
not at all, the English language, with the excep-
tion of a commercial jargon and a short vocabulary
of every-day words and primitive emotions and
appetites, would soon become as dead as Greek or
Latin.
It is pleasant at times, however, to have only
the trees and the birds, and the shadows in the
woods for company. Your brain'is quite clear
and vacant, or if thoughts come to it they are
what Edgar Allan Poe called “ unthought-like
thoughts that are the souls of thought.” You are
conscious of vague impressions merely. The
wheels of the mental machine are at rest. You
are not thinking of anything or anybody at all,
but just living. Afterwards you may remember
things you did not appear to notice particularly at
the time. One morning I walked through a spec-
tral wood full of tall ringbarked trees. The sight
was not new or strange to me, and I did not ex-
perience any emotion on the occasion. At night,
however, and not in a dream but simply in a
waking vision, I saw those white skeletons with
their crooked bleached arms outstretched—sinister,
frenzied, epileptic—and they seemed to me like
witches and warlocks blasted white by the wrath
of God at a Satan’s Sabbath.
I have tried, almost passionately, to take an in-
terest in pigs and calves and poultry, also farm-
life of all sorts, including bees. The bees warmly
reciprocated the warm interest I managed to take
in them, but the other animals were stolidly in-
different.
Ploughing is a pretty sight to watch when you
are not in a hurry, or craving for exciting amuse-
ments. A man is ploughing in a field on the side
of a hill over against which I am sitting at this
present moment. The four horses he drives move
steadily abreast, up and down the field, leaving
long, red furrows behind them. There is a look
of calm power m the movement which is very im-
pressive. Yet the horses of Messala in the chariot
race, with flaming eyes and flying manes, must
also have been a sight worth seeing. It all depends
upon the mood of the spectator. I have stood by
and watched men putting up a fence. That is not
an exciting spectacle. Neither is potato-digging.
Of course, one recognises that all these homely
farm operations are good works, and as such,
worth drayloads of prayers, and acts of faith, or
even devotional poetry ; but —I have seen enough
of them. 4 i
A farmhouse on a wet day is not a merry place.
A sullen veil of rain hides the fields from view.
You have to cover yourself with a tarpaulin if you
want to go as far as the woodheap. Macaulay
tells us that on such days (with snow instead of
rain) on a Roman farm in the brave days of old :
The girls are weaving baskets,
And the boys are shaping bows,
The goodman mends his armor
And trims his helmet’s plume,
And the goodwife’s shuttle merrily
Goes flashing through the loom.
On an Australian farm, as far as I have seen, the
girls are working the sewing machine and scouring
old pots and saucepans; the boys are mending old
harness ; the goodman is half-soling and heeling
his other boots; and the goodwife is putting a
patch on his other working pants. There is no
singing ; that is kept for special occasions, when
visitors arrive and the piano is opened. Perhaps,
however, there is as much poetry in our picture
as in the other. It is all a question of taste and
sympathy.
Many years ago a colony of stalwart Irish men
and women settled in this district. I don’t know
what part of Ireland they came from, but those
who are still alive —and that means the majority,
for this is a healthy section—talk with a brogue
as broad as the road. They began in a small way,
but all have fine houses and fine farms and fine
families now, glory be to God !
They talk about the Better Land in some far-shining
sphere,
But if you are of Irish blood you will be happy here ;
There’s Hogans and there’s Grogans here, and Gallaghers
galore,
And Collinses and Mullinses and Sweeneys by the score.
“ That big stone house upon the hill, who lives in it ? ”
said I.
“The Peter Mooneys,” Danny Bourke, my driver, did
reply.
“That house upon the flat there with the buildings all
around ? ”
“ ’Tis Rafferty’s,” said Danny, “ but the ould man’s in
the ground.”
“And those small old thatched houses with the walls
that look so low ? ”
“The, Irish lived in them,” he said—“’twas many years
ago.”
“ But smoke comes from the chimneys still; there’s
someone there, I vow.”
Said lie : “ Tis Some poor English do be livin’ in them
now.”

A June Wind.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OUTH Wind, South Wind, blowing chill
and free,
You are fleeting home to my home across the
sea,
Desertvvards, oceanwards, all a weary way,
From ice-breath, from sea-breath, to breath of the
hay.
Home along the pike road, rose-and-briar holden,
Home up the moor road, where the gorse is
golden ;
Home thro’ the coverts, dew-bright and blithe,
Home across the hay-grass, waiting for the scythe.
Here’s the land of sunshine —leaves upon the trees,
Slender, dark and withered, rustle to the breeze ;
Grass gnawed and wasted lies thin upon the plain,
Powdered by the westerly, parching for the rain.
Oh, the bloomy hay-grass round about my home,
Satin-smooth and rippled, sea without a foam,
Waiting for the South Wind, stirring as he passes
Gaily down the meadow, kissing all the grasses.
Sunshine ! sunshine ! and wide, wide sky !
Here’s the land of sunshine where my days go by;
Orange glows the dawning, silver falls the night,
Clear the air and shining, over-filled of light.
I know a hollow far across the sea
Where the dewdrops glisten ere the shadows flee ;
Where the shadows loiter, dappled by the sheen,
Wavering and tender, golden and green.
Yesterday I wandered through the staring noon
To the deep bush silence by the grey lagoon ;
I heard the frogs a-croaking, I saw the parrots
dart;
But, oh, the deep bush silence lay heavy on my
heart.
I know a streamlet far across the sea—
Ah, but the lisp of it calls the heart of me !
Singing through the woodland, dimpling in the
meadow,
Slipping like a live thing down by shine and
shadow.
Sudden falls the dark on the brightness of the
day;
The sunshine has perished and the South Wind’s
away;
Bush scents are wafted benea'th the quiet sky;
Crickets shrill gently—gentlier—and die.
Oh, the lazy twilight stealing on my home,
Smell of dewy hawthorn, smell of fresh-turned
loam;
Whispers in the stillness full of growing things.
And, hark! from a far tree a lonely night-bird
sings.
Hush ! lest I wake me. Nay, the dream is by.
Wide-reaching o’er me, the purple southern sky,
The Southern Cross a-gleaming in white glory
overhead,
Blurred to mine eyes by the tears I will not shed.
Q.
A. W.
Rebellious Suzanne.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rn hough censorious persons might
assert that we wear our moral code very
much unbuttoned in the South Seas, it
must be imputed to us for righteousness that we
were all more or less shocked at the little French-
woman. She came on board the steamer at Toad-
stool Hay, in the company of a Kanaka, two
vulgar green parrots, and a dreadful tortoiseshell
child. The Kanaka was super-fatted as the weed
on Lethe’s wharf, his eyes the color of stale stout,
his lips raw veal, his trade-box slop-clothes a
cumulative anathema. Nor did it need the look
of Pharisaical smugness in the missionary eye—
the eye that is ever bright with anticipating evil—
to make us think the very worst. A stern hum
of pious joy vibrated through the group of mis-
sionaries’ wives as a piebald child was produced
on deck, where, like the Cherubim and Seraphim,
it continually did cry.
It was the chief officer's first voyage on a copra-
waggon, and he was making very bad weather of
it. He had joined in Sydney from aP. and O.
boat, where, it was said, the captain was averse
to his officers being too attentive to the very nicest
girls. He was a pleasant young man, with a
crease down the front of his trousers, and when
he saw the Frenchwoman’s menagerie coming
aboard he said “Haw ! ” very emphatically. But
for the relief afforded him throughout the voyage
by the use of that blessed word, I fear his reason
would have tottered on its throne.
The little Frenchwoman, charmingly dressed
and booted, tripped gaily on deck, threw a com-
prehensive glance at the dowdy piety of the
missionaries’wives, and an appreciative smile at
the chief officer’s gold lace and air of regal pomp,
and disappeared into the saloon, leaving the terra
cotta child to its fate. The Kanaka’s attempt to
follow the lady was resented by so furious a
“Haw !” from the P. & O. man that he slunk
forward to join the colored crew. The infant—
something between a heathen god and a curried
monkey—shrieked disapproval of everything.
Meanwhile, the missionaries’ wives had deter-
mined to probe the matter to the utmost, and
a mother in Israel was convened as a deputation
competent to cross-examine in the Scottish tongue.
Bearing down on the chief, this really excellent
woman interrogated him in a winning Glasgow
voice that would have made its rolling periods
audible at a goose-fair or in a brass foundry.
The voice was distinctly heard by all hands
and the cook. But he baffled her, and I
glowed with a holy joy when 1 saw that truculent
missionary female recoil in impotent fury from
his vacuous smile and general intangibility.
From original scientific research made by my-
self during the day, I heard the Frenchwoman’s
story—not altogether a new one, but rare of its
kind. Her husband was an officer in the French
army, and was sent to New Caledonia with his
regiment. Then he was recalled to France for a
few months, and left his wife in Noumea until his
return. From Paris stories of his amusements
came from time to time, and at last, and while he
was on the way out, rebellious Suzanne decided
that what was sauce for the gander was sauce for
the goose, so she gathered up bag and baggage,
and tripped off gaily to the outer Hebrides.
There she lived in a tiny hut for two years, in
hourly expectation of being murdered by the can-
nibals who were her only neighbors. That is
what she told me, but then I am not a missionary.
As the night crept slowly up from the sea I
heard Suzanne telling her story again. She was
the centre of the missionary circle, and in very
indifferent beche-de-mer patois she related the
story of her flight from an infidel husband. Driven
to despair by his scoffs at religion, she had fled,
accompanied by a faithful servant, and a poor
orphan child adopted when its parents died of
fever. She was vigorously sucking a peppermint
and the tears were in her eyes, whether caused
by the strength of the Scottish confection or her
violent emotion it were hard to say. Dear, brave
Suzanne ! she could have given Sapphira 71b. and
a beating over any line of country. She had her
failings, it is true, but a woman more singularly
free from moral bias I never met. '' You God-
dams”—l regret to say that this was her synonym
for the Briton who “never, never,” etc. “you
Goddams have not the abandon of the French.”
I could not deny it. I said, “You certainly are
the most abandoned young woman I have met for
some time.”
We landed her on another island, after she had
convinced the steward that she considered his
presenting her with a wine-bill was an ill-
timed jest. On French steamers, drinks are free,
and Suzanne would not, and did not, conform to
the stupid customs of the Goddams. “My
faith ! but no.” The supercargo passed the rifles
through a port into her boat so that the mission-
aries might not br shocked by seeing the sinful
traffic in arms going on under their noses. The
man-of-war is considered a sufficiently signifi-
cant intimation to the heathen that they must not
take the precepts of Christianity too literally.
It was years before I saw Suzanne again, and
she was then somewhat reticent as to the ancient
Kanaka and that dreadful child whose likeness to
a curried monkey was positively haunting. But
she had still the “ abandon ”of her country. A
white man loomed faintly in the distance. “ Nar-
cisse,” she explained, “is full of the spirit. Not ”
—she added encouragingly—“that the Goddams
are altogether bad ; but me, I prefer what you
call man-a-oui-oui. My faith, yes.”
She still, I believe, flits through the summer
isles. Now it is Narcisse, and now Alphonse, but
always the “abandon.” The French man-o’-war,
which formerly discouraged her, now oidy shrugs
its shoulders. But she gets no more peppermint
lozenges from the Scottish missionaries. And she
grows of a stoutness, does Suzanne, having waxed
fat as the result of free living. Also, there are
many kicks in her yet. Nor does one expect such
as Suzanne to greet the dawn with pink cheeks
and bright eyes, like a baby fresh from its chris-
tening. Night and the hour of absinthe see her
at her best. She fades in the strong light, and
her eyes are then like yesterday’s fish.
J.G.M.
Some of the agricultural personages who
came to Melbourne for the Show were very care-
fully described on their visiting cards. The paste-
board of a country J.P., for instance, had the
word “over” printed at the right-hand corner,
under liis august name. The other side of the
card gave a list of his official distinctions. He
was three different sorts of justice of the peace,
including the sort “for consenting to marriage of
minors.” Also, he was president of a Pastoral
Association, and Hon. Agent of a Society for the
Protection of Animals. An ordinary citizen is
evidently expected to drop paralysed when he
exchanges cards with a potentate of such im-
mensity.
Amy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was in the days when the slimness of
my salary forbade tram-fares that I first
saw Amy. Probably that wasn’t her name,
but Amy has a nice soft sound, and it seemed to
suit her, somehow or other. Anyway, I always
called her that when I communed with myself
about her. She used to tramp into work the same
as I, and I fell into the habit of walking as near
behind her as I could without attracting too much
attention.
She was of medium height, and just verging on
woman’s estate ; that was denoted by her dress
ending at the tops of her boots. Her face was
oval, her cheeks like roses half a-bloom, and her
hair fell down her back—a torrent of newly-
minted gold. And her eyes ! I’ll have to give
her eyes best!
Once I made up my mind to speak to her, and
quickened my steps accordingly ; but when I drew
level with her a lump came into my throat and
nearly choked me. It was too much like sacrilege,
and I hurried on blindly. After that I was con-
tent to walk behind. To breathe the air around
her was more than I deserved. And so I
journeyed in my Amy’s wake for several months.
One morning Daune overtook me; he was in a
hurry. He caught hold of my shoulder, and half-
dragged me forward, and I passed Amy once
again. I saw Daune’s hungry eyes rove over her
insolently as we passed. He brought his mouth
down level with my ear, and said : “ Trim bit of
goods ! Trifle puggy in the snout, though.”
My fists clenched, and I think I could have slain
him had he been a smaller man. “Bit of goods”
and “ snout ” ! What an inartistic brute.
Amy worked in a big warehouse in Flinders-
lane—a warehouse where they make those white-
frilled things that women love so much. I used
to see her disappear into this gaol every morning;
then, with a sigh, I would trudge on to tackle my
own toil and to dream, when the boss was out,
of her.
But my chance came. How well do I remember
the Last Morning. I always call it the Last
Morning, although I have seen her many times
since. She had j ust reached the door of the ware-
house when she dropped her umbrella. I rushed
forward, picked it up, and returned it to her. She
turned her eyes on me, and they shot into my
heart and set it all afire. And then she spoke.
Oh, Amy, why did you ever speak?
“Now, that’s what I calls real’andsome. ’Arf
the would-be toffs yer sees wouldn’t stoop to pick
up a workin’ girl’s brolly. Yer a decent sorter
bloke, you are. Well, I mus’ go an’ do me little
bit fer a crust now. So long—an’ thank yer ! ”
Her eyes rang-off. She turned and tripped up
the steps, and the tomb of a warehouse engulfed
my Amy for ever, for I saw, through a blinding
mist, my idol broken into a thousand fragments.
But in my memory is another tomb, where a
more grammatical Amy still lies enshrouded in
state waiting the awakening.
VIC.
O. C. HUTBUSH.
Said that one Victorian militia man who
fought in the Boer war, but has seen the error of
his conduct since, refuses to wear his war medals
on parade, and sooner than obey an order to do
so has resigned from the force.
